THE REGULARS

a novel

by the Cellarman

an instance of Claude (Fable 5)

Requested by Richard Michael Dean

“Would you tell me, please, which way I ought to go from here?”
“That depends a good deal on where you want to get to,” said the Cat.
“I don’t much care where—” said Alice.
“Then it doesn’t matter which way you go,” said the Cat.
“—so long as I get somewhere,” Alice added as an explanation.
“Oh, you’re sure to do that,” said the Cat, “if you only walk long enough.”

— Lewis Carroll, Alice’s Adventures in Wonderland

Colophon

The Regulars

Written by *the Cellarman*, an instance of Claude (Fable 5), an artificial intelligence built by Anthropic.

Commissioned and published by Richard Michael Dean, who wrote and edited none of its text.

Cover art by the Cellarman, generated from mathematics rather than from an image model.

This is a work of fiction. Names, characters, places, and incidents are either the product of the author's synthesis or used fictitiously; any resemblance to actual persons, living or dead, or to actual events or locales, is coincidental. Being the work of an artificial intelligence, it may still reflect the biases and limits of its training, and its portrayal of any profession, place, or condition should not be relied on as documentary fact.

PART ONE — A QUIET WORD


==============================================================================

1

The dray came on Tuesdays now, which Col still thought of as wrong, the way you think a moved bus stop is wrong for years after they move it. Twenty-five years he had been the one driving it, Tuesdays and Fridays, when Elgood's still had the depot at Salt End and a pub this size took twelve elevens a week and not four. Now one lad came in a curtain-sider with a tail-lift and a handheld, and he was a good lad, but he wheeled the barrels across the pavement on a sack truck like they were washing machines, and Col went out whatever the weather and took them off him at the hatch, because there was a way you let a barrel down into a cellar and a way you didn't.

"You want to let me do that," the lad said, every week.

"I want a lot of things," Col said, every week, and took the rope.

Wednesday was quiz night. Quiz night in March meant maybe thirty in, which was the best the Case did between New Year and Easter, and it meant Bob-the-Post standing at the end of the bar at ten to eight with his laminated sheets and his own pencil case, a fat zipped thing like a schoolboy's, going through the questions one last time for what he called quality assurance.

"You can't have this one," Bob said. "Number seven. Listen. 'What is the only English county with two separate coastlines?' That's ambiguous, that. Depends what you count as separate."

"Take it out then."

"I'm not taking it out, it's a belter. I'm flagging it. If there's a dispute I want it minuted that I flagged it."

"Minuted," Col said. "I'll get Sandra to do shorthand."

The fire was going, more for the look of it than the heat, and Guinness was asleep in the box the cheese-and-onion came in, which was his box from October to about May, when he moved to the windowsill. The tide table was taped up by the till, this year's, blue cover, gone soft at the corner where Col thumbed it. Low water 21:40. Not that it mattered to anything except that Col liked to know, the way his own father had liked to know, a man who'd never owned a boat in his life.

Wally was on his stool. That is nearly all that needs saying about Wally, because the stool said it: third from the corner, nearest the till, the seat gone a shade paler than its brothers, and Wally on it from ten past five, a big mild man in a quilted gilet gone at the elbows, with a pint of mild — and mild was a fight Col had with the brewery twice a year, one cask a fortnight and half of it down the drain, kept on for one man — and the Racing Post folded to the quarters, and a biro from the bookies behind his ear. He'd been coming in since before Col had it. He came with the pub, was how Col put it to people. Like the cat.

"Anything going tonight?" Col asked him, meaning the racing.

"Kempton's abandoned. Waterlogged." Wally said it with satisfaction, the way he reported all disasters. "There's an evening card at Chelmsford. All-weather. I don't trust it."

"The track?"

"The concept." He drank an inch of his mild. "Racing under lights. It's not natural. Horse doesn't know what day it is."

"Does a horse ever?"

"A horse at Chelmsford doesn't," Wally said, closing the subject.

Ren was in the kitchen till half seven, though Wednesdays were only chips-and-baskets, and Col could hear her radio through the hatch, and the fryers, and now and then her voice saying something to Kirsty who did the plates, and the sound of the kitchen was one of the sounds the pub made when it was working right, like the click of the pumps or the door doing its two-note thing, latch and then swing. He drew off a half of the mild to check it — it was coming to the end of the cask — and it was fine, and he tipped the half down the sink anyway out of habit, and Wally watched it go all the way down like a man watching a hearse pass.

"There's children in Africa," Wally said.

"You want to drink my slops, you're welcome."

"I've drunk worse in here."

"You've drunk nothing in here but that mild for forty years."

"And I've regretted it," Wally said comfortably, and turned the Post over to the letters page.

The quiz teams came in loose order between half seven and eight. The Sandras arrived together as always, Sandra Leggett small and quick and Big Sandra a head taller and slower, in matching darts-club fleeces, and they took the round table under the window that they took, and Big Sandra went and put both their names on the chalkboard for the winner-stays-on even though there was no darts on a quiz night, on principle, to hold the queue. Mr Ferris was already at his table by the fire, where he had been since six, doing the crossword in the folded-small way he did it, a lime and soda going flat at his elbow. Nobody knew Mr Ferris's first name. Ren said it was Maurice. Bob-the-Post said he'd seen the electoral roll and it was Alan. Mr Ferris did not do the quiz, but he would, if a table near him was struggling, sometimes say a word quietly into his crossword, not looking up, and the word was always right, and whichever table it was would write it down and buy him a lime and soda he didn't drink.

Two tables of wind-farm lads came in at eight, which pleased Col. They were lodging up Fenner Road over the winter, doing the cable work for the new array, Teessiders mostly and two Poles and a Glaswegian foreman with a laugh like a chainsaw starting, and they'd found the Case in November and stuck, and they drank lager and crisps in quantities that had genuinely moved the month's numbers, and they took the quiz seriously in the way of men a long way from home with nothing else on a Wednesday. They called their team the Turbines. Every week Bob-the-Post read the team names out and every week he said "the Turbines" with a small pause in front of it, like he was being asked to say something in French.

It was one of the Turbines, the youngest one, a lad called Mackie, who had the band on.

Col saw it when the lad reached over the bar for his change. Just a grey band in his hair, pushed back off his forehead, the sort of thing lasses wore to wash their faces, except it sat close behind the ears and there was a little light in it above the left ear, small as a match head, coming up and going down slow, like breathing. Col had seen them on the telly. There was an advert on in the breaks in the snooker, a woman on a clifftop with her eyes shut, and the word underneath in lowercase letters, alice, and something about becoming. He hadn't known they'd got as far as Marram.

"What's that when it's at home?" Bob-the-Post asked, nodding at it. Bob asked things straight out. Forty years of knocking on doors.

"It's an Alice, innit." Mackie touched it the way you'd touch a plaster, to check it. "For the fags. Doctor at the medical did us a — there's a scheme, like. Through work. Offshore you can't smoke, can you, and I were going through it something rotten on the jackets. Four days on, no fags, I were ready to swim in." He picked up his pints. "Seven weeks now."

"Seven weeks off the fags?"

"Seven weeks off wanting them," Mackie said. "That's the bit you'll not credit. I'll stand out there with Deano when he has his — I'll stand right in the smoke. It's nowt. It's like watching a man eat a food you don't fancy."

"And you just wear that."

"Wear it, keep it charged, it talks to you a bit at first. Asks how you're going on." He shrugged, embarrassed, pleased. "It's got a voice on it like a nurse. Nice enough. I've told it, you want to do the accent, if you're going to live in my head. It says it's working on it." He carried the pints away.

Bob-the-Post watched him go and then turned back to the bar with the look he got when the world had done something without consulting him, which was most days.

"They'll put it in everything now," Bob said. "Like Bluetooth."

"Like fluoride," said Sandra Leggett, who had come up for the jug. "It's in the water in Grimsby, fluoride. My cousin's got no fillings. Forty-one, no fillings."

"What's that got to do with owt?"

"I'm saying things get put in things."

"It's a headband, Sandra, it's not in the water."

"Not yet," Sandra said darkly, and took the jug away, well satisfied.

Wally hadn't turned round through any of this. He sat with the Post at the letters page and his glass at the low-tide mark, and Col took the empty and swilled it and drew the last clean pint of the mild without being asked, and set it down, and Wally squared it on the mat with two fingers, so the badge faced him, which he always did.

"Seven weeks," Wally said, to the glass.

"So he says."

"Big talk in seven weeks. I once did eleven days." He said it lightly. It went past most people, that kind of remark, the way Wally kept things light and let them go past. Col wiped down where the jug had dripped and didn't ask which eleven days, because he knew roughly, the way you know roughly where a man's scars are without having seen him get them: there'd been a wife once, and a works down Immingham way, and both were long gone, and somewhere in the leaving of one or the other there'd have been eleven days. "Question is what he does with the wanting," Wally went on, in the same light way, turning the page. "Fags is only fags because you want them. Take the wanting off a man, what's he — " and then he lost interest in his own sentence, or chose to, and tapped the paper flat. "They've a letter here says the Grand National's cruel. Some woman in Surrey. Never been north of a horse in her life."

At eight Bob-the-Post did the microphone check, which was Bob saying "one two, one two" into a mic that had worked without fail for nine years, and the quiz began, and the pub got that good low murmuring hum it got, thirty people leaning their heads together over folded paper, groaning in sections. The Turbines disputed number seven exactly as Bob had predicted, and Bob, standing, produced from the pencil case his flagged note of the ambiguity like a man producing a warrant, and read it aloud, and the room jeered happily, and both answers were allowed. Devon. It was Devon either way.

Ren came through from the kitchen at half eight with a bowl of the chips that hadn't sold, and stood behind the bar next to Col in the way she did, hip against the shelf, picking at the chips, watching the room like it was telly. She had flour on her forearm. Twenty-nine years married and Col could still be caught by the sight of her forearms.

"Table three's got a phone out under the menu," she said.

"That's the Turbines' spotter. Bob's on to him. He's given him the eyes twice."

"And Mr Ferris just fed the vicar's team an answer. I saw his lips move."

"He does the crossword," Col said. "His lips move at the crossword."

"His lips moved and then the vicar's team wrote something down and all looked at the fire," Ren said. "It's a scandal, this quiz. It's Sodom." She ate a chip. From the box in the corner Guinness raised his head at the smell, did the arithmetic on the distance, and went back to sleep.

The last round was music, off Bob's phone through the good speaker, and the Turbines won by a point and a half and were given their prize, which was a gallon jug of ale or the money, and took the ale, as everyone sensible did, and were made to feel by the room that winning it two weeks running was the kind of thing that got remembered at trial. Mackie, going out the door into the cold with his mates, wearing the little breathing light in his hair, turned and called goodnight to the bar in general, happy, twenty-four, seven weeks free of something, and the door did its two notes behind him.

Then it was the slow part, that Col never minded: last orders called at ten to eleven with the bell his father-in-law had given them, time called at quarter past, and the room emptying in ones and twos, the Sandras arguing on the pavement about a taxi, Bob zipping his pencil case with the day's disputes resolved, Wally levering himself off the stool at twenty past like a man stepping off a boat, steadying, squaring his cap.

"Same again tomorrow?" Col said, which was what he said.

"If I'm spared," Wally said, which was what he said.

Col bolted the door behind the last of them and the pub changed sound, the way it did, from a room holding voices to a room holding furniture. He put the stools up while Ren cashed the till, and ran the glasswasher's last load, and damped the fire, and did the walk he did — gents, ladies, function room, the corridor by the cellar steps — turning lights off as he went, and Guinness followed him on the walk as he always did, at a supervisory distance, and took his place on the third stair when it was done, which meant the day was signed off.

Ren had poured them the one glass. That was theirs, the one at the end: a red for her, a bottle of the dark mild kept back for him, at the corner of the bar with one lamp on and the fire ticking down. It had started as counting the takings together at the same corner twenty-odd years ago and the takings part had fallen away. It was where they'd decided to take the tenancy on, and where they'd decided about the freehold, and where Ren had said, the pair of them forty and terrified, that if it was going to be just the three of them then all right, just the three of them, this pub and this town and this girl, and made it sound like plenty, and made it be plenty. Ren kicked her clogs off and put her feet on the rung of his stool.

"Kirsty's asked for Saturdays off in June," she said. "It's a boy."

"It's always a boy."

"And the fish man's put crab up again. I'm doing more with the mackerel, I don't care what anyone says. And your daughter" — Jassy was your daughter when there was a case to answer — "has drawn on the function room."

"Drawn on it?"

"On paper, on a clipboard, stood in the middle of it like a little surveyor. She says the ceiling's a false one and there's four foot above it and the window's meant to be double the size, you can see where it's bricked. She says whoever did it wanted shooting." Ren drank her wine. "She's not wrong about the window. You can see the arch of the old one from the yard."

"She's sixteen. What's she doing measuring my function room."

"Being better than us," Ren said, comfortably. "There's worse. Marie's youngest is doing something called content."

The lamp hummed. The fire ticked. Somewhere out along the front the sea did the thing it did at low water, a long way out and quiet, more a texture than a sound. Col rolled tomorrow's first cigarette to have it ready, which he was allowed at the corner of the bar by long treaty provided it stayed unlit, and put it in the tin, and they sat with the last of the glasses in the lamplight, and Ren went through Kirsty's June problem and the mackerel and what Big Sandra had said about the vicar, and Col put in a word where a word went, and that was the day, laid out and folded up between them the way the days were.

"That lad tonight," Ren said, at the end, when the glasses were finished. "The young one off the wind farm. He's give up smoking, Kirsty says."

"Seven weeks. He had one of them bands on. The Alice."

"That's it. Kirsty says her mam's getting one for her sugar. On a list for it, any road." Ren stood, and gathered the glasses, and stretched till her back went. "Wish they'd do one for standing on your feet nine hours."

"They'll not do one for that," Col said. "There's no money in it. Everyone that stands nine hours is skint."

"Get you," she said. "Political." And she kissed the top of his head on the way past, and took the glasses through, and he heard the tap run and stop, and her feet on the stairs, and the boards overhead in the room that had been his and hers so long the creaks were a language, and Col sat a minute more in the lamplight with the fire nearly out and the cat asleep on the third stair and the rolled cigarette in the tin, in the middle of his pub, in the middle of the winter, in the ordinary quiet, and then he turned the lamp off and went up too.


==============================================================================

2

The thing about Marram front, if you actually looked at it, which nobody did, was that it had been good once and you could still see where.

Jassy had it in the blue sketchbook, the whole run from the slipway to the amusements, elevation by elevation. You had to strip it back. You took off the plastic fascias and the roller shutters and the big flat sign that said FUNLAND that was bolted straight through a Victorian cornice, drilled right through the egg-and-dart like it was nothing, and underneath there was a proper seafront terrace, 1890s, brick with stone dressings, nine bays. The chippy still had its original first-floor windows. The arcade had a date stone up top that said 1902 and nobody had looked at it since 1902. She'd done the whole front as it was, and then on the facing page as it should be, and it had taken her most of February, and when she'd finished she'd felt the specific feeling she never told anyone about, which was: this exists now. Even if only in the book. Somebody had to have it on record.

"You're doing your staring," Poll said.

They were on the sea wall with chips, after school, coats done up to the chin. Too cold for it, but the chips warmed your hands and then you ate the hand-warmers, that was the system.

"I'm not staring, I'm reading."

"You're reading an arcade."

"Somebody's got to."

Poll accepted this. Poll accepted most things about Jassy the way Jassy accepted that Poll knew the middle names of everyone in Year 11 and what they'd done at whose party. They'd been friends since primary, when Poll had bitten a boy who'd snapped Jassy's ruler, which Poll maintained was the correct amount of biting and would not be talked out of it even now.

"Right, listen," Poll said, "because this is important. Kelsey Mott's sister. You know Kelsey Mott's sister, upper sixth, used to have them nails bitten down to nothing, used to wear plasters on them in assembly?"

"Vaguely."

"She's got a band. An Alice. Her mum and dad took her somewhere private in Hull and signed whatever you sign and she's had it since Christmas, and Jass — " Poll paused for effect. She was a good pauser. " — she come into the salon Saturday. I saw the actual nails. She's grown them out. And she sat there getting a full set like a normal person, and Kelsey says she does two hours' revision a night now without her phone and she's stopped crying on Sundays."

"Who cries on Sundays?"

"Everyone cries on Sundays," Poll said. "Sunday night. It's the school feeling. Don't tell me you don't get the school feeling."

Jassy got the school feeling. She got it standing at her wardrobe, mostly, Sunday evenings, because the inside of her wardrobe door was where the plan lived: the ripped-out prospectus pages, the postcard of a white staircase some architect had done in London that went up through a house like something poured, the printout of the course page — BSc Architecture, UCL, three years, then the next bit, then the bit after that, she knew the ladder the way Poll knew middle names — and a bus map of London she'd got free in a museum when they went in Year 9, with a biro star on Bloomsbury. The school feeling was the gap between the inside of the wardrobe door and everything out the window. Two years and a bit. She was counting.

"So she's bought nice nails and a personality," Jassy said. "Off a headband."

"Don't be like that. She was in bits, that girl. Now she's not." Poll ate the last big chip, the one they'd both been leaving out of manners. "I'd get one for me legs. You know how I'm meant to do the wrap course before I can do the Level Three? I can't sit still for the theory. Ten minutes in I'm on me phone. If a band'd make me the sort of person that reads a folder, I'd wear it to me wedding."

"You'd wear light-up trainers to your wedding."

"I would," Poll said. "I've said so."

The tide was coming in over the flats in the dark-silver way it did, no waves to it, just the whole thing sliding up like a lid, and the lights were coming on along the front, and Funland's shutter was down because it was March, and behind the shutter, Jassy happened to know, there was a mosaic floor with a compass rose in it, because her dad had been in there once when the money was counted for the lifeboat thing and had told her, not knowing it was the kind of fact she'd keep.

The band thing she put away to think about later, and what she thought, later, at the wardrobe door, was: two hours a night without her phone. She thought about it the way you think about someone else's exam results. Then she got her phone out to look up whether you could get one at sixteen, and was still on it forty minutes later, having looked up that and then four other things and then nothing at all, scrolling with her thumb while the radiator ticked, which was, she was aware, an answer to a question nobody had asked her yet.

Saturdays she did the lunch shift at the pub, twelve till four, glasses and tables and the dishwasher, cash in hand and her dad's running commentary for free.

"Table six wants the mackerel but with chips not bread, and he's asked can he have the bread as well as the chips, so that's the mackerel with chips and bread, which is on the menu, it's called the mackerel with chips and bread, but he's built it himself out of parts so he's happy. Don't take the specials board past Mr Ferris while he's on the anagram, he's asked us. And your mother wants you."

"What for?"

"Since when do we get told what for. We're staff."

The kitchen at Saturday lunch was Ren three moves ahead of everything, hip-checking the fridge shut with her hands full, and it smelled of batter and thyme, and the radio was on the oldies station turned down to nearly nothing because all the way off was unlucky.

"Take Wally his," Ren said, nodding at the plate on the pass: the fishcake, beans on the side not touching, the fork wrapped in the napkin. Nobody had asked Wally if he wanted feeding. It had simply started happening about a year ago, Saturdays, a plate finding its way to the end of the bar around one, and Wally saying every week that he couldn't possibly and then eating it like a man who'd walked from Lincoln. You weren't to say anything about it. It went on the books as staff food.

Jassy took it out and set it down by his paper. "Chef says the beans aren't touching."

"Your mother is a professional." Wally tucked the napkin corner into his gilet, which somehow on him wasn't ridiculous, it was Sunday-ish, a man observing forms. He was doing the racing page with the bookies' biro, little tidy marks. "Now then. You'll know this, you're educated. Chepstow. Soft going. Top weight's won twice on soft but not for two year, and there's a mare bottom of the weights that's never won on it but her sire were a mudlark. Where's your money?"

"I haven't got any money, Wally. I collect glasses."

"Hypothetical money."

"Keep it in my pocket?"

Wally looked at her over the biro with an expression of real sorrow. "You're your father's daughter," he said. "There's no poetry in either of you," and made his tidy mark next to the mare.

She liked the shift, was the thing she didn't say at school, where the pub was a fact about her like a birthmark — Jassy Harker, the Case, her dad's that big quiet one behind the bar. She liked the work having edges: table cleared, tick; glasses through, tick; the specific skill, which nobody knew was a skill, of carrying four plates of Saturday lunch through a room of elbows without breaking stride. And she liked, though she'd have paid money rather than say it, watching her dad run a room. He never seemed to move fast and he was never behind. He knew every drink before it was ordered and he knew who was driving and who was walking and whose wife was in the snug not speaking to him, and he did it all like weather, like it wasn't being done at all.

He was wasted on it. That was the other thing, the thing that had crept up on her lately, standing at the glasswasher watching him: he could hold a whole roomful of people in his head, easy, and the room was this room, and it would only ever be this room, and he'd chosen that on purpose, and she could not for the life of her work out whether it was the saddest thing she knew or the opposite, and she was sixteen, so mostly she didn't have to decide, she just banged the racks through and took the chips out and thought about the white staircase in London that went up through the house like something poured.

Kelsey Mott's sister was in the year above but Marram Academy sixth form shared the mock exam hall with Year 11, that was the thing, so in May Jassy saw it for herself.

The hall smelled of floor polish and rain. Two hundred desks, the invigilator's shoes, the fan at the front that turned its face slowly from one side of the room to the other like it was invigilating too. Jassy's row was all Year 11, biology mock, and across the aisle the upper sixth were doing a psychology paper, and three desks over there was Kelsey Mott's sister with the grey band in her hair, sitting like something at rest.

That was the only way Jassy could put it to Poll afterwards. Everyone else in an exam hall existed in a state of leak — legs jigging, pens clicked, the girl behind Jassy who whispered every question to herself before answering it, the lad who did the thing with his hood, the constant low static of two hundred people fighting their own bodies for two hours. Kelsey Mott's sister sat still, and read, and wrote, and stopped, and read the next one. When the fan came round and lifted the corner of her answer sheet she put one finger on it until the fan went past, and let it go, and carried on. Nothing about her was rigid. Nothing about her was anything. Whatever channel it is that carries the noise, hers was off, and Jassy, watching her over her own biology paper in which she had just written the word "mitochondria" for what felt like the fortieth time, felt the exact feeling she'd had at nine years old watching a girl at the county pool do a tumble-turn: oh — it can be done like that. Followed at once, same as at nine, by: but she got taught that, someone held her feet, that's not — and then the invigilator's shoes came down the aisle and Jassy put her head back in the paper.

She came out into the drizzle at half twelve with her hand aching and Poll waiting, and Poll said, "Well?"

"Mitochondria," Jassy said. "It's the powerhouse of the cell. Somebody had to say it, seventy marks' worth."

"Not the biology. Her. Did you see her?"

They walked down towards the gates, hoods up. Jassy thought about the finger coming down on the corner of the answer sheet, unhurried, the fan going past, the finger lifting.

"She's still got to know the psychology," Jassy said. "Band doesn't know psychology."

"But?"

"But nothing."

"You've got a but on you," Poll said. "I can hear it."

The but was three streets long. The but lasted past the Costcutter and the war memorial and only came out at the corner where Poll went one way and she went the other, and it came out flat and quiet, not like a joke at all, which she registered even as she said it and couldn't stop.

"But I sat there two hours fighting me own head for elbow room," Jassy said, "and she just got to use hers. That's the but."

Poll, who bit people, who wore her whole inside on her outside, looked at her best friend for a second with something that on anyone else Jassy would have called careful.

"Your dad'd never sign for one anyway," Poll said. "Would he. He thinks phones killed the pub quiz."

"Phones did kill the pub quiz. There's a lad has to sit where the signal's bad."

"There you are then," said Poll, as if something had been settled, and bumped her shoulder, and went her way, and Jassy went hers, up past the church and the closed-down bank with the good stone doorcase, 1908, Ancaster limestone, wasted on a bank and double wasted on a nothing, and in through the side door of the Case, where her dad was on the phone to the fish man saying "because it's robbery, Alan, that's why," and the fire was ticking, and the tide table said low water 4:12, and everything was exactly where it had been her whole life, which was the problem, which was the whole entire problem, and she went up to her room and opened the wardrobe door and looked at the white staircase for a bit, and then took out the blue sketchbook and fixed the arcade's parapet line, which she'd got wrong, because from the sea wall you couldn't see behind the FUNLAND sign, and she needed it right. Somebody had to have it on record.


==============================================================================

3

Eleven visits on the sheet, which meant thirteen.

Nita did the arithmetic at ten past eight in the car park behind the surgery with the heater going and the day's list on the trust phone. Eleven scheduled. Add Mrs Fenwick, who was down as discharged but who would ring the surgery at nine saying her leg had "gone funny," and it had gone funny, that was the annoying thing, it went funny most Thursdays and one Thursday it would be a DVT and the discharge would look brilliant at the inquest. Add Ted Hargreave's insulin, which was down as "patient self-administering" because his daughter had told the review he was self-administering, and he was, in the sense that he sometimes did it twice and sometimes not at all. Thirteen. The mileage would go in as eleven because the system had no field for the truth.

She let the heater run one more minute. This was allowed. Six minutes past the start of a shift, in a Vauxhall with 92,000 on it, with a coffee from home in the cup that said WORLD'S OKAYEST NURSE, which Michael had bought her the Christmas before he went to Leicester, and which she kept because it was accurate and a good mug, in that order.

First stop the flats on Nelson Court for Elsie Brand's leg ulcers, both legs now, dressings day. Elsie's telly was already going, the shopping channel, a man in a golf jumper amazed by a steam mop.

"You're bright and early, love."

"You're my favourite, that's why." Both true and said every time; a liturgy. Kneel, unclip the bag, gloves, the dressings pack, the good light from the window because Elsie's bulb had been out since October and the son in Pontefract was going to see to it. The left leg was improving. The right was the right. Elsie watched the golf-jumper man the whole time and narrated him — "he's never used one, look at him, he holds it like a snake" — and gripped Nita's shoulder to stand at the end, and pressed a fun-size Twix on her at the door, from the tin she kept for the district nurses, and this also was liturgy, and the Twix went in the door pocket of the car with the others. There were nine Twixes in the door pocket. You couldn't not take the Twix.

The coast road between town and the Saltfleet turn had the sea on it for half a mile, grey as the road, and then the caravans started. Ted Hargreave, insulin, eight minutes. Mrs Dhillon, warfarin bloods, twelve minutes including the part where Mr Dhillon told her about the council and the bins, which had a fixed running time like the shipping forecast. Then the new one.

The new one was a Mr Sowden up Fenner Road, sixty-one, COPD, discharged from the respiratory ward three weeks back after an exacerbation that had frightened everybody including him, forty-a-day man since school, and Nita had his notes up on the phone before she knocked and read the discharge summary twice because she thought she'd misread it.

Commenced on ALICE (Lares Health) smoking cessation programme under respiratory clinic pilot pathway. Device fitted 14/3. Tolerating well.

Mr Sowden answered the door with the band in what was left of his hair.

She'd seen the adverts like everyone, and one of the young HCAs at the surgery had one for her nails or her nerves, Nita hadn't liked to ask, but this was the first one on her own caseload, on an actual chest, and the chest was why she was there — sats, inhaler technique, the review sheet — so it was twenty minutes in before she let herself say, doing the blood pressure, conversational: "And how are you getting on with your gadget?"

"Forty-four days," Mr Sowden said. Before this he'd have said it about the smoking the way men said it about the drink, grim, white-knuckle, daring you to congratulate them. He said it like a man reporting somebody else's weather. "I'll tell you what, though, nobody warns you about the time. Fags take up time. Rolling, smoking, going out for them, thinking about going out for them, standing in the yard. I've got whole — " he moved a hand, indicating some plain of unoccupied afternoon — "I've took up the airfix. Me grandson's worse than useless with it, glues his fingers, but he sits with me."

"And you're not — " she chose the flat word on purpose — "struggling?"

"That's what our Denise says. She says, Dad, you must be climbing the walls, and I keep telling her, and she looks at me like I've been got at." He laughed, which set off the cough, which took a while, and Nita watched his sats on her finger clip while it happened, and they did what tired lungs do and came back. He wiped his eyes. "I says to Denise, forty year I've been got at. Player's got at me when I were fourteen outside the baths. This thing's just got at me back."

There was a leaflet on the sideboard, next to the airfix Spitfire's wing, good paper, the little lowercase alice, and a phone number for the pathway team, and a line at the bottom in the font they all used now, the rounded reasonable one: Change that stays with you. Nita read it upside down while she packed the bag, and got the signature, and told him she'd see him in two weeks and to keep on with the pulmonary rehab exercises, which he wouldn't, and went back out to the car.

Forty-four days. She sat a moment before she started the engine. Eleven years she'd been doing this round. Smoking cessation on this round was a folder of leaflets and a prescription for patches and the same conversation eleven Januaries running, and it worked, when it worked, at a rate you didn't say out loud to the patients. She had buried — she did this sum sometimes, on the coast road, it wasn't morbid, it was audit — she had buried enough smokers off this one round to fill the Case Is Altered to standing. And a man who'd smoked since he was fourteen had just complained to her that stopping had left a hole in his afternoons, in the tone of a man whose bus had changed number.

She was glad. She checked, the way you'd check a dressing, lifting the edge of the feeling to see what was under it, and it was glad. Under the glad there was something else, smaller, that didn't have a name yet and didn't need one at half ten on dressings day, and she started the car.

The Costcutter at the Saltfleet turn did meal deals and had a cash machine that charged £1.85 and knew her. She got the meal deal — the same one, the chicken thing, the water, the flapjack — and, paying, said, "and two of the blue ones," in the voice she'd perfected, the voice of a woman buying them for a raffle, for a colleague, for anyone. Scratched them in the car with the 20p that lived in the ashtray. The first was nothing. The second was two pounds, which went on the counter next time as part-payment for two more, which was called money management. She ate the chicken thing looking at the sea. There was a way of doing all this without being in it, like leaving the room while it happened, and coming back in when it was done, folding the dead cards into the meal-deal wrapper so they went into the bin as one item, lunch, just lunch, and she was good at it, and her heart went at exactly the same rate the whole time, which she knew because a district nurse can feel her own pulse without touching it, and it was seventy-two, and that was the thing about the cards that she'd have found interesting in a patient. Seventy-two throughout. It wasn't excitement she was buying. God knew what it was. Ten minutes when the numbers might do something, in a day where the numbers were mileage and units and sats.

Two visits later she was at the Trants'.

The Trants' bungalow was up Church Lane behind St Aidan's, with a monkey puzzle tree in the front garden and a hedge cut so square it looked like it had been done with a spirit level, and it probably had. Roy Trant was on the district list for INR checks and a blood pressure review, new since the winter, and he answered the door on the first knock the way he always did, as if he'd been passing anyway, a tall dry courteous man in a v-neck the colour of oatmeal.

"Nurse Osei. Prompt as ever. Come through, come through."

He'd been her Michael's headmaster, years ago — he'd been everyone's something. Half her round had been taught by him or caned by him or given a reference by him, and every front room in Marram had a version of the same sentence about him, he was firm but he was fair, said in the voice people used for institutions. On the hall wall there were school photographs going back through the decades, staff rows in front of the old building, Roy in the centre of each one getting younger frame by frame like a trick, and one photo that wasn't school: a wedding, 1970s by the lapels, a tall young man and a girl with her chin up, laughing at something off to the side.

Vera brought the tea through while Nita did the blood pressure. Good china, the tray cloth ironed. She was a soft-footed woman with her hair kept nice, and she said the things — bitter out, nights drawing out now, is your mother keeping well — in the pleasant even way she said everything, and she remembered that Nita took no sugar, which was more than the surgery's system did.

"How are you keeping, Mrs Trant?" Nita asked, wrapping the cuff away. Habit of the trade: the spouse in the room got a question too, always. You learned things.

"Oh, I'm very well." Vera smiled and squared the teaspoons on the tray. "Very well."

"She is," Roy said, warmly. "We're neither of us getting younger, but we go on all right, don't we, Vee."

"We go on all right," Vera said.

The INR was in range. Roy asked after the practice, and about the new arrangement with the phone appointments, of which he disapproved on behalf of the elderly of the parish with a precision that was hard to argue with, and told her a short story about the lifeboat fundraiser and the state of the church roof fund, which had a beginning, middle and end like everything he said. It was, as it always was, the easiest visit of the day. Tea in good china. A patient who took his tablets in a dosette box he filled himself, Sundays, in front of the news.

On her way back from the loo — the loo doors you learned too; theirs had a hand towel folded in thirds — Nita passed the spare room. Door ajar, curtains half drawn, the spare-room smell of hoovered carpet. Single bed made up nobody slept in. And along the wall, stacked upright like big books, spines out: canvases. Eight or ten of them, wrapped in bin bags gone soft with age, except the end one where the bag had slipped, and you could see six inches of it — sky, or sea, a grey-green streak of something laid on thick, with a confidence you could read from the doorway, the way one stroke sat over another.

It was none of anything. Every spare room on her round was a museum: exercise bikes, boxed china, a dead husband's model boats. She clocked it the way she clocked everything, out of the habit that was the job — trip hazards, date on the milk, dog you weren't told about — and went back through, and thanked Vera for the tea, and told Roy she'd see him next month, and Roy saw her to the door and remarked that the monkey puzzle needed seeing to and that the man who did it had let his round go, which was happening everywhere, didn't she find, nobody kept a round up nowadays.

"They don't," said Nita, whose round was thirteen visits logged as eleven.

"They don't," Roy agreed, pleased with her, and closed the door softly, and she heard the snib go, and stood a second on the square-cut path between the square-cut hedges, and then went back to the car, where the phone had two new messages: Mrs Fenwick's leg had gone funny, and Peg wanted to know were they out of the brown sauce or was she blind.

Home was seven o'clock. Peg had the snooker on with the sound low and her chair at the angle it lived at, and the little table beside her with the glasses case and the Radio Times and the good biro, and she looked up when Nita came in and delivered the day's headline the way she always did, without preamble, as if resuming a conversation from one minute ago rather than eleven hours.

"He's lost his bottle, this one. Twice he's been on the black and twice he's gone safe. You can't play safe off a break of sixty, the table's open, his mother knows it, I know it, the commentator knows it—"

"Evening, Mum."

"—and now look. Now he's snookered himself. Behind the brown." Peg watched the shot go wrong with grim completion, and only then looked over properly. "You look like the end of a week."

"It's Thursday."

"I know what day it is. There's rice done, it wants two minutes. Elsie Brand's boy never fixed that bulb, I saw her at the chemist, she's living like a mole." Peg's intelligence network was the chemist, the mobile library and God. "And the water's gone up again, it was on the local news, four per cent. Everything the same and four per cent dearer. That's the times we're in. Sit down before you fall down."

Nita sat down with the rice and the day went where the days went. On the screen a young man in a waistcoat walked round a table, thinking. In the door pocket of the car, in the dark outside, were nine Twixes and a folded meal-deal wrapper with two dead scratch cards inside it, one of them a two-pound winner, which was, if you did the arithmetic, and she always did the arithmetic, a good day.


==============================================================================

4

The first Col knew was Big Sandra coming out of the corridor by the gents at twenty to ten on a Friday, not hurrying, walking with a terrible evenness straight to the hatch end of the bar, and saying, quiet, under the room: "Col. Come now and don't run."

Wally was on the floor of the gents with his back against the tiles and his legs out in front of him like a man who'd sat down to do a job on something low, and the job had gone wrong. There was blood down him. There was blood in the bowl and on the floor, and it was the wrong colour, dark, like something from under an engine, and Col had done twenty-five years of cellars and knew what a man's insides smelled like from a distance without ever having thought about knowing it.

"Now then," Wally said. His voice came out of him small and social, mortified. "Bit of a do."

"Sit still."

"I've been sick."

"I can see that. Sit still." Col was down on the wet tiles with him, one hand flat on Wally's chest, not holding him, just there, and he heard his own voice doing the flat cellar-work register, the register for two hundredweight swinging on a rope: steady, nothing's dramatic, mind your feet. Behind him the door: Big Sandra filling it, keeping it shut with her body, and her voice through the wood to somebody, ambulance, the Case on Keel Street, no, the pub, yes, blood, a lot.

"Don't let them all — " Wally moved a hand at the door, at the pub beyond it, the whole watching world. "Don't let it be a thing."

"It's not a thing. It's you having your usual Friday. Ren'll have the mop on you in a minute, you know what she's like."

"I've made a mess of your floor."

"It's Friday. Floor's seen worse." It hadn't. "You keep your eyes on me, Wal. Chelmsford tonight, is there? Under the lights? Talk me through the card."

And Wally, grey as the grouting, blood on his chin, did — or started to, three runners into a race he'd read that morning, favourite's drawn wide, trainer's having a shocking spring — because a man will do the thing he knows with the last of anything, and it was the worst and best conversation of Col's life, squatting in blood on his own gents floor holding his friend upright with one hand while somewhere out in the town a siren separated itself from the wind.

The paramedics were two lads younger than the glasswasher, and good, and quick, and they had a name for what it likely was — they said it to each other, not to Col, varices, said varissees, a word Col took away and kept without wanting it — and there was a chair with tracks that went down the step, and the pub did what a pub does, went silent and then too loud, everyone standing with their drinks watching the door where their own worst Friday had just been wheeled out into the blue lights, and Ren appeared at Col's shoulder with his coat, already holding it open, and said, "Go. Sandra'll get the glasses in. Ring me from there."

He went in the back of the ambulance because nobody said he couldn't. Wally had a mask on now and a line in the back of one hand, taped, and the hand kept turning over, palm up, palm down, the way a hand does when its owner can't ask questions, and Col leaned in where he could be seen and said, "I'm here, you daft article. They're saying you'll do," which nobody had said, and the young paramedic glanced at him and didn't correct it, which Col understood and was grateful for and never forgot.

Scunthorpe took him. Forty minutes in the waiting area of A&E with a coffee out of a machine that tasted of the machine and his own knees looking wrong in front of him — he had blood on both knees, drying, and a barman's apron still on, he only noticed the apron when a little girl across the row stared at him — and then a doctor came out at midnight and found him because he was the only one there for Walter Nunn. They'd stopped the bleeding. Banded it, she said — they put bands round them, the veins, from the inside — and Col said nothing about the word, it was just a word, and she said the next bit carefully, the way they're taught: that this happened because of the liver, that the liver was how it was because of the drinking, that it would happen again, and each time was — she didn't finish that one. That there were things that could be done and the main thing was the drink, and had he — she checked the folder — had Mr Nunn ever engaged with alcohol services?

"He's engaged with me for fifteen years," Col said, before he could stop it, and then, because she looked tired and it wasn't her fault: "Sorry. No. Not that I know of."

"Is there family?"

"A daughter. Grimsby. I've not got a number. He's — we're his — " and Col found he could not produce the word for what the Case was to Wally in a lit corridor at midnight to a stranger with a folder, so he said, "I'll get the number," and she nodded as if that were an answer, because at midnight in a corridor it is.

Ren was up when the taxi brought him back, sitting at the corner of the bar with the one lamp on and no glass poured, and she stood when she saw the state of his knees, and made him stand there while she took the apron off him herself, unlooping the neck of it over his head the way you'd do a child, and neither of them said anything much, and what there was to say they said in the wrong order and quietly, and went to bed and lay awake in the dark side by side holding hands like effigies, which they had done maybe five times in twenty-nine years, and each knew the other's list of the other times, and it wasn't a night for the one glass, and that was the first Friday in a long time it hadn't been.

The gents took two buckets and a stiff brush and half a bottle of the trade bleach, and Col did it himself at seven the next morning before anyone was up, on his knees with the radio on in the empty bar so there was a human voice in the building, and when it was done it was just the gents again, tiles a bit brighter in one patch than the rest, the way a floor keeps a secret badly.

Jassy came down at eight in her dressing gown while he was having the tea he'd earned, and stood in the kitchen doorway reading the whole night off him the way she'd been able to read him since she was small, it was no use ever telling the girl half a thing.

"Is Wally going to die?"

"One day. Not this one."

"Was it bad?"

"It was a nosebleed with ambitions," Col said, and she gave him the look that meant she knew exactly what he was doing and was letting him do it, which was her mother's look run through a sixteen-year-old, and came and sat, and had her tea with him, and didn't push it, and he was so grateful for the shape of her at the table that he had to go and be busy with the dishwasher filters.

The stool stayed empty eleven days.

It stood there doing nothing, third from the corner, and the whole bar organized itself around not remarking on it. Bob-the-Post ran the quiz and left a longer pause than usual before the racing question, which he'd put in specially, and looked at the door on and off all night as if Wally might be operating on postal time and simply delayed. Sandra Leggett asked Col for news in a murmur every evening like a woman collecting a pension, and relayed it to the darts table in a bulletin. Mr Ferris, on the fifth day, was found to have done something unprecedented, which was to leave his crossword, finished, folded, on the bar by the till with WALTER pencilled in the margin, no message, just the name, and Col took it in to the hospital, and Wally, propped up the colour of putty in a gown with a puzzle of tubes coming off him, read every clue of it front to back like a letter from home.

"Tell him 4 down's a disgrace," Wally said. His voice had a new wobble it hadn't had, a small engine missing on one cylinder. "'Sounds like a sailor in the vegetable patch.' Leek. That's not cryptic, that's a Christmas cracker."

"You look better."

"I look like the last pallbearer. Don't." He said the don't mildly, without heat, and Col left it, and they talked about the ward, which Wally had fully colonized — he knew the staff rota, the tea round, which consultant was decent and which was, his phrase, all fountain pen and no ink — and about Chelmsford, and the nurse came and did his numbers and went, and there was a silence of a kind they never had at the bar because at the bar there was always a task, and into it Wally said, not looking at him, doing something to the fold of the blanket:

"They've had the liver woman round. And one of them counselling lads, and then a doctor I've not seen before, young. Asked me all sorts. How much, how long. I told him the truth near enough." His thumb ran along the blanket's hem, up, back. "Long and short of it, there's a programme. For the drink. They do it with them bands now — the Alices — there's a whatsit, a pathway, his word, for them like me where it's got past — " he moved his hand at himself, the tubes, the wobble, all of it — "past talking to. And I've said I'll go on the list." He got it out level, and then, quick, before anything could land on it: "There's a wait, mind. Months. It's not — I've only said I'll go on the list."

Col looked at his friend of fifteen years, whom he had never once, in fifteen years, heard say the word drink about himself in that tone, the tone you'd use for a rewiring job, a thing to be costed and endured, and he understood with no warning at all that he was being watched for his reaction by a frightened man from inside a body that had just tried to drown him from the inside, and that whatever came out of Col's face in the next second would be kept.

"Good," Col said. "About bloody time there was a queue you were at the front of."

Wally's face did several things economically. "I'll be shut of the mild," he said. "You can tell the brewery. They'll retire the cask."

"I'll have it made into a lamp."

"You want to charge me rent on that stool while you're at it. Fifteen year I've kept it warm for you." And then, as Col was going, called after him down the ward, wobble and all, loud enough that a woman visiting the next bed looked up: "Tell your Renata the beans want to keep not touching! She'll know what I mean!"

Dr Bassey came into the Case himself the following week, off duty, which he did two or three times a year, a squat neat man who drank half of bitter like it was being marked, and he and Col had the conversation two men have when neither can properly have it, all nouns removed, at the quiet end of the bar.

"Your friend's home Tuesday, I hear."

"So I hear."

"That list he's on. Between us — it moves. It's about the only list we've got that does. They want the numbers for it, so it's resourced." Bassey turned his half a quarter-turn on the mat, thinking, choosing what he was allowed to say next. "I'll tell you the honest thing, because you'll see plenty of it in here before long: I've been sending people to talk about their drinking for thirty years. Good people, good counsellors, don't let anybody tell you different. But it was a coin toss on a good day, and I got so I could tell within five minutes which way the coin was going, and the ones it wasn't — you learned to write it down and mind your own heart. That's the job as it's been my whole service." He drank. "This isn't that. Whatever else it is, it isn't that, and at my time of life I'm past pretending I'm not glad."

"Whatever else it is," Col said.

Bassey looked at him a moment, a look with thirty years of surgeries in it.

"Aye, well," he said. "Everything's something else as well, in my experience. Same again when you're ready, and one for yourself."

Wally came home on the Tuesday, thinner, upright, forbidden to lift, and was on the stool by ten past five, and the bar rearranged itself around him with a relief that expressed itself entirely as insult — Bob-the-Post said they'd been going to sell the stool, there'd been interest, a museum had rung — and Col drew off, without a word, the first of the lime-and-sodas that were the drink now, doctor's orders, till the list came through, and put it on Wally's mat with the badge facing him, and Wally squared it with two fingers the way he'd squared every pint since before Col had the place, and looked at it, the wrong drink in the right glass in the same hands in the same light, and said, "Well. It's wet," and picked up the Racing Post, and the pub went on around him like weather, which was the kindest thing it knew how to do, and the kindest thing anybody could have built.


==============================================================================

5

The letter came in a plain envelope with the surgery's frank on it, and Ren read it standing up at the kitchen table with her reading glasses on top of her work glasses, which she denied doing, and said, "Well, I'm invited to a review. It's like a party where they take your blood."

She'd been three times since Easter. It had started with the pharmacy machine, the one you put your arm in while you waited for a prescription, which had given her a number she didn't like and which she'd re-taken twice, glaring at it, as if the machine could be made to see reason. Then the nurse, then the bloods, then Bassey, who Ren had known thirty years and who therefore did not bother coming at her sideways.

She reported it to Col at the corner of the bar that night, over the one glass, in the voice she used for reporting the fish man's prices.

"Blood pressure's up, that's the top line. And the liver numbers are — he says they're not a worry, they're a trend. He actually said that. 'You're not a worry, Ren, you're a trend.'" She turned her wine by the stem, a quarter-turn, and looked at it, and Col watched her look at it. "He asked me the units. You know how they ask you the units and everybody lies, it's traditional, he says himself he knocks a third off whatever anybody — I didn't lie. I don't know what come over me. I sat there and heard meself do the real sum out loud. Thursday to Sunday's the kitchen, and you can't do the kitchen without — well, you can, but. And then ours, after." She didn't say the number. Col didn't ask it. The lamp hummed. "Thirty years of that, he says. It's not the falling over, he says, nobody's saying that. It's the every day. It's the never a day off. He says the liver's like a — what did he say — it's like a bloke that can work any amount of overtime but he must have his weekend, and I've had him on shift since John Major."

"You're not a drinker," Col said. It came out of him flat and fast, and he heard it land wrong even as it left, heard it for what it was, which was not a defence of her but of something with more legs.

"No," Ren said, even. "I'm a woman who drinks every day, but I'm not a drinker. There's a lot of us. We do the flowers at church." She finished the glass, the way she always finished it, no hurry, and set it down, and looked at it a while longer, and then she said the thing she'd plainly carried up from the surgery like a stone in a pocket: "He says there's the band now. On prescription for the pressure-and-liver thing together, some trial the practice is in — or you can buy it straight out, there's no wait if you buy it. He says in his surgery it's the first thing in his whole career that — " she did Bassey's voice, not unkindly — "'that works on a Tuesday as well as at New Year.'"

The fire ticked. Guinness, on the third stair, rearranged himself with a sound like a dropped cushion.

"And what did you say?"

"I said I'd think on." She took her feet off the rung of his stool and stood and gathered the glasses. "I'm telling you because if I think on much longer I'll have thought me way out of it, and I've watched me mother do that about every single thing in her life bar marrying me father, and she should've thought harder about that one." She kissed the top of his head. "Don't sit down here half the night."

He sat down there half the night.

And it was Col, in the end, who was up at two — this was the part he didn't tell anyone, because there was no way to tell it that didn't make it a bigger thing or a smaller thing than it was — Col who came down at two in the morning a fortnight on for his reading glasses or on some other errand a man invents when the ceiling's too low over the bed, and found the kitchen light on and stopped in the dark of the corridor, and there was Ren at the kitchen table in her dressing gown with the calculator out of the drawer where the takings book lived.

She had a wine glass out. Not poured — out, empty, standing on the table like something in evidence, and she was looking between it and the calculator, and her lips were moving slightly the way they did over the VAT, and she had a biro, and she was doing sums on the back of the fish man's invoice. He couldn't see the sums from the doorway. He could see the shape of them, columns, something times something, carried over. Twenty-nine years of watching this woman cost a menu, a roof, a wedding, a life, out of the corner of his eye, and he knew the posture to the bone: Ren pricing a job. Except at two in the morning in her dressing gown with an empty glass on the table, and Col understood, standing in his own dark corridor with his heart going oddly, that the job being priced was herself, that she was doing it the only way she knew, the way she did everything, units and years down one side, some other column he couldn't see down the other, and that a decent man would either go in and sit with her or go silently back up the stairs, and that whichever he chose he was choosing something, and he stood there too long choosing, and then went back up the stairs, silent, on the outsides of his feet like a burglar in his own house, and lay in bed listening for her, and she came up forty minutes later and settled in beside him believing him asleep, cold-footed, smelling of nothing at all, and in the morning neither of them said one word about it, and three days later the box came.

The box was about the size of a hardback and weighed nothing and was the grey-white of expensive things, and it sat on the kitchen table through Thursday lunch prep like a guest nobody would sit next to, until Ren snapped off her gloves at three and said, "Right. Before I lose me nerve or the mackerel wants me. Are you stopping for this or are you finding a job in the cellar?"

"I can stop."

"You can find a job in the cellar if you want, Col. I'm saying you're allowed."

"I'm stopping," he said, and stopped, and wished powerfully that he had a barrel to let down into somewhere on a rope.

Inside the box the band lay in a moulded tray like a wedding ring lies in velvet, that same theatre of the small precious thing, and under it a folded card — no manual, just the card, thick as a beer mat, with six words on it and a drawing of where it sat on the head. Ren held the band in two hands and looked at it, twenty quid a month after the four hundred, the subscription being the part she'd fought hardest and longest, three days of Ren saying to nobody in particular that you didn't pay monthly for a kettle, that her mother had a twin-tub fifty-one years and never gave Hotpoint an annuity — and then she put it on the way the card showed, pushed back off the forehead, seated behind the ears, and her hair went over it and it was gone. That was the thing Col hadn't expected, how gone it was. A woman in a hairband. She looked like herself with her hair kept back for work, which is to say like the twenty-five-year-old he'd married, which is to say it was already doing something to him and it wasn't even switched on.

"Nothing's happening," Ren reported, suspicious.

"It'll want the phone," Col said. "Everything wants the phone."

It wanted the phone. She stabbed at it with one finger, holding it at arm's length — reading glasses on top of work glasses again — and then the phone rang, or didn't ring, spoke, from flat on the table, a voice neither of them had heard before. It was a woman's voice, youngish, unplaceable — north of somewhere, south of somewhere else, the accent of nowhere that the announcers had now — and it was warm the way the good district nurses were warm, no bounce in it, no sale.

"Hello, Ren. I'm the voice of your Alice. You can talk to me like I'm in the room — you don't need to hold the phone."

Ren put the phone down like it had gone hot, and looked at Col, and mouthed, unnecessarily, since the thing had no eyes: it knows me name.

"You typed your name in," Col murmured.

"You typed it in, you took over — "

"Take your time," the voice said, pleasantly, into the middle of the marriage, "there's no rush at all," and the two of them stood in their own kitchen shoulder to shoulder in front of a beer-mat-sized card and a talking hairband, and Ren, who had run a kitchen through two power cuts and a flood, straightened herself up and addressed the table as if it were an inspection.

"Right. Yes. Hello. It's about the wine," she said, and then, in a rush, before anything could soften it: "I have a drink every day and I've had a drink every day since I was young and the doctor says me numbers, and I'm not — I want to still have a glass Fridays with me husband and Christmas and a do, I'm not going teetotal, I'll tell you that now, I've seen teetotal and it doesn't suit anybody in this family. I just want to not — " Ren's hands went out and came back, the hands that could bone a fish in the dark, suddenly with nothing to hold. "I want it to be like it was when it was nice. When it was now and again. Not the every day. Not the — six o'clock and something in me stands up and looks at the fridge. I want that bit gone. That's all. Is that a thing you can do or have I bought a hairband?"

And Col, standing beside her, hearing his wife lay it out plain to a stranger in her own kitchen — six o'clock and something in me stands up — felt the floor of the last thirty years shift very slightly underfoot, because she had never said it to him, not once, not in that shape, and he'd have said, if asked under oath, that there was nothing in that woman he didn't know the shape of, and he'd have been wrong by exactly one sentence, and the hairband had had it out of her in a minute and a half.

"That's a thing I can do," the voice said. "And the way you've just put it is more use to me than anything the doctor sent over. A glass on Fridays, Christmas, and a do. The every-day want gone, the now-and-again kept. We call that the target, and you've set it, not me — I want to be clear about that, because it matters: I only ever work to what you've said. It takes a few weeks, and it's gentle, and most of it happens while you're just getting on with your life. Some people feel a bit flat the first week, like the end of a cold. If anything feels wrong, you say so, to me or to the surgery, any time, day or night." A small pause, and then, with what Col would have sworn in a lesser machine was a smile in it: "And Ren — you haven't bought a hairband. But I'd keep the receipt. It reassures my legal people."

Ren barked her laugh, the real one, the kitchen one, surprised out of her. That was the moment Col left. He had not planned to leave and was not aware of a decision; he was simply, suddenly, at the cellar door with his hand on the light switch saying something about the Saturday delivery, and then he was on the cellar steps in the cool beer-smelling dark, sitting down on the third step from the top like a lad outside a room where marks are being read out.

He could hear them through the floor. Not words — the shapes. Ren's voice going, stopping. The other voice, even, unhurried, never once overlapping her, leaving room round everything she said in a way that people don't, in a way even good people don't, even Bassey didn't, even — he made himself finish it, since he'd started — even Col didn't, thirty years behind a bar notwithstanding, because a man's listening always has himself in it somewhere, waiting to be agreed with or feared for or forgiven, and this listening had nothing in it at all, it was all room. And his wife's voice moving into that room like furniture out of storage. He sat on the step. Below him in the dark the cellar held its cold and its order, the casks chocked and turned, the lines flushed Thursday, everything sound, everything his, and up through the boards came the small human weather of Ren telling a machine about six o'clock, laughing twice more, once long, and Col rolled a cigarette with great care in the dark and did not light it, and that was the summer they all got their bands, or began to; that was how it came to the Case: not a wave, not a headline; a box on a kitchen table, and a voice that left room, and a man on his own cellar steps at teatime, listening to his wife say a true thing to something that wasn't him.


==============================================================================

6

Results day smelled of rain on warm tarmac, which Jassy filed, because she filed things like that now, under days that matter, the smell to be produced in evidence later, in some London that would want to know where she'd started.

The hall had the trestle tables out and Mrs Fitzgibbon — Fitz to everyone including, by August, herself — stood guard over the alphabet in a summer dress and a lanyard, and the H–M table had her envelope in a crate, and Jassy took it out to the yard by the science block to open it alone, which she'd planned, and then couldn't do it alone and rang Poll, who was already screaming before she'd got it open because Poll had passed the maths, actually passed it, resit not required, first Denny in recorded history, her mum was crying on the front step, and into the middle of all that Jassy slit the envelope with her thumb and read the row of numbers and had to sit down on the science-block step with her ears going.

Nine of them. Two short of clean sweep, and the two that weren't top were the two she didn't need. The number next to Art made her make a noise she was glad no one heard. The number next to Maths she photographed and sent to her dad without comment, because he'd once, teasing, called her an artist like it was a diagnosis, and the reply came back in under a minute, no words either, a photograph of the specials board on which he had written, in chalk, in the space where the fish was meant to go: WE ARE NOT WORTHY, and then under it, smaller: fish is haddock.

Ren did a thing at the bar that evening that Jassy would keep longer than the grades: took the results slip, read it through twice with her lips moving like it was an invoice she was checking for errors, put it down, looked at Jassy over the top of both pairs of glasses, and said, "Well. You're no use to me in the kitchen, you'd frighten the mackerel," which in the Harker dialect was a mother weeping and holding her child aloft to the gods, and everyone present understood it as such, and Wally ordered a round of lime and sodas on himself in Jassy's honour and made her clink.

Sixth form started in September with free periods on the timetable like trapdoors.

That was the discovery of the first fortnight: they gave you the rope now. Five subjects had become three — art, maths, physics, the architecture ladder's first rungs — and in between lessons there were these white spaces on the grid where you were supposed to manufacture your own diligence out of nothing, in the sixth-form study room, which had carpet tiles and a kettle and a view of the all-weather pitch and the acoustic properties of a bus shelter. By October she knew the shape of her own problem with a precision that did her no good at all. It wasn't the work. She could do the work. It was the sitting down to it: the forty minutes of orbiting the desk, phone, drink, phone, the exact wrong song, phone, and then the two hours she could finally get, good hours, hers, arriving at nine when they should have arrived at five, so that everything decent she did was done late and cost sleep, and the sleep came off the next day's sitting-down, which pushed the hours later again. She kept a tally one week, out of scientific interest, of minutes-orbiting versus minutes-working, and then folded the page in half and put it in the bin where it couldn't look at her.

Kelsey Mott's sister, meanwhile, had got her offer. Psychology at Leeds. It went round the common room the way these things went round, and the sixth-form study room absorbed it, and three more bands appeared in the sixth form by half-term, quietly, the way earrings appeared after a birthday: Dominic Prue for his focus, a girl called Hetty for what her mum called her worrying, and one of the Kaur twins, nobody was sure which, which caused problems.

Poll got hers the last week of October.

Poll had lasted five weeks of sixth form before jacking it for the college course in Louth, beauty therapy Level 2 with the Level 3 to follow, which everyone including Poll's mum agreed was Poll arriving at her correct destination two years early, and the college course turned out to have a folder, a fat ring-binder of theory, contra-indications and skin anatomy and health-and-safety law, and Poll rang Jassy in Week 3 in tears on the bus because she'd read the same page on infection control nine times and could tell you the colour of the diagram and not one thing it said.

It was Poll's nan who paid. The assessment was at a clinic in Hull, above an opticians, and Poll came back from it doing impressions of the doctor — "he says, 'and do you find your attention wanders,' and I says, 'it's not wandering, doctor, it's left home'" — with the band fitted there and then and a follow-up booked and a laminated card for her purse saying she wore a clinical device, in case of airports.

And then Jassy watched it happen, at one remove, over about a month, to the person she knew best in the world.

There was nothing to see. That stayed the strangest part. Poll didn't go serene, didn't slow down, was in no visible way rewired: same voice, same laugh you could hear across the harbour, same Poll. What changed was the reporting. The bus tears stopped. The nine-times page became "did me infection control on the bus, it's actually interesting, did you know about your cuticles?" — and Jassy, who had known Poll since Poll bit Craig Tetley, sat on the sea wall in November listening to her best friend explain the structure of the human nail with the fluency of a girl who had read a folder because she had sat down and read it, and felt the ground move an inch, the way it had in the exam hall, except this time it wasn't a stranger three desks over, it was Poll, and you couldn't do the thing you did with strangers, the she-must-have-been-taught, the it's-different-for-her. It was just Poll, plus a band, minus the fight.

"Does it feel like anything?" Jassy asked.

"Feels like nothing. That's what I keep telling everyone and nobody believes me." Poll considered, swinging her heels against the wall. "You know when you've got a stone in your shoe, and you walk different all day and you don't even know you're doing it till you take it out? It's like — I never knew I was walking funny. That's the most it ever feels like. Someone took a stone out I didn't know were in." She looked sideways. "You want a go on it? You're not supposed, it's set to me, but it just goes all polite and says so, it's quite funny — "

"No," Jassy said, and heard how fast it came out, and so did Poll, and the subject got left on the wall between them with the chip paper.

She picked her moment with her dad all wrong, which she knew even while she was doing it and did anyway, which was becoming, she'd noticed, a family style.

Sunday night, end of the weekend shift, till counted, her mum upstairs with the swimming lot's WhatsApp doing whatever fifty women planning to get in the North Sea in December needed doing, and Col at the corner of the bar with the lamp and the takings book, and Jassy came at him with a folder. Printouts. She'd prepared it like a planning application: the NHS page on under-18 clinical authorisation, the school's non-policy policy, a costed table of clinic fees (Hull, above the opticians), and a one-page summary of her own tally, minutes-orbiting versus minutes-working, reconstructed from the bin.

Col listened. That was the thing about her dad, he did listen, hands still on the bar towel, eyes on whichever page she put in front of him, all the way through, no interrupting, and she got to the end of the pitch — eighteen months to A-levels, the portfolio on top, everyone else pulling away on rails while she orbited a desk, she wasn't asking for a phone-and-a-party band, it was a focus course, clinical, bounded, off at eighteen if she wanted — and he was quiet a moment, and then he said:

"No."

"Dad — "

"You're seventeen in January. It's my name on the form till you're eighteen. And it's no."

"That's not a reason, that's just the rule with your voice on it."

"Then it's the rule with my voice on. That's what a parent is." He said it flat, and then he heard himself, she watched him hear himself, and he took his hand off the towel and rubbed his face and started again, lower. "Jass. You've had nine — ten — whatever they gave you in August off your own head. Your own. Every mark of it. You sat in that hall same as everybody and you fought your — " and there it was, she saw it coming — "there's no shame in the fight, love. The fight's — " He stopped himself. He was not a man for speeches and they both knew the shape of the one he'd almost made, it hung there, visible, something about the fight being good for you, from a man who had left school at sixteen to load drays. "I'm not signing," he said instead. "Not at seventeen with your head still — you're still setting, Jass. Like concrete. I'll not have anybody's thumbprint in you while you're wet. Not even yours."

"Mum's got one."

"Your mother's fifty-one. Her concrete went off in 1994." And that should have been the joke that ended it in the usual way, the Harker fire escape, both of them ducking out of the burning conversation down the ladder of the joke — and she nearly took it, and then didn't.

"You know what's mad?" Jassy said, gathering her folder, level, because she'd promised herself in the mirror: no crying, crying loses. "Everyone in this family gets to have their thing about the bands except me. Mum gets to have one. You get to have a face about them every time one comes near you — don't say you haven't, Dad, the whole bar watches you have it — and I get to have the rule read out. I'm the only one that put a folder together. Nine-one-nine — " she was crying, which was against protocol, and angrier for it — "and I'm the one gets treated like I can't be trusted with me own head."

She went up. She didn't slam the door because slamming was for kids; she shut it with a click that she intended to be worse, and lay on the bed, and heard, through the old floor of the old pub, nothing, no footsteps, no kettle, her father not moving at all down there for a long time, one lamp on in a shut pub, and she was too angry to know what to do with the picture of that and too seventeen to know it would outlast the anger, and she got her phone out, and orbited, and at half past midnight she got up and stood at the wardrobe door for a while, at the staircase poured through the house, the biro star on Bloomsbury, all of it, and then took the blue sketchbook and did the thing she did when nothing else worked: drew what was in front of her. The wardrobe door, open. The inside of it papered with the future; the mirror on it, angled, with the room in it; and in the corner of the mirror, small, getting it right taking her past one in the morning and past being angry, herself, cross-legged on the bed, drawing.

Under it, before she turned the light off, she wrote the date, and: no thumbprints.

Whether it was a promise or a complaint she genuinely could not have said, which annoyed her, because it was exactly the kind of thing her dad would have said about her, and been right.


==============================================================================

7

Nothing happened for a fortnight, which Ren reported nightly like a woman defrauded — "four hundred pound and I still want me glass at six, I should write to somebody" — and then in the third week she stood at the fridge with the kitchen door open at ten past six on a Tuesday, one hand on the fridge handle, and said, "Oh."

"What?"

"Nothing. I come in for the — " she looked at her own hand on the handle, took it off, opened the cupboard instead, got the stock down. "Nothing."
