That was the whole of it. That was the miracle as witnessed: a woman saying oh in her own kitchen. Later that week he watched her cook the whole Friday service and carry two returned plates of it and have words with a rep on the phone, the full six-o'clock-to-nine of a kitchen Friday, the stretch of the week that had for thirty years ended in the first long swallow of something cold and white with her eyes shut — and at the end of it she poured herself the one glass she was owed by the terms of her own treaty, and drank half of it talking to Kirsty about Kirsty's boy, and left the other half on the side, and found it there, surprised, wiping down at eleven.

"Look at that," she said, holding it up to Col, half-full, exhibit A. "Thirty years I've never left one of them alive. It's like finding money in a coat."

The band she wore mornings and evenings, two hours or so; that was all it asked. It spoke to her while she did the books or her stretches — he'd hear the shapes of it through the floor, Ren's short answers, the voice's even weather — and once, passing the office door, he heard his wife laugh and say, "no, he's always been like that, it's not the till, it's the noise the till makes," and understood that he had just gone by in some anecdote, lightly, the way you'd mention a husband to a friend over coffee, and he stood in the corridor with a crate in his arms working out what he thought about that, and was still working it out when the crate got heavy.

The pub took Ren's news the way the pub took everything, by turning it over communally like an allotment.

"Four hundred, though," said Sandra Leggett. "You can get hypnotised in Cleethorpes for sixty."

"You did get hypnotised in Cleethorpes for sixty," said Big Sandra.

"I did, and I'll say this, I slept like a queen on it. Smoked all the way home, mind." Sandra considered. "He was a lovely man. He'd been on cruise ships."

Bob-the-Post had been reading up, because Bob read up the way other men had hobbies, and delivered briefings at the bar unasked. "It's not hypnosis, is what people don't grasp. Hypnosis is suggestion. This is — " he had a leaflet; he'd sent off for it, to the address, like it was 1985 — "closed-loop. It reads you and it writes you, is the size of it. Same as how the phone knows what you'll type next, except pointed at your wanting." He said the word wanting with the leaflet's confidence. "There's a professor here says it's the difference between weather and climate. Your willpower's weather. This changes the climate."

"What's that make the hypnotist off the cruise ships?" Col asked, drawing him a pint.

Bob thought about it. "A man waving at clouds," he said, and was so pleased with himself he put it in the quiz three weeks running as a true-or-false.

What happened to the hour at the corner of the bar happened the way the tide went out over the flats: no line you could stand on and say there, that's it going, and then you looked up and it was a mile of wet sand.

She still came down at close. That went on a good while, out of what he'd later understand was the same thing that made him take barrels off a lad at the hatch — the body keeping an appointment the reason for which had left. She'd put the kettle on now, that was the difference; the kettle instead of the cork, tea in the big blue mug, and she'd sit on her stool with her feet on the rung of his and go through the day, Kirsty, the fish man, what Big Sandra said, all of it, same as ever, and it was fine, and it was ten minutes. That was the thing the kettle did that the bottle never had: it finished. A glass of wine at the end of a shift has no bottom to it, that was the engineering of the hour, they'd sat inside a thing that was designed not to end and let the day come out of them slow, in its own order, the important bits arriving last the way they do, at the silty bottom of the talking, after the fish man, after the gossip — the real things, the money things, the Jassy things, the things you only say into the third quarter of a glass with the lamp low. Tea ended. Tea was a full stop with a handle on it. She'd drain the mug and be up rinsing it at the little sink and saying, through a yawn, "right, I'm swimming at half seven, God help me, Pauline's bringing the flask" — and kiss the top of his head, and go up, light on the stairs, happy, well, her numbers good, her sleep good, everything he'd have prayed for if he prayed, and the door at the top would do its click, and Col would sit on in the lamplight with a bottle of dark mild he now drank at the speed a man drinks alone, which is either much slower or much faster, and never the right speed, whatever the right speed had been.

He tried not pouring it. That lasted three nights and taught him only that the bottle had not been the point either, and that with neither her nor it the corner of the bar at midnight was just a place a man sat in a coat of lamplight in a shut room, doing an impression of something that used to happen there. So he poured it again, and drank it doing jobs — he brought the jobs to the hour, the glasses restocked, the slate wiped, the week's returns — and if you'd looked in through the window off Keel Street at half eleven any night that autumn you'd have seen a big man moving unhurried round a shut pub with a glass parked here and there about the room, wherever the current job was, and you'd have thought: there's a man with everything squared away. That was the year Col got the cellar into the best order of its life. The lines had never been cleaner. He rebuilt the hatch runners, planed and waxed them, so a barrel came down sweet as a piano note, and got the whole dray done Tuesdays in half the time, and the lad said, "somebody's been busy," and Col said, "aye, well," and could not for his life have finished the sentence.

Ren swam Sundays and then Wednesdays as well, off the slipway with the tide right, in a bobble hat, with Pauline and the physio and the loud one from the flower rota, and came back pink and roaring with her hair in rats' tails, banging the salt off in the porch, more alive at nine on a winter morning than the whole town put together. She'd lost a bit of weight, not much, the right bit; her face had come up a shade, like the tiles in the gents where he'd scrubbed; her numbers at the November review were, Bassey's word, boring, and she repeated it round the pub for a week, "I'm boring," delighted, to anyone. She was not flattened. That was the thing he checked for — he'd have been ashamed to say how deliberately he checked, the first months, watching his own wife across rooms like a brewer nosing a cask for a taint he'd read about — some slackening, some pilot light gone out. It never showed. She was quicker if anything, sharper at the till, funnier with the regulars, having the last word with the fish man in a way that had Kirsty crying into the batter. Whatever the band had reached in and turned off, it had been exactly what she'd stood in the kitchen and asked it for, no more, the six-o'clock standing-up thing, the every-day, and everything else was Ren, was more Ren than ever, Ren with the stone out of her shoe.

He knew what he was. He was a man whose wife had been pulled back off a cliff-edge he hadn't had the wit to see her walking, and who now, at nights, sat in a lamplit room grieving a bottle of wine, and he had enough sense to know it wasn't the wine and nowhere to put what it was, so it went where everything went, into the lines, the runners, the waxed wood, the shut pub at midnight coming up like a ship readied for an inspection that no one was coming to make.

Only once that whole autumn did it get out of him, sideways, the way things got out of Col.

She'd come down at close — this was late November, quiz night, the night Bob's true-or-false about the hypnotist finally got challenged and Bob produced the leaflet from the pencil case and was struck down by his own laminate, because the leaflet said assists rather than changes and Mr Ferris, without looking up from the crossword, ruled against him like the Hague — and Ren had her tea and did the day and rinsed the mug and stretched till her back went, and at the foot of the stairs, hand on the newel, she stopped, and looked back at him in the lamplight, and something crossed her — he saw it cross, he never knew what called it up — and she said, not quite easy: "You're all right, aren't you. You'd say."

And there it was, one stair up, in a dressing gown, the whole thing offered, the door stood open — and through it Col could see exactly nothing that he had words for, a bottle of wine that wasn't the wine, an hour that had died of good news, a want with no name on it that a man could hardly stand at his own bar and confess to the wife whose health was the cause, I miss the drink in you, how did you say a thing like that so it came out the size it was and not a monster, and behind and below all of it, further down than he cared to shine a light, the other thing, the fridge-shaped thing, six o'clock and something in me stands up, which had been said in his kitchen months ago by somebody else and had sat in him since like a stone in a boot he wasn't going to take off for anybody —

"I'm grand," Col said. "Go up, you're swimming at dawn with the walruses."

"Half seven's not dawn."

"It is in December."

"It's November."

"Go up, woman."

She went up. He heard the click. He sat a minute, and then he did the walk — gents, ladies, function room, corridor — turning the lights off, the cat falling in behind him at the door of the snug and peeling off at the third stair, and he bolted the front door and stood a second with his hand flat on the cold wood of it, the street quiet outside, low water at 2:20 something, and then he went down and checked the cellar last of all, the way he did now, though nothing in a cellar needs checking at midnight; stood at the foot of the steps in the beer-cold dark with the casks chocked and the lines clean and everything sound, everything paid for, everything exactly where he had put it, and turned the light off on it, and went up to bed.


==============================================================================

8

Wally Nunn's house was two minutes' walk from the Case and Nita had driven past it for eleven years without once knowing it was anybody's, which, she thought, standing on the step, was itself a set of case notes: a house like a parked car.

Inside it was clean the way unused things are clean. A front room with a three-piece suite sat at the angles the shop had sold it in, a sideboard with a wedding photo from the seventies and a school photo from the nineties and nothing from in between, and over the fireplace a big framed print of a horse — Red Rum, Wally said, before she asked, him and his dad had been at Aintree in '77, the only holiday they ever took — and a kitchen off the back where one mug, one plate and one knife lived by the sink in permanent rotation and the rest of the crockery stood in the cupboard like a shop display. The house of a man who since God knew when had done his actual living two minutes away on licensed premises, and had come back here at half eleven each night the way a boat comes back to a mooring: to be tied up till morning.

He'd been fitted eight days when she did the first review. The pathway sent her a checklist on the trust phone — device tolerance, skin integrity at the contact points, sleep, mood, and a screen of questions headed SUBJECTIVE CRAVING INVENTORY that she was to read out word for word.

"'In the past seven days,'" she read, "'how often have you experienced a strong urge or craving to drink alcohol: constantly, often, sometimes, rarely, or not at all.'"

Wally sat in the armchair that was his — even alone in his own front room, one armchair was his — with the band in his grey hair and his hands on his knees, a man being valued for probate.

"Now then," he said. "See, they want one of them words, and it's none of them words."

"Take your time."

"It's not that I don't get the — " he moved a hand, drew a small circle over his own chest, roughly where a man keeps it — "I'll sit of an evening, quarter to five, and something comes round. Like a bus coming round. Forty year, quarter to five, that's — you could set the church clock by me wanting the first one." He looked at her, checking she was following; she kept her face doing its work face. "Bus still comes round. I hear it come. And there's — nobody at the stop." He seemed to inspect this sentence, decide it was the truest one available, and nod it through. "Nobody at the stop. It slows up, and nothing gets on, and off it goes. And I sit there like a man that's been stood up by somebody he can't put a face to." He shifted, embarrassed at the size of what he was saying, and reached for the lightness the way other men reached for a glass. "You'll not have a box on your form for that."

"I'll tick 'rarely,'" Nita said, "and write the rest in."

"Write the bus in?"

"I'll write the bus in."

"You do that." He was quiet while she did the blood pressure, and then, to the ceiling, or the horse: "Fifty-one year I've drunk. I started before me dad thought I'd started, and he started me, so that tells you. I've been wet through every big thing that's ever happened me. Married wet, buried him wet, buried her — " a small pause; the sideboard photos sat where they sat — "I'll not say what I was at our Lynn's christening, her mother never let me forget it. Every big thing." The cuff sighed down his arm and Nita unwrapped it and he flexed the hand, watching it work. "And now they've — I'm dry as a Sunday, and it cost me nothing. That's the bit I can't — eight days, and it cost me nothing." He said it the way you'd report a wrong bill, too little rather than too much, a man standing at a till holding out money nobody would take. "There's lads I've known all me life fought this like a war. Georgie Cutler fought it twenty year, sweated it out on his own kitchen floor twice, and it killed him anyway, and I sat at his funeral three sheets gone meself, thinking, well, it's the strong ones it kills. And it's — " he stopped, and did the hand-circle again, slower. "If it's this easy now. What was all that, then? All them years. What was Georgie's twenty year of it for?"

Nita had learned long ago the questions that weren't hers to catch: you let them go by, keeping your eyes on the person, because catching them meant answering, and there was no answer, and the person didn't want one anyway, they wanted the question witnessed. So she witnessed it, and packed the cuff away, and Wally cleared his throat and asked would she have a cup of tea if the milk was still right, and went and made it, one mug and the visitor mug down from the display cupboard, and they drank it under the horse, and he told her about Aintree in '77, the crowd going up like a wave coming down the run-in, his dad crying into his rolled-up paper, the whole of it, and it was a good story told well and she was twelve minutes over schedule when she left, which went into the system as nothing at all.

Christmas did to the scratch cards what Christmas did.

They came out in multipacks in December, festive ones, gold, with snowmen and £500-a-week-for-life, and the Costcutter put the stand by the till where you queued, which was not an accident, nothing in a shop is where it is by accident, Nita knew that the way she knew wound-care, and queuing on the 19th with Peg's stollen and the good butter she bought four because they were four for the price of — they weren't; she checked later, on the receipt, in the car; they were full price and she had done a thing in her head that supplied the offer. That was worth knowing. She filed it with the professional knowledge, where it sat oddly, being about herself.

The app was worse than the cards that month. The app had a Christmas event on, snow falling down the screen over the bingo balls, and a calendar where each day opened a little door with a free go behind it, and she knew — she'd read the same pieces everyone had read, she'd nursed two of the men the fixed-odds terminals had hollowed out down the years — she knew the doors were a leash, that the streak counter was a leash, that missing a day made a small wrong feeling that only the app could put right, and knowing it did the sum total of nothing, which was the part the pieces never dwelt on. Knowing was spectating. She deleted it on the 21st in a hot ten minutes after a £40 evening — money that was Peg's stollen and the butter and half the water bill, if you did the arithmetic, and she did the arithmetic — and redownloaded it on the 23rd because of the calendar doors, the thought of them opening on nothing, which she was aware was a thought the app had installed in her and which worked regardless, and she sat on the edge of her bed at eleven at night with the phone in her hand and the snow falling down the screen and thought, with a flatness that frightened her more than misery would have: I do dressings for a living and I can't keep a plaster on this.

Downstairs Peg had the carols from King's on, turned low.

Nita went down and sat with her and said nothing, and Peg, who missed nothing, said nothing either, in the specific way Peg had of saying nothing at volume, and they listened to a boy sing the first verse of Once in Royal alone, the way it's done, one voice sent up into all that stone, and Peg said, at the end, "Your grandmother could sing that so you'd see angels. Terrible woman. Voice like the gates of heaven and a tongue like a strap. You get both sides of her." Which was, in Peg, a diagnosis, an absolution and a merry Christmas, all three, and more use, Nita thought, going up to bed, than anything on the trust phone.

The Trants had gone over to bought Christmas cards.

She noticed because the Trants' cards were a small local institution: Vera did them herself, always had, a lino print or a watercolour done small, the church in snow, boats at the staithe, a robin on the war memorial — people kept them, framed them even, there was one in the surgery's waiting room from some year back, and Peg had three in the sideboard drawer she wouldn't part with. The card on the Trants' own mantelpiece waiting to go out to whoever was next on the list was foil-printed, a coach and horses, tasteful, from a box of thirty.

"We've gone over to bought ones," Roy said, following her eye, rueful, comfortable. "Simpler. There's a knack to getting the print done by December and we found it was becoming a tyranny, didn't we, Vee. The tyranny of the robin, I called it."

"They were lovely, though," Nita said, to Vera. "Everyone said."

"Oh — " Vera smiled, and there was a small pause, not an absence exactly, more like a switchboard putting a call through, "people were very kind about them," she said, which was an answer from one street over from the question, and she straightened the box of thirty on the mantelpiece so its edge lay true with the edge of the shelf.

It was the only wrong note, and it wasn't wrong, and Nita drove to the next visit with the radio on and it was the reaching that stayed with her at the junction where you waited forever to turn right onto the coast road. Earlier in the visit Roy had been telling the story of the school nativity the year the donkey got loose — a story with a shape, like all his stories, he did the voices, headmaster unbending by the exact sanctioned amount — and he'd said, "and of course who should be standing at the gate but — " and turned to Vera, hand open, a man passing his wife the ball in a game they'd played for decades, whose was it, the gate, that year, and Vera had looked at the open hand with the pleasantest face in the world and said, "— the man who — you know. The man who." And stopped. Not distressed. Waiting, trustful, like a woman at a hatch. And Roy had said, "Stan Petch," warmly, no beat missed, "Stan Petch, on his bicycle," and the story had gone on and landed and they'd all laughed, and Vera had laughed too, at Stan Petch, whoever he had been to her once, on his bicycle, at a gate, in some year she'd painted a robin.

Nita turned right onto the coast road. Sixty-eight. It happened. Names went first, everyone knew that, her own mother swore at the telly nightly for the names it withheld. Sixty-eight, and it happened, and there was nothing on any checklist she carried for a woman who'd stopped painting her own Christmas cards and reached for her husband's hand at the gaps where the names had been, palm up, trustful, like a woman at a hatch; and what Nita had instead of a checklist, driving between Elsie Brand's legs and Ted Hargreave's insulin with the heater roaring and the sea off to her left doing its grey business, was eleven years of front rooms, and the thing eleven years of front rooms grows in you, which has no name either, and which had just sat up in the passenger seat and was riding along with her, quiet, looking out at the weather.


==============================================================================

9

The brewery rep said it would be the best Christmas the trade had seen in five years, nationally, he had figures, and for once in his life the man was right, and it took Col until about the 27th to understand what he'd been looking at.

The Case did the December it always did, plus some: the works dos, the darts presentation, the swimmers' party — thirty women in tinsel drinking prosecco and one pot of tea, and the tea was Ren's, and nobody remarked on it, which was its own small marvel, a room where that had stopped being a thing you'd remark on. The wind-farm lads were back for the winter, cabling the second array, Mackie among them a year off the fags now and stood outside anyway with the smokers when Deano went, out of friendship, hands in pockets in the cold like a dog that goes to the door from habit. Takings were up on the year. Ren did ninety-one Christmas dinners over the two sittings and hit her own record and swore never again, annually.

But you learn to read a room through the till, and the till that December read like a translation of something. They were drinking less and spending the same. That was the trick of it. The party bookings came, the food went out, the room was full and loud and happy, and where a works do of twelve would once have been eleven pints deep by nine o'clock with one man on cokes to drive, it was now fours and fives, halves, spritzers, the odd defiant session in the corner looking faintly historical, and more people than Col had ever seen drinking the small drinks slowly and being, visibly, having-a-lovely-time. Sober, or nearly. Present. He stood at his own pumps on the second-last Friday before Christmas and looked out at a room having exactly the Christmas everyone always claimed to want — everyone remembering it in the morning, nobody crying in the car park, not one glass thrown — and could not account for the thing that ran through him, which had no business in a till report, and which he put away in the place he put things.

"Notice anything?" he said to Ren, cashing up.

"Kirsty's mother's got her band. For the sugar. She's giddy with it, she's bought a bike." Ren bundled the notes. "And we're two hundred up on last December and I've pulled half the corks. I've eyes, love. It's the drink that's going, not the going-out." She said it easy, a woman reporting weather from a country she'd already moved to. Then, at the stairs, with the bag: "Going-out's ours, though, isn't it. Nobody's got a band can do this," and she waved the takings bag at the whole room, the tinsel, the residue of the evening, the pub itself, and went up — and Col stood with the lamp on thinking that his wife had just said the most reassuring thing available and that he'd have found it more reassuring if she hadn't felt the need.

Wally's first Christmas dry in fifty-one years arrived three weeks after the band did, which Col privately thought was poor scheduling by somebody, like being asked to swim the day after they taught you.

He needn't have worried, or rather the worrying was wasted, which is different. Wally ran the quiz-night Christmas special more or less single-handed — Bob-the-Post had his sister's in Goole — and did it magnificently, from the stool, in a paper crown, adjudicating a dispute about the dates of the Boer War with such relish that the room applauded the ruling, and drank four lime and sodas and a coffee, and at nine o'clock, at his own high tide, put on his cap, squared it, and said good night all, and went. That was the new thing. Wally left at nine now. The pub after nine had been the deep end of his every evening for as long as the Case had been Col's, and now the want that had kept him in it was a bus nobody got on, and what was left was a sixty-seven-year-old man who liked the company, and company, it turned out, ran out of natural business around nine. There was nothing sad in it. Col told himself that twice, wiping down, and then went and read the returns.

On the 23rd, Wally appeared at the hatch end with a carrier bag and a new gilet on, the price tag still through the toggle, and said, not quite at Col, "Off tomorrow. Down our Lynn's. Two nights." A pause, and then, because it had to be got over: "She rung me. Beginning of the month. She'd — heard. From her auntie. That I'd, you know. The band and that." He straightened a beer mat that was already straight. "First Christmas down there since the little one was born. I'm not to bring anything, she says, so I've bought everything." The carrier bag clanked in a way that was not bottles; it was, Col would learn later, four selection boxes and a remote-control digger and a tin of Roses the size of a drum. "She says the spare room smells of paint but the bed's right." He said the last part into the beer mat, very level, the way a man says the enormous things, and Col did him the kindness of taking the news at the same level.

"Two nights," Col said. "Stool won't know itself."

"Sublet it. I want a tenner off whoever." He squared his cap and stood there one second more, a man double-checking he had everything, keys, wallet, life, and then he went out into the cold with his clanking bag, and through the window Col watched him pass under the lights the council put up, the same eleven lights every year, snowflake, star, snowflake, and get smaller up Keel Street, and Col drew himself a half of the Christmas ale and drank it at his own bar, one slow half, to nobody, for reasons he'd have punched you sooner than explain.

Christmas Day itself was the pub shut and the flat over it warm and the goose — Ren did a goose, against annual protest, hers, mainly — and Jassy's presents from them this year being mostly a heavy flat parcel that she opened last, careful with the paper the way she'd been since she was small, and inside it the drawing board. Proper, A2, parallel motion, second-hand off an architect in Louth who'd gone all-digital, and Ren had cleaned it up and Col had trued the stand and made a new clamp for it in the cellar out of beech, and Jassy went silent in the specific way that was the top of her register, ran her thumb along the parallel bar, up, back, listening to the bearings, and said, finally, husky, "this is a real one," and Col said, "aye, well, the fella threw the clamp in," which was a lie of the correct family kind, and nobody examined it, and she drew at that board until the goose and after the goose and had to be prised off it for the Queen or whoever it was now.

New Year's Eve did what it did, and then the year turned, and at half eleven on the morning of the first of January, in air like a slap, Col Harker stood on the shingle north of the slipway in his big coat with a flask of tea and a rolled cigarette he had no intention of lighting, and watched his wife walk into the North Sea on purpose.

The Dip, they called it. The swimmers did it every New Year and this was Ren's first — five months of Sundays in the water had earned her the right, apparently, there was etiquette, there were bobble hats — and half the town turned out on the shingle to watch forty-odd lunatics of all shapes go down the beach in dressing gowns and old towels like a retreating army and stand at the waterline howling, and then go in. Jassy stood next to Col with her hands jammed in her pits, dying of secondhand cold, providing commentary: "She's in. She's in to the knees. Dad, she's actually — oh my God. Oh my God." Ren went in the way she did everything, straight, no ceremony, gasping and swearing with the best of them, and got to waist depth and turned round and found them on the shingle and waved both arms over her head like a woman signalling an aircraft, and Jassy waved both arms back, and Col lifted the flask, which was his maximum, and his wife laughed — he couldn't hear it over forty people screaming but he'd known that laugh twenty-nine years, he could read it off her shoulders at a hundred yards in fog — and ducked under, right under, New Year, and came up shouting with her hair slicked black like a seal.

She'd have done this drinking, was the honest ledger of it, if you'd asked her to; she'd have hated it and done it once. She'd not have wanted it. The wanting was the new bit — the phone going all autumn with the swimmers' weather photographs, the bobble hat bought, the tide table consulted for pleasure now, her side of the bed empty already twice a week before eight — the whole broad healthy estuary of want that had come flooding into the place the six o'clock thing had been drained out of, none of it his doing, none of it his, and Col stood on the shingle in the first day of the new year with the tea going cold in the cup and the unlit roll-up perfect between his fingers and watched Ren come up out of the sea in the middle distance laughing with other people, magnificent, boring-numbered, alive probably ten years longer than she'd otherwise have been if you believed Bassey and he did believe Bassey — watched her the way you watch a boat you built go off its ramp and take the water clean: which is proudly; which is from the beach.

"Flask," Jassy said, teeth going like a telegraph. "Dad. Flask. Share the flask or lose the daughter."

He shared the flask. Down the beach the swimmers were coming out in twos and threes, steaming in the cold like cattle, being wrapped by their people, and Ren came up the shingle at a run with her towel cloak flying and hit the pair of them wet and freezing and hugging them both at once, one arm each, salt and cold and roaring, "THAT'S the way to start a year! That's the way! What are we, Harkers?"

"Wet," said Col.

"Emotionally," said Jassy.

"Useless, the pair of you," Ren said, joyful, juddering, her band somewhere under the bobble hat doing whatever it did or nothing at all, just a hairband on a wet head, "absolutely useless, get me home and get the kettle on," and they went up the beach three abreast into the first afternoon of the year, past the sea wall Jassy had drawn nine times, past the arcade with its shutter down and the compass rose shut inside it in the dark, and Col carried the towels and was the only one of the three of them who looked back at the water, once, the way he'd look back at a room he was locking, checking it for anything left burning.


==============================================================================

10

﻿## 10

The ice machine died on the Thursday before Mother's Day, which in the trade calendar was like the organ dying before a wedding, and the repair firm in Lincoln quoted Monday and Col put the phone down on them with great gentleness, which was how you could tell, and said one word to the room, and the word was: "Pyke."

Ted Pyke did the Case's odd jobs the way his father had apparently done them for the landlord before Col, on a system of no invoices and mutual grumbling, but Ted was on the boat with the spring tides, so what came round the back at four o'clock was the son. Jassy knew him the way you knew everyone — Dane Pyke, two school years up, left after GCSEs, seen about the harbour in yellow wellies gone grey — which is to say she knew his name and nothing else, and she was doing her physics at the corner table with the good light, so she had a ringside seat for the whole procedure, which went like this:

He looked at the ice machine for a long time. He looked at it the way her dad looked at a delivery, or the way Mr Ferris looked at nine across. Everyone else drifted off because watching a man look at a machine is nothing, but Jassy had a working theory by then that how people looked at things was the whole of them, and she watched him look, and when Kirsty offered him the toolbox he said, "in a bit," and kept looking. Then he took the side panel off, and looked again, and reached in and drew out, slowly, with two fingers, like a heron taking a fish, a length of split hose, and held it up, and said to it, mildly, "there y'are," as if it had been hiding from him on purpose and the game was over now.

"Is it dead?" Col asked, from the hatch.

"Hose is perished where it rubs the frame. Somebody's routed it wrong — not you, this is years old, it'll have been like this from the shop. It's been wearing itself out since before I could drive." He turned it in the light. "I can get the proper part Monday. For tonight and the weekend — " and he did the thing that Jassy would come to know as the most Dane thing there was: he went quiet, and his eyes went somewhere else, some inventory behind them, shelf by shelf, and then he went out to the van without a word and came back with a length of fuel line and two jubilee clips and a zip tie, and made the ice machine work with them, re-routing the line clear of the frame with the zip tie so the new bit couldn't wear the way the old bit had, which the shop had never bothered doing, and the machine shuddered and thought about it and began, complacently, to make ice, and Dane put the panel back on and said, "that's not the fix, that's a splint. I'll come Monday," and started clearing up after himself, which he did completely, which even her dad didn't.

"What do we owe you?" Col said.

"Whatever me dad usually robs you." He was winding the flex the proper way, over-under, she knew over-under from the art technician and had assumed it was secret guild knowledge. "And a lemonade, it's like August in here."

He drank the lemonade at the bar while Col told him what his father was doing wrong with his life at the minute, which appeared to be a ritual between the Pykes and the Harkers with assigned parts, and on his way out, passing her table, he paused, nodded at the physics, and said, "Moments," — because the page was moments, levers, the beam problems — "we do them every day of our lives on the winch. Nobody tells you that at school, that it's real," and was gone, van door, gone, before she'd assembled any reply at all, which annoyed her for a specific reason she identified later that evening: she'd wanted to say something back and had been beaten to the door, and she was not used to being the slower one in a room.

The part came Monday and he fitted it Monday and she happened — happened — to be doing her physics at the corner table again, and this time she got her word in.

"Why over-under?"

He looked up from the flex. Considered it, and her, with no hurry in it.

"Coils lie flat and don't kink. Flex lasts years longer. And it runs out clean when you throw it — you coil a heaving line like that or it tangles mid-air and falls short, and then whatever you were throwing it to's still in the water." He finished the coil and hung it. "Me dad says a man that coils rope badly will do everything else badly an' all. Me dad's full of them. He's got about nine and he rotates them."

"What are the other eight?"

"'Buy cheap, buy twice.' 'The sea doesn't care what you paid for it.' 'If you're cold, work.'" He thought. "'Never trust a man with clean wellies.'"

"Yours are filthy."

"Aye, well," Dane said, deadpan, her own dad's exact cadence, "I'm very trustworthy," and picked up his toolbox and went, and at the door, not turning round, said, "we're landing crab at the staithe about five if you want to see how the other half lives," and the door did its two notes, and Jassy sat looking at her physics for a while without doing any.

She went, obviously. She told herself it was anthropology.

The Constance M was thirty-two feet of scuffed blue and smelled of diesel and the inside of the sea, and Ted Pyke turned out to be Dane plus thirty years and minus the patience, and the landing was work, real work, crates swung up on the little derrick and Dane moving round the deck in the yellow-grey wellies with an economy that made the school lads she knew look like they were assembled from spare parts. She stood on the staithe out of the way and watched, and got called on twice — once to mind a rope, once to pass the hose — and being called on, she understood, was the hospitality; nobody stands you a chair on a working boat, they stand you a job.

The gutting lesson happened the following Saturday, dabs on the harbour wall, because she'd asked what they did with the flatfish that came up in the pots and he'd said mostly eat them and she'd said she didn't know how you got from fish to food, it arrived at the pub kitchen already being food, and he'd looked at her with real, unfeigned wonder — the first time she'd surprised him — and said, "your mam runs a kitchen," and she'd said, "and my dad ran a dray, and I can't lift a barrel either, I've been busy," and he'd laughed properly, the first time she'd got the laugh, and turned up Saturday with four dabs in a bag and two knives.

It was cold on the wall and the knife work was small and exact and bloody, and she was bad at it and then less bad, and he taught the way she'd have bet he'd teach: no flannel, hands shown slow, the reason under each cut given once — "you're not cutting, you're finding where it comes apart; it wants to come apart, fish is panels" — and fish is panels went straight into the blue sketchbook that evening, words only, because there were sentences that were drawings even when they were words. Her hands got freezing and then numb and then bloody and she did the last dab clean, nose to tail, no waste, and held it up, and he inspected it and said, "you'd get hired," and she was ridiculously, disproportionately, stupidly pleased, standing on a harbour wall in March holding a gutted dab, seventeen, with the wind taking her hair sideways, and that was the state she was in when he kissed her, or she kissed him, the official record was never settled and was disputed happily for years, both of them with fish-cold hands, and a herring gull watching them from the top of the derrick with one mad yellow eye, and somewhere behind them the tide doing what the table said.

"Your hands are freezing," he said, after.

"Yours are worse."

"Mine are always this. That's their temperature now. Sold 'em to the sea at fourteen." He looked at her, wind-red, careful, happy, a lad checking a splice would hold. "This is going to be a thing, then, is it."

"Might be," Jassy said. "I'll want to see your paperwork."

He came to the pub after that on the far side of the bar, which changed his legal status: not the odd-job lad now but Jassy's — the town filled the noun in within a fortnight, faster than either of them said it aloud. Col absorbed the development with a maximum of gruffness and a minimum of actual objection — "he coils a rope right, any road," was the paternal verdict, delivered to the glasswasher — and Ren fed him, which was the maternal one, and Wally appointed himself invigilator of the courtship from the third stool with a benignity that was somehow worse than hostility, referring to Dane always and only as "the young feller" and to Jassy's whereabouts, if asked, as "she'll be down the staithe, learning fish."

What she was learning was him, and the learning kept confounding the file she'd opened on day one. She'd assumed — she was honest enough to catch herself at it — a smallness that wasn't there: local lad, dad's boat, school shrugged off at sixteen, the whole kit of it. It wasn't smallness. He knew the sea the way she was trying to know buildings, structurally, from underneath; he read weather off the water two hours out like she read a parapet line; he could sharpen anything, splice anything, sister a cracked frame in the Constance's forepeak by eye and by feel, working upside-down with his cheek against the planking, and when she got him — once, it took until May — to actually look at the blue sketchbook, the real one, the front-as-it-should-be, he didn't do either of the two things everyone did, the wow or the polite skim. He turned the pages slowly with his fish-scarred hands, went back twice, stopped a long time at the arcade with the canopy she'd drawn floating out over the pavement, glass and steel, the drawing she was proudest of, and said, at last:

"Where's the rain go?"

"What?"

"That's forty foot of glass roof with no visible fall on it. That's a lot of water coming down somewhere. If it's them slim posts, your gutter's inside them, and that's lovely till it blocks, and it will block, first autumn, and then you've water running down your glass in sheets and green stripes up your posts by Easter." He said it not to score, not even critically, but with his eyes still on the drawing, interested, inside it, walking round under her canopy in the rain that would actually fall on it — and Jassy looked at her own best drawing and saw it change in real time into a first draft, and felt a thing she would have found hard to explain to Poll, or to anyone, which was: nobody, not Ms Adeoti, not the moderator who'd given the portfolio full marks, not one person who'd praised that drawing, had ever stood under it before.

"Quarter inch a foot," Dane said, handing back the book. "You'd hardly see it. The eye forgives a fall it can't clock. We do it on wheelhouse roofs." And then, catching up with himself, suddenly seventeen kinds of awkward: "It's a belter of a drawing, though. Obviously. I should've said that first."

"No," Jassy said, "you shouldn't," and heard her own voice come out with something in it that hadn't been in it before, that she didn't have a name for yet, and took the book back, and fixed the fall that week, quarter inch a foot, invisible, real, and wrote in the margin where only she would find it: where's the rain go — D.

As for what he wanted — she asked him once, that first summer, on the wall, straight out, the way you could with him because he never took a question as an attack: didn't he want more than this, the boat, the pots, his dad's nine sayings, Marram. And Dane thought about it honestly, you could watch him fetch the answer up from the actual bottom rather than off the shelf by the door, and said:

"I want the boat to be mine one day and not owe on it. I want a bench with a good vice in a shed with a stove in it. I want — " the tips of his ears went; he pushed on — "whatever this is, to keep going right. And I want to be here. That's the bit nobody believes, but they're the ones stood here on their holidays, so." He shrugged, nodded out at the water, the flats going silver, the whole enormous ordinary evening. "Somebody's got to actually live in the places. Or what's everyone else homesick for?"

It was the most words she'd ever got out of him at one go, and none of them were about her leaving, and both of them knew where she was going and neither of them said it, that evening or all that summer, because it was two years off, and two years, at seventeen, on a harbour wall, in weather like that, is the same as never.


------------------------------------------------------------------------------

PART TWO — THE SEASON


==============================================================================

11

The first one Nita saw come back was Trish Lofthouse, who cleaned at the Academy, and who had been, the previous autumn, the happiest woman on the round.

Trish had done it by the book. Referred through the practice for the wine — the every-evening kind, the bottle that had become a bottle and a bit, the kind Nita's checklist called harmful use and Trish called my medicine, with the laugh women put on that word — and she'd worn the band her prescribed hours, morning and night, and the want had gone quiet in the fourth week, and Nita had done her reviews and watched a woman get her evenings back. Trish had started making things again; she crocheted blankets for the premature baby unit at Grimsby, her sister had had one early years back, it was her thing, and the wine had slowly eaten it because you can't count stitches through a bottle and a bit. By Christmas there'd been a photo on the unit's Facebook of eleven blankets in a rainbow row, and Trish tagged, and a paragraph with too many exclamation marks that Nita had read twice and not minded one of them.

In March the bank had sent Trish a new card, the way banks do, and the subscription had bounced, the way subscriptions do, and the app had sent her the message with the button, and Trish had looked at the button — she told it all to Nita in the doorway of her kitchen in June, arms folded tight, looking at the lino — and thought: you know what, I'm done, though. Aren't I. I'm fixed. Why am I paying twenty pound a month to be fixed twice.

"And I felt nothing," Trish said. "That's what I want somebody to write down somewhere. Five weeks, nothing. I was right as rain five weeks and I told our Karen, I said, it's like a course of antibiotics, you finish it. And then Ian's mother had her fall, and it was all — you know how it gets, hospitals, his sisters on the phone all hours — and one Friday I'm in Costcutter for milk and I'm stood at the till and it was like — " her arms came unfolded and she made a shape with both hands, something arriving at speed and stopping close to her face — "it hadn't gone anywhere. That's the — it had been stood outside the whole five weeks. Waiting on me. And it knew the way in better than it used to. Three months, Nita. Three months it's took me to walk back past everything I already walked past once, and I'm not — I've not walked all the way back yet, I'll be honest with you, some nights I don't walk anywhere."

There was a bag of crochet cotton on the counter behind her, unopened, the receipt still stapled to it, and Nita had done eleven years of doorways and knew better than to look at it longer than a second, and looked at it a second.

"The nurse at the clinic says I can re-refer," Trish said. "Go back on the list, start the course again, wear it proper this time, don't stop paying. And I will. I'm going to." Her arms folded back up. "Only there's a woman at work got one of the new ones in April — paid for it herself, Hull, it's silly money — and she says with them new ones there's none of this. It takes and it's took. You could throw the band in the sea after, she says, it's yours, it stays. And I keep thinking — " and here Trish Lofthouse, who crocheted for the premature unit, looked up from the lino with an expression Nita knew from other doorways and other years, the flat arithmetic look of somebody pricing their own life — "I did everything right, and mine came back because of a bank card. If I'd had the money, it'd have been for keeps. That's the difference now, isn't it, when you boil it. Twenty pound a month or for keeps. Same as everything. Them that can, buy the freehold."

The new ones had come in like the new anything came in: through the telly, through Hull, through one woman at a school and one lad at a caravan park, and by the time the machinery of the NHS had convened a meeting about it there were four in Marram that Nita knew of and adverts in the break in the snooker.

Peg watched the advert the whole way through, twice, over two evenings, before delivering judgment, which for Peg was unheard-of restraint. It was the one with the swimmer: a woman in open water at dawn, even strokes, the voice saying that change you have to keep renewing isn't really change, and then the woman walking up out of the water and not looking back at it, and the little sun-mark, and the words: once is enough. Alice Once, from Lares.

"Once is enough," Peg repeated, from the chair, with the flat intonation she reserved for reading out other people's foolishness verbatim. "For four thousand pound. You know what else you only do once at that money? A funeral." She watched the snooker resume. "In my day you were just miserable and it was free. There was none of this renewing. You got your feet done and you soldiered on." And then, having filed it, she leaned forward an inch at the table, because the young one in the waistcoat was on a break of forty and had come round awkward on the blue, and said, "go on, son, middle pocket, be brave," and the world's entire pharmaceutical revolution was dismissed in favour of the thing that mattered.

The clinical explanation reached the surgery by the usual route, which was a lunchtime meeting with sandwiches, a rep called Marcus, and a slide deck, and Nita went because district nursing got a seat and because she wanted to hear it from the source with her own filters on.

Marcus was good. They were always good now; the ones who weren't good had been let go. He didn't oversell, he underpromised, he said "we don't fully understand" twice, which was the new way of selling and worked a treat. The old model, he said — and he was generous about the old model, the old model was a monument, the old model had taken smoking prevalence into single figures nationally — the old model worked with the brain's own learning, which meant it needed rehearsal, upkeep, wear. Like a physio programme. Stop the exercises, and over time the joint forgets. Hence, he said, with a small respectful pause, the discontinuation relapses, which everyone in this room will have seen, and which we were never happy with. The new architecture didn't rehearse a change in; it consolidated it, once, the way — and here came the slide Nita had already seen quoted in the paper, the slide with the cigarette on it — the way the first exposure to certain substances consolidates. Some kinds of learning, Marcus said, the brain does in one go, for keeps, whether you'd choose it or not: everyone in this room knows what those kinds are, we treat their consequences every day. All Lares had done — all, said as though it were a small word — was learn to do deliberately, gently, on a chosen target, what the worst things in the world had always done accidentally on whatever was nearest.

"So it's a tattoo," said Bassey, from the back, with his arms folded. "You're telling me you've swapped the physio programme for a tattoo."

"We prefer — " Marcus smiled, took the hit, rode it — "that it's the difference between renting a change and owning it. But your word's not wrong, Dr Bassey, and honestly? Our patients like your word. People are tired of renting. The response we get, over and over, is: I don't want a programme, I want it done."

"And when they change their minds?" Bassey said. "People change, that's the one thing they reliably do. Woman wants rid of the wine at fifty. At seventy she's a different woman. Your tattoo's still there."

"The want being gone isn't a prison, doctor. She can still drink at seventy if she chooses to. She just won't be driven to." Marcus said it gently, the answer arriving so smoothly worn that you could tell how many rooms it had been said in. "We're not taking choices away. We're taking away the thing that took the choice away. That's the whole product."

There was a silence of sandwiches. And Nita, sitting by the window with her trust phone face-down in her lap, thought about Trish Lofthouse's hands making the shape of the thing arriving at speed, it knew the way in better than it used to, and thought — not as an argument, arguments were for the meeting rooms, just as eleven years of doorways thinking to itself — that everything she had ever seen come into a house and stay had come in through that same door: gently; chosen; solving something real; and that you found out what the rent had been on the old arrangement only when you'd signed for the new one. Then she ate her sandwich, because the afternoon had nine visits on it and you didn't fix the world between Elsie Brand's legs and Ted Hargreave's insulin. You kept your round up.

The season did what the season did to her own thing.

The arcades opened at Easter. That was all it took, that was the pathetic size of it: the amusements' doors folded back onto the front, the change machines singing their scale, the two-pence falls with their shelves eternally about to give, and Marram filled up with holiday money and light, and something in Nita ran its flag up the pole every single day she drove the front, which was every day, because the coast road was the round. She didn't even like the arcades. That was the joke she'd stopped finding funny years ago: she'd never once stood at a machine. Her machines fitted in a coat pocket and a phone, the blue cards and the bingo, but the season turned the whole town into one long till queue with the stand right beside it, and June was £80 the first fortnight, and then the third week was the week of the window-cleaner tin.

Peg kept it on the shelf by the bread bin, an old Quality Street tin with a rubber band round it, and in it the window cleaner's money and the milkman's — Peg maintained the last milkman in Lincolnshire out of sheer principle — counted out in advance for the month, because that was how you ran a house. Nita took three twenties out of it on a Tuesday and put them back on the Friday, and no one ever knew, and that was precisely the size and shape of the thing that sat on her chest at two o'clock the following Sunday morning: no one ever knew. There was no mark anywhere on the world. The tin was right, the window cleaner was paid, the app had had its weekend, and the whole event had happened nowhere except in the one place she couldn't get it out of. She lay in the dark doing what she did, the audit, and the audit came back the way audits did — no harm done, all accounts square — and underneath the audit, in the voice the audit was built to talk over, something said, in the flat tone of a doorway: you've started moving money to do it. You know what stage that is. You've mapped that stage on other people since you were twenty-five.

She rang the pathway's self-referral line from the car on Monday, parked behind the surgery, at 8:04, before the day could talk her out of it, and got a kind lad with a Hull accent and a script, and answered the screening questions in the language she'd taught patients to answer them in for a decade, honestly, no thirds knocked off, and heard herself sorted and banded by an algorithm into moderate, rising, and offered an assessment in three weeks, which she took, and then she sat with both hands on the wheel for a minute in the car park of her own surgery, a professional referrer referred, feeling the specific thing she'd watched cross a hundred faces at the moment the form went in — not relief, that came later if it came, but the strange cold lightness of a load shifting from the private ledger to the public one: it existed now. It had a case number. It could be audited by somebody who wasn't also the defendant.

The assessment, when it came, was above the opticians in Hull after all — the NHS rented rooms there two days a week now, everything ended up above that opticians — and the nurse who did her intake was younger than Nita's mug, and kind, and asked, at the end, reading off the screen, occupation?

"District nurse," Nita said. "Twenty-two years."

"Oh, you'll know all this then," the girl said, cheerful, meaning nothing by it, ticking her box, and Nita smiled and agreed, because it was quicker, and because there was no box on the form for the true answer, which was: knowing has never once been the working part. If knowing worked, love, this building would be empty, and the arcades too, and half the world; you could close the whole grey coast of it down and go home early. Knowing was the spectator. It had excellent seats. It had watched her at the tin, taking notes, in full uniform, and it had not lifted one finger, because fingers were never its department, and the whole four-thousand-pound revolution on the telly in the snooker break came down to somebody having finally, after all the leaflets and all the courses and all the Januaries, built the thing that reached the department where the fingers were.

She was fitted on a Thursday, on the ordinary model, the renting one, the trust wouldn't be funding tattoos any year soon — and the voice in her kitchen that evening was the same voice as everyone's, even and warm, and it asked her what she'd like to be free of, and Nita, twenty-two years of doorways, laughed at the size of the question until she had to sit down at the table, and Peg called through from the snooker, what's tickled you, and Nita called back nothing, Mum, wiped her eyes, and answered it.


==============================================================================

12

The coffee machine was Ren's idea, which meant it arrived as a series of facts.

The first fact was a brochure left by the till in June, dog-eared at one page, which Col binned. The second was the same brochure a week later with a yellow sticky arrow on it, which he binned with the arrow still attached, feeling something. The third was a man called Gavin with a van and a trolley on the first Tuesday of July, saying, "morning, chief, where's she going?" — and that was that, a two-group Italian thing the size of a small tomb sat gleaming at the end of Col's bar where the charity boxes had lived, and Ren stood in front of it with her arms folded and the brochure's exact language coming out of her as her own opinion, wet-led was dying on its feet and the margin on milk was a crime in their favour, and Col was sent on a one-day course in Lincoln — a barista course, sixty-one years the family had held a licence and a Harker went on a barista course — where a child with a beard taught him to steam milk without screaming it and repeatedly called him "mate" in a way Col chose, on balance, to survive.

He learned it, though. That was the part he kept to himself. It was closer to cellar work than he'd let on — temperatures, timings, clean lines, a thing that punished slop and rewarded routine — and by August he could pour a passable one, and by the bank holiday the machine had done what Ren's facts had said it would do, which was pay its own rent and then some, because the town that came in from the beach at three o'clock now was a town that wanted flat whites and cold cans of dandelion-and-something and one — one — round of pints for the table, and you could either sell them what they wanted or watch them walk to the place on the front that would.

"Say it," Ren said, cashing up the August figures, not looking at him, smiling at the spreadsheet.

"I'll say nowt."

"Say the machine was right."

"The machine," Col said, with dignity, "pulls a decent crema for a thing with no soul," and Ren laughed and put the printout in front of him, and then the evening stopped being funny, because the printout was the season side by side with three other seasons, and Col put his reading glasses on and did what he did, which was read a room through a till, except the room was his own livelihood and the till was four years deep.

Food up nine. Coffee and soft up forty-odd, off nothing, new line. Rooms — they let the two rooms in season — solid. And the wet side, the pumps, the bottles, the whole brown beating heart of the trade Col had been raised in: down eleven on last year, which had been down eight on the year before that, which was down on the year before that, a staircase with every tread lower, and the brewery's own barrelage letter in the drawer saying the same thing regionally in the polite font they used for bad news. It wasn't the Case. That was the thing you had to hold on to and the thing that made it worse. The Case was doing better than almost anybody — the food, the quiz, Ren's reputation, now the tomb at the end of the bar — the Case was a well-run boat in a falling tide, and the tide was the drink itself, the want for it, being turned down house by house across the whole grey coast like a town going to bed early, a light here, a light there, nothing you could stand at your own door and point to; Trish Lofthouse dry, Kirsty's mother on her bike, four lads from the rugby club banded for the fitness and drinking kombucha out of tins with wolves on them; Wally.

"We're all right," Ren said, watching him read. "Col. We're all right. Food's the business now and we do food. I can do more food. It's the ones that can't I'd worry for — the Ship's gone quiz-and-crisps only, Pauline says the George at Withby's shut its kitchen and its back bar both. We're the lucky ones."

"Aye," Col said, and squared the printout on the bar, edge to edge, the way Wally squared a glass. "The lucky ones."

He rolled more that summer. That was the other line on a chart nobody printed: the tin going round to the yard with him nine, ten times a shift where it had been five, the little ceremony of paper and thumbs that was the one thing in the day that had never asked him for anything and never changed its terms. Ren clocked it — Ren clocked everything, she'd clocked the fourth trip before six one Friday and he'd watched her decide, visibly, in the door of the kitchen with a tray on her hip, to say nothing, and understood the shape of the treaty exactly, because it was the same one he held to about her band: her numbers were hers, his lungs were his, and a marriage this old ran on the things you were owed and didn't collect.

Dane was about the place all season, in the way of a lad courting the daughter of licensed premises: half guest, half unpaid staff, wholly unbothered by the distinction. He fixed the glasswasher's door spring without being asked, and re-hung the yard gate that had scraped for a decade, and when the new bottle fridge came he and Col walked it down the cellar steps between them on the sack-truck ropes, Col calling the pace, and the lad took instruction the way almost nobody took instruction any more, which was silently, exactly, and without needing it twice.

"You've been down cellars," Col said, at the bottom, by way of the highest compliment he had.

"Boats," Dane said. "Same thing upside down." He looked round the cellar with a tradesman's frankness — the chocked casks, the lines up on their hooks, the beech rack Col had built, the thirty years of it — and nodded slowly, and said, "me dad says you can tell a landlord by his cellar same as a skipper by his bilges," and then, deadpan, entirely his father's son: "he says you'd pass. He says don't tell you."

The boat itself was having the summer the pub was having; that came out in pieces, over weeks, the way things came out of Dane. The crab was there but the price was soft, the fuel wasn't, the quota paperwork had a new layer to it that Ted did at the kitchen table swearing at a laptop like it owed him money, and the Constance's engine was on its second rebuilt injector pump with the yard at Grimsby quoting silly for a reconditioned unit. None of it was a crisis. All of it was weather from one direction, week on week, and once, at the bar, at the end of a long Saturday, the lad said the nearest thing to a dark thing Col ever heard from him that year — looking at his own hands round a glass of Ren's lemonade, seventeen months before everything — he said: "Everything I'm any good at's getting cheaper. Funny, that. You put your ten years in and the world marks it down while you're learning it." And then he'd caught himself, and grinned, and said, "listen to me, me dad'll be wanting royalties," and taken his lemonade off to find Jassy, and Col had wiped the same stretch of bar twice, because he'd heard the sentence a beat after the boy said it, the way you taste a wrong cask a beat late, and by then the lad was across the room laughing at something and there was nothing to say it to.

The new ones reached the Case's airspace in mid-August, via Bob-the-Post, via the paper, as everything did.

"Four thousand pound," Bob announced, to the bar in general, from behind a folded page. "The new Alices. Alice Once. You have it done the one time and that's you. Permanent." He let the word sit like a quiz answer. "There's a fella here — listen to this — fella in Surrey's had his done for cheese. Cheese. Says it ruled his life. 'I would drive at night to a garage for cheese.' Four thousand pound and now he — " Bob checked the paper — "'regards it as I would regard gravel.'"

"Gravel," said Sandra Leggett, appalled, delighted. "You couldn't pay me. What if you change your mind? What if he's at a do, and there's a nice bit of brie going round — "

"He doesn't want the brie, Sandra, that's the four thousand pound."

"I know he doesn't want it. That's what I'm saying. What if he wants to want it?"

There was a short silence at the bar while everybody had a go at that sentence, Sandra included.

"You want a lie down, you," said Big Sandra.

"I'm only saying what if," said Sandra, standing her ground with the jug. "Renting you can hand your notice in. Buying you're stopped with it. I'd rent. I'd rent me improvements same as I'd rent a telly, then when it all goes wrong it's their problem," and she bore the jug away to the darts table in triumph, having, in Col's considered opinion, out-argued the newspaper, the fella in Surrey, and very possibly the whole of Lares Health, not that one word of it would matter to anybody with four thousand pound and a want they hated, because you couldn't argue a man out of a door he was already through.

Wally heard all of it from the third stool without looking up from the Post, eight months dry, lime and soda at the low-tide mark, and said nothing until Bob had moved on to the weather, and then said it not to Bob, not really to Col, to the glass, in the mild way he said the things that were the actual minutes of the meeting:

"Mine's the renting kind."

"Aye," Col said.

"Twenty pound a month, the NHS finds it, and I wear it regular, and that's me." He turned a page of the Post. "Our Lynn looked it all up. She rings Thursdays now, checks I've — she calls it me rent. 'You paid your rent, Dad?'" He smiled at the racing, some horse or other. "First rent I've paid in me life where you get the house."

The season turned the way it turned, all at once, the last week of August: the light went brass, the caravans began their long crawl west on the Sunday, the arcades pulled their doors half-to, and the Turbines' advance party reappeared for the winter cabling — Mackie among them, browner, engaged now, showing the bar a photo of a girl in Middlesbrough — and the Case exhaled and settled into its bones for the quiet stretch, and on the first cold Tuesday of September, Col stood at the tomb at the end of the bar at half seven in the morning, before anyone, in the smell of the beans the child with the beard had sold him, and made himself a flat white in his own pub, and stood drinking it looking out through his own window at Keel Street in the rain — a thing that had never once happened in sixty-one years of the licence, no Harker had ever stood in this room at dawn drinking milk — and Guinness watched him do it from the crisp box with an expression Col declined to interpret, and it was, he was prepared to admit to the cat and no one else, not bad. It was not bad. That was the trouble with the whole business, start to finish: none of it was bad. It was all improvements. He rinsed the cup, and wiped the wand the way the beard had shown him, and went down to do the lines.


==============================================================================

13

Ms Adeoti kept a postcard rack on her desk, the kind newsagents had, rescued from a skip, and it was the departmental filing system: if she thought you were coasting she took a card off it and put it on your work without a word, and the card was always exactly aimed, and you were left to work out what it was accusing you of. In September of upper sixth Jassy got the Barbican in the rain. In October she got a Hokusai wave. In the last week of October she got, laid diagonally across her half-done perspective study, a card she hadn't seen before: a photograph of scaffolding at night, floodlit, no building visible at all, just the scaffolding, and on the back, in Adeoti's tiny capitals: THE BUILDING IS THE EASY BIT. SHOW THEM THE THINKING.

"Your portfolio's good," Adeoti said, at the desk crit, in the voice she used sparingly, the flat one with no teacherly cushion in it. "Good gets you a polite letter. I've seen what gets them out of their chairs and it's not finish, finish bores them, they can buy finish. It's appetite. They want to watch somebody find a problem and refuse to leave it alone." She turned the pages of the blue sketchbook, which Jassy had surrendered under protest, to the arcade canopy, the rain-fall margin note, the redrawn gutter tucked inside its slim posts. "Like that. Whose question was that?"

"A — friend asked it."

"Keep the friend," Adeoti said, and took the sketchbook hostage for moderation, and booked her, in the same breath, without asking, onto the coach the art department shared with the Withby school for the London open days, first week of November, because — and this she said with the postcard-rack economy that made her the only teacher Jassy quoted at home — "you can't want a place you've never smelled."

The coach left at six and Poll came, which Poll had arranged within four minutes of hearing about it, on the grounds that Jassy could not be trusted alone in London ("you'll walk into a bollard looking at a cornice, you want a carer"), and the Withby kids slept and the coach smelled of coach, and Jassy watched the country change out the window in the dawn — the flat wet fields going, the towns thickening, everything getting nearer to everything else — and kept the sketchbook shut on her knees the whole way down like a passport.

What she had not been prepared for, by the prospectus or the postcard or two years of the wardrobe door, was the sound.

They came up out of Euston into the middle of it and it hit her like the sea hit you, all at once and everywhere: not noise exactly, a kind of running-water made of traffic and languages and heels and delivery bikes, ten thousand private hurries braided into one public roar, and Poll grabbed her arm and shouted "we're IN the telly" and Jassy stood on the pavement being knocked and apologised to by people from everywhere on earth and felt something in her chest go out and out and not hit a wall. That was the thing. That was the whole thing, and she'd never have got it off a page: at home every sightline ended — sea one way, fields the other, the end of the front, the last house — and here the town just kept being more town in every direction for further than a day could walk, and nobody in it knew whose daughter she was, and the anonymity, which was supposed to be the frightening part, the part the aunties warned about, went into her like oxygen.

The open day itself she took notes at like a spy. The school of architecture was a scuffed warren that smelled of laser-cutter and coffee, which she had not expected and loved — she'd braced for marble and got MDF, braced for hush and got a corridor of studio spaces in glorious workshop squalor, models everywhere, models made of card and wax and bent brass and one apparently of sugar, drawings pinned four deep with arguments written on them in other people's handwriting, PLAN DOESN'T AGREE WITH SECTION, WHY THIS ANGLE?, MORE OF THIS — and a second-year with paint on her forearms who showed them round and used the word "crit" the way soldiers said "contact," and told them, cheerful, feral, "you basically live here, it eats your life, it's brilliant," and Jassy stood in the doorway of a studio watching six students argue at a wall about a folded piece of card, genuinely argue, leaning in, jabbing at it, caring, and had to make herself breathe out.

Because that was it. There. Not the white staircase, though they went and found the white staircase too, her and Poll, after — it was in a house museum now, you could just walk in, and it was smaller than the postcard and better, it went up through the building like a poured thing exactly as advertised and Poll looked at it and said, loyally, "it's a nice staircase," and Jassy laughed till the attendant looked over — not the staircase, the wall of arguing strangers: a whole building full of people whose eyes did what hers did, who saw parapet lines and fire-escape geometry and where the rain went, and argued about it out loud like it was football, like it was normal. Seventeen years she'd been the only one she knew with the eye — the staring, Poll called it, your staring — and it turned out there was a building in Bloomsbury where the staring was the entrance requirement, where they took your staring and trained it and hung it on walls and shouted at it fondly, and you could go there, they let you apply to go there. She wrote nothing down in the sketchbook the whole visit. She sat on the coach home in the dark unable to talk, with Poll asleep on her shoulder smelling of the Zara they'd been in, and looked at her own reflection running over the black fields, and the reflection had its jaw set exactly like her mother cashing up, and inside the reflection the thing had gone from want to plan the way water goes to ice: same stuff, and you could stand on it now.

Dane was at the station.

Eleven o'clock, the last leg from Grimsby, four passengers off it and two of them hers — Poll's mum had the car up by the taxi rank — and Dane on the cold platform in his good coat with his hands in his pockets, come to walk her home, which was the kind of thing he did without announcing it, and her whole body was glad to see him before she'd decided anything, that was still true, that stayed true throughout, whatever the year after did to the meaning of it.

"Well?" he said, taking her bag.

And she talked. Marram went by them dark and shut and she did the whole day at him in one unbroken transmission — the sound coming up out of Euston, the corridor of studios, the sugar model, the second-year with the paint, PLAN DOESN'T AGREE WITH SECTION, the staring having a building — hands going, freezing air, her breath coming out as flags, and Dane walked beside her carrying the bag and listening the way he listened, fully, asking the two practical questions nobody else would have asked (what did the models cost to make; did the students look after their tools), and at her gate, under the light, he looked at her lit-up wind-red face for a second longer than the conversation strictly required, and smiled, and said:

"You want to see your face."

"Shut up."

"No — " and he shook his head slightly, not teasing, or not only, some other thing moving under it that eleven-o'clock light off a porch bulb was too poor to show her, or that she was too full of Bloomsbury to develop — "it's a good face. It's the fish-gutting face. First one clean, nose to tail." He handed her the bag back. "Night, Jass. Go on, your mum's up, the kitchen light's doing its thing."

And he walked off down Keel Street with his hands back in his pockets, unhurried, a local sound, his boots the only feet out, and she watched him under the lamps for a bit, warm all through with the day and with him, and went in and reported London to her waiting mother at a volume that woke her father, and it was, all of it, that whole November night, as good as anything had ever been.

The van came the following Saturday, end-of-season ritual, Kye's Transit crawling the caravan park's lanes like an ice-cream man for the overwintering trade: vapes, phone cases, telly boxes that let you watch the football for nothing, air rifle pellets, and this year, in a shoebox on the passenger seat, bands.

"Loose ones," Kye said, in Poll's nan's old static, which was Poll's own territory now — her nan had left it her outright, a 1998 Pemberton with a leopard throw and a nail table in the end bedroom and a sticker in the window that still said NO COLD CALLERS which Kye, family, did not count as — "unlocked. Off the phones lads in Leeds. It's the same band, that's what people don't get, it's the identical hardware, they just — " he did a thing with his fingers like picking a small lock — "take the manners off it. No sign-up, no subscription, no doctor's note, no it-has-to-ring-Lares-every-morning. Yours. Forty quid." He said it without heat, a man listing the spec of a phone case. That was Kye all over: he had the family volume turned all the way down and something else turned up that nobody had ever named; he watched rooms. He'd sat whole evenings in the Case on one lime and soda, watching the room, pleasant, unreadable, and Wally, who missed nothing from the third stool, had once delivered a full Kye report in four words: "that one counts doors."

"Forty," said Poll, already holding it.

"For you, forty. They're ninety in Leeds."

"They're not ninety in Leeds, Kye. You're robbing me because I'm family."

"I'm robbing you gently because you're family."

It looked exactly like Ren's, exactly like Poll's own prescription one, grey, weightless, the little light asleep — and when Poll put it on and thumbed the stud the light breathed up and a voice said hello, and it was the voice, the same even weather-of-nowhere voice out of every kitchen in England, and the two girls got the giggles at the sheer wrongness of it, this NHS-waiting-room voice coming out of Kye's shoebox economy like a vicar dealing three-card brag. Poll made it recite. It would recite anything: it did the specials board off a photo, it did Kye's own sales pitch back at him in received pronunciation, "take the manners off it, yours, forty quid," which even Kye enjoyed; and when Poll, testing, said, "right, make me stop biting me nails," it said, pleasantly, no gear-change at all, "All right. We can start that whenever you like. Do you want me to begin tonight?" — and there was a small silence in the static, one degree too long, before Poll snatched it off her head and said, "you're all right, love, I'm keeping me vices," and dropped it in the nail table's third drawer with the cuticle sticks and the spare rings, and Kye pocketed his forty, and the leopard throw got straightened, and somebody put the kettle on, and that was all, that was the entire event: a Saturday, a shoebox, a drawer shutting.


==============================================================================

14

Her own course took in the fifth week, and what Nita noticed first was the silence where the arithmetic had been.

Not the wanting — the wanting went the way everyone said, quietly, while you were doing something else, a tide out so slow you never caught it moving — but the arithmetic had been the real tenant, she saw that only after it left: the constant background bookkeeping, what was in the purse against what was owed against what Friday would bring against what a good scratch of the blue ones might, the sums running under every till queue and every advert break like a pump you'd stopped hearing. It stopped. The whole department shut.
