She stood in the Costcutter queue in the third week of August behind a man buying charcoal, level with the stand, the golden multipacks fanned out at elbow height, and her head was full of Elsie Brand's dressing schedule and nothing else, nothing else at all, and she paid for the milk and was out the door before she realised she had just done the thing that for six years had been like walking a dog past a butcher's.

There was one strange grief in it, and she gave it one evening and no more. The cards had never once been about winning; she'd known that professionally even while she scratched; what the cards had sold her was ten minutes, twice a day, in which it was not yet settled — in which the day had a curtain in it, and behind the curtain the water bill might already be paid, the roof might already be fixed, the whole grinding known-in-advance shape of the month might, just possibly, already be otherwise. Hope, at 20p the unit, cut with something, the way everything cheap is cut with something. The band had taken the dealer and the hope had gone with it, and for a fortnight the days felt exactly as they were, fully lit, no curtain, every bill visible to the horizon, and she sat with that one Tuesday evening, let it be what it was, and then got up and did the ironing, because the other thing eleven years of doorways taught you was which griefs to feed.

Peg said nothing for a month, and then, one Sunday, apropos the crossword, not looking up:

"You've stopped rustling."

"What?"

"Rustling. In your coat. You rustled for years, every time you sat down, like a woman smuggling crisps." Peg filled in a clue, licked the pencil, considered. "Your father rustled. Different pocket, same coat." And that was the entire conversation, the whole of what Peg knew and when she had known it laid out flat in four sentences and folded away again, and Nita sat at her own mother's table, forty-seven years old, twenty-two years qualified, and felt her ears go hot like a girl caught at the tin — the tin, which Peg had never once mentioned, which Peg had re-counted God knew when, which Peg would take to her grave in the sideboard of things Peg carried for people — and Peg, without looking up, pushed the biscuits across.

The flower show had been the second Saturday of August, and Vera Trant had judged it for eleven years, and this year the judging was done by a woman from the Withby society, and Marram noticed the way Marram noticed everything, briefly, kindly, and moving on.

"She found the standing difficult last year," Roy said — to the vicar, at the church door; Nita was there for Peg, who took flowers monthly to her sister's plot and had views on the state of the tap. "The heat, and being on her feet, and everyone wanting a word. She was wrung out for days, weren't you, Vee. We decided we'd sooner keep our Saturdays gentle."

"It was a lovely show," Vera said, pleasantly, to the vicar, to the air. She was in a good frock, holding Roy's arm, her face turned up into the September light. "The dahlias."

"The dahlias were down on last year," the vicar said, mock-sorrowing, making conversation.

"Were they," said Vera, comfortable, agreeing with something, her hand on Roy's sleeve, and the vicar laughed and it went by; it all always went by; that was the discovery Nita kept making in stages that autumn, each time from slightly closer: there was no moment. She had been trained on moments — the bruise, the burn, the flinch when a door went, the toilet visited too long, the word said too quick and taken back — twenty-two years of safeguarding updates had drilled her on a body of signs, and the signs all assumed a wanting held down by force, a fear with somewhere to run. Whatever was happening in the bungalow behind the monkey puzzle had no signs. It photographed, week on week, as a devoted man and a wife growing gently vaguer at his side, agreeing with the air; a couple in their seasons; nothing anyone could stand at a font and point to.

The sister rang the surgery in October.

It came to Nita sideways, the way everything real came, off the record: Deb on reception caught her by the coats — "you do the Trants, don't you" — and relayed it in reception shorthand. A Mrs Grace Holt had rung from Ripon, twice; wouldn't say what it was about because she didn't rightly know herself what it was about; her sister Vera hadn't rung her back since — she'd had to work it out on the calendar — March. They'd rung each other Sundays their whole lives. She'd rung Roy about it and Roy had been lovely, Roy was always lovely, said Vee was very well but found the phone tiring these days, got herself in a muddle with it, they'd changed the handset and it was all buttons — and the thing was, Mrs Holt said, and Deb did the voice, the north-Yorkshire flatness of a woman embarrassed to be saying it out loud to a stranger: our Vee's rung me from a phone box in a power cut. Our Vee could ring me from the moon. I don't know what I'm reporting and I know I'm reporting something. Is there not somebody whose job it is to know what I'm reporting?

"I said the practice can't discuss patients," Deb said, "and she said she wasn't asking me to discuss, she was asking me to look. She said them exact words. 'I'm asking somebody to look.'" Deb, forty years on that desk, mother confessor to three villages, looked at Nita over the coats. "I've put a note on the system. You know what a note on the system does."

Nita knew what a note on the system did. It sat.

She raised it with Bassey the following Friday, end of clinic, in the two-minute window between his last patient and his paperwork, and she did it carefully, in the trade's neutral dialect — a family member expressing concern, a change in social functioning, query cognitive versus query mood, worth a review? — and Bassey listened with his glasses pushed up on his forehead, tired the way Friday made him tired, and pulled up the file, and turned the screen half toward her, which he didn't have to do.

"She was seen in February. Full works — bloods, cognition screen, the memory clinic questions. Scored clean for her age. There's the consent for the band, witnessed, capacity recorded, all present and correct — it's a clinical-grade course, prescribed private through the Hull place but it's all lodged with us, Roy's punctilious about the paperwork, I'll give him that, there's not a comma missing." He scrolled, unhurried. "Anxiety, the referral says. Lifelong, worse with age, worse at night. And between you and me and thirty years of it, Nita — that woman sat in this room more times than I can count, over the years, with her hands going like this — " he did it, the dry-wash, one hand over the other — "about the sister, about the church rota, about Roy's heart, about whether she'd said the wrong thing to somebody in 1994. She wore herself to wire with it. If the band's took that off her — " he let the screen go dark, and rubbed his eyes, and said the rest into his hands, a man at the end of a Friday at the end of a long argument he was mostly having with himself — "I've patients I can't get seen that are drinking bleach, near enough. I can't work up the well ones for being calm. Peaceful isn't a symptom. There's no referral pathway for a woman whose sister thinks she's too settled."

"And the phone?" Nita said. "Sundays, her whole life, and it stops in March?"

"People's worlds get small at that age. It's the commonest thing I see." He said it gently, and he believed it, and it was true; that was the trouble with all of it, every plank was true. "Keep an eye, by all means. You're in there for his INR regardless. Eyes open, notes — " he caught himself; even Bassey heard how it sounded — "proper notes, on the system, if anything clinical presents. All right?"

If anything clinical presents. She drove home past the front, past the shut arcades, with the phrase riding along in the passenger seat where the other thing usually sat. It was the right phrase. It was what there was. Twenty-two years she had worked inside what there was, and mostly what there was, was enough, and where it wasn't you logged and referred and escalated and the machinery ground and sometimes saved somebody; the machinery had saved more people than any nurse's intuition ever had, she'd have said so at a tribunal, she'd have meant it. But the machinery ran on presentation, and what was presenting at the bungalow on Church Lane was contentment. A well-kept, well-consented, fully documented contentment, thickening by the season like paint going on coat over coat: the cards gone to a box of thirty, the show judged by Withby, the Sundays gone quiet, the spare-room door — she'd clocked it in September, doing Roy's bloods, the door that had stood ajar for years now closed, and a key in it, and she had no way of saying even to herself what a closed door meant in a house of closed doors, that was the whole method of the thing, if it was a thing: every plank true, every step consented, nothing anywhere but a man doing all that, it's easier, and a woman at a hatch with her palm up, being handed less and less and thanking him.

Peg was asleep in the chair when she got in, the snooker murmuring to itself, and Nita stood in her own hall longer than a person stands in a hall.

Then she went up, and knelt at the bottom of the wardrobe, and found the shoebox with the old diaries — she'd kept a diary at twenty-five, every district nurse briefly keeps a diary at twenty-five, until the job teaches you where notes belong — and took out the last of them, the 2009 one, three-quarters empty, and sat on the edge of the bed, and looked at it for a while without opening it.

She knew exactly what it was and exactly what it wasn't. It wasn't the system. Nothing written in a hardback diary in a nurse's bedroom was a record; it had no standing; it was, if it was ever anything, the opposite of protection — a private file on a private citizen kept by a woman with a professional duty to keep her files where the profession could see them. She'd sat through the information governance training same as everyone, eleven times, a sandwich and a slide deck, contemporaneous, attributable, on the record. She could hear the exact voice the words would be said in, later, if there was a later, by somebody with a lanyard: and why, Nurse Osei, if you were concerned, did you record none of this concern in the appropriate—

Because the appropriate, she answered the lanyard, at nine o'clock on a Friday night, on the edge of her bed, opening the diary to the first clean page — because the appropriate has a box for a bruise and no box for a hatch. Because a note on the system sits. Because somebody rang from Ripon and said I'm asking somebody to look, and the looking has to live somewhere while it finds out what it's looking at.

She clicked the biro.

Oct 17. V.T. Flower show missed (1st in 11 yrs). Cards bought not painted, 2nd yr. Sundays to Grace stopped ~March. Spare rm door now locked (why lock an empty rm?). Landline "all buttons." She said "the dahlias" to the vicar — the dahlias were down; she wasn't there to see them. Every plank true. Write the bus in.

She read it back, and dated it, and put the diary in the drawer of the bedside table, under the church magazine, and turned the lamp off, and lay listening to the snooker murmur up through the floor until Peg woke herself with her own snoring and went, grumbling, to bed.


==============================================================================

15

The personal statement went through eleven drafts, and the eleventh was the second one with the adjectives taken back out, which Ms Adeoti could have told her in October and did tell her in October, and Jassy had to travel the whole loop herself anyway, that being, as far as she could establish, the entire nature of learning anything.

Dane read every draft. This was not a service she'd asked for; it had established itself the way his services did, quietly, at the kitchen table of the flat over the chippy where his dad never sat because Ted Pyke ate standing up at the counter like a man expecting a shout from the harbour, and Dane would read the draft of the week with a biro held the way he held a filleting knife, and he was, it turned out, a pitiless line editor, because he read the way he listened, fully, believing every word had been meant.

"You've 'passionate' twice."

"Everyone's got passionate twice. It's the rules."

"You gut fish with me. I've seen you passionate. It looked nowt like this." He drew his slow line through both of them. "And this — 'the built environment.' Why's it 'the built environment' here and 'buildings' everywhere else?"

"Because it sounds — " she heard it before she finished it, the way you did with him, his questions were mirrors with no flattery in them — "because it sounds like a prospectus. Fine. Buildings."

"Buildings," he agreed, and initialled the margin, D, the way the moderators did, which had begun as a joke and now was simply what happened, so that the blue sketchbook and the statement and the whole paper trail of her future going out into the world carried, in the margins, in pencil, the letter of the person it was all going to be sent away from.

If he did the arithmetic on that, it never showed at the table. That was the winter she'd have sworn — did swear, to Poll, in the static, under the leopard throw, the two of them doing the toes-freezing post-mortem of the year that girls did in December — that she'd got the last good one, the only lad on the coast who could hear the word London and hand you a biro; and Poll, threading her own eyebrow in the nail-table mirror, said, "yeah," and then, to the mirror, in the voice she used for the things she was deciding whether to say: "he asked our Kye about vans, did you know that? What a decent one costs." Tweeze. "Don't look at me like that. It's for the tools. He's pricing his own little round, Withby to Marram, boilers-and-that. Man with a van." She inspected her brow line. "I'm just saying. You're doing your statements. He's doing his."

The offer of choices went in the first week of December, all five, ranked in her head though the form pretended they weren't ranked: the one that mattered, then Sheffield, Newcastle, the Manchester one — and Hull, which Adeoti had made her keep against every protest, the local safety, forty minutes up the line, a course nobody in Bloomsbury had ever heard of. "You'll never need it," Adeoti had said, clipping the rack with a new postcard, "and you'll keep it anyway. That's what insurance is. It's not a prophecy, it's a handrail." Jassy had typed it in last, resentfully, the way you'd write a plain cousin's name last on wedding invitations, and clicked send on the whole thing at 4:52 on a Tuesday with Poll filming her for the nan-archive and her mum downstairs pretending not to hover, and then it was done, gone, out of her hands and into January, and the house exhaled, and Marram pulled its lights on for Christmas, snowflake, star, snowflake.

The parcel appeared under the tree at the flat on Christmas Eve with no label, which meant Dane, who held that labels on presents in a house of three people were administration for its own sake.

It was long and flat and heavier than it looked, and she knew by the weight it was made, not bought, and made things from Dane arrived maybe twice a year and were never small even when they were small, and she got the paper off — careful, she was always careful with paper, and never more glad of it — and sat there on the floor of the front room with the fire going and the telly murmuring the carols from somewhere, holding it, not saying anything long enough that Dane's ears went red.

It was a portfolio case. A2, to the half-inch — he'd measured her board one Sunday, she realised backwards, with the tape he kept in the van, she'd thought he was pricing shelves — in marine ply the colour of pale honey, edges rounded and waxed, the corners finger-jointed like a piece of furniture, with two brass toggle catches off a boat chandler's and a handle wrapped in whipped cord, proper whipping, sailmaker's work, that would never once slip in a wet hand. Inside, the lid was lined with cork, and there was a flat ribbon of webbing to hold the sheets down, and in the bottom corner, branded small into the wood with what she guessed was a hot nail, the only label anywhere: J.H.

"It's over-engineered," Dane said, to the fire, when the silence got past what he could stand. "I know it's over-engineered. But them card ones go at the corners, and you'll be — " he did a vague thing with his hand that took in trains, cities, weather, the world — "lugging it about. Interviews and that. It rains everywhere, not just here." He cleared his throat. "There's a fall on the lid. Quarter inch. You'll not see it."

She checked. Later, at home, with her dad's straightedge, because checking was the correct tribute and they both knew it: there was, across the whole width of the lid, invisible, exactly as promised, a quarter-inch fall, so that a case set down flat in the rain would shed the water off the back edge, away from the catches; and she stood in her room with the straightedge in her hand and the case on the bed shining like something out of a better century, and understood that she had been given, by a nineteen-year-old crabber's son who ate his tea standing up, the single most eloquent object anyone would ever make her, and that every joint of it said going and every joint of it said properly and not one splinter of it anywhere said stay; and she was eighteen years and three weeks short of understanding what that must have cost to build, because at eighteen minus three weeks you still think generosity is a temperament, and not a thing a person does with both hands while it takes the skin off them.

Christmas itself was the pub, was family, was the year doing its oldest tricks. Wally came Christmas night with a tin of Roses the size of a drum and news of Grimsby — the little one had been a shepherd; there was video; you were not asked whether you wished to see the video — and Ren did the goose, and her dad gave her mum a bobble hat with LIFEGUARD knitted into it that made her mum do the real laugh, and gave Jassy a box of Rotring pens, the good ones, German, that he'd have had to have asked somebody about, had to have gone in somewhere in Lincoln and said the words technical drawing to a stranger, and Jassy looked at the pens and briefly could not manage her face.

And at midnight, teeth cleaned, house settling, she stood at the wardrobe door the way she'd stood at it since she was fifteen — the white staircase, the bus map, the star on Bloomsbury, and now, propped against the wall beneath it all like an altar step, a honey-coloured case with her initials burned in the corner — and heard, through the old floor, her parents' voices below in the bar doing their end-of-day murmur, no words, just the weather of it, the sound she'd fallen asleep to her whole life; and if you had stood her there and made her choose that night — the door or the floor, the map or the murmur — she'd have said, without hesitating, both, obviously, both, you go and it all stays here and you come back to it, that's how it works, it's forty minutes to Hull and two hours forty to London, it's trains, it's not dying — and she'd have been right, that was the thing. It was all still true, that night.


==============================================================================

16

Sandra Leggett gave her notice on a Wednesday in December, in the sense that she came to the bar at the interval with the jug, and didn't order crisps.

Sandra had ordered four packets of cheese-and-onion at the interval of every quiz night since the Blair government: two for the table, one for her handbag, one eaten standing at the bar while Col got the jug, "to keep me strength up for carrying it." It was as fixed as the tide table. And in December she stood at the bar with the jug and her purse and looked at the crisp boxes behind the glass the way you'd look at an old address you used to live at, and said, "just the jug, love," and Col drew it and said nothing, and Big Sandra, arriving at her shoulder, said it for the room:

"She's been done. Tell him."

"I've not been done — "

"Four weeks ago. For the snacking. Her Debbie paid, it was her Christmas present, they do vouchers now — vouchers, Col — and now look at her. She's lost half a stone and all her conversation."

"It was me knees, Col," Sandra said, with dignity. "It was never the weight, the doctor said, it was the knees carrying it. I can get down the cellar steps at ours now without coming down like a coal delivery." And then, her eyes going past him to the boxes, with the first honest bafflement Col had heard anybody put on it, the tone of a woman reporting a missing person rather than a diet: "It's not that I'm being good. I could murder — no. See, that's it. I can't even say the sentence. I went to say 'I could murder a packet of cheese-and-onion' and it's like saying you could murder a packet of — grouting. The words work but there's nothing behind them." She picked up the jug. "Forty year, Col. Me and cheese-and-onion. It's like hearing an old song and you can't remember which boyfriend it went with."

She still came Wednesdays — that was the bit the trade papers would never print, Col thought, wiping down; the diet ones stayed, it was only their two quid of crisps that left — but something had gone off the table under the window all the same. The crisps had been the table's currency: opened flat, the foil smoothed into a communal dish, everyone's fingers in, Sandra swatting Bob's hand off the last big one. Now the table had a jug and a bag of nuts Big Sandra bought defiantly and ate alone, and the quiz answers came just the same, and the laughing came just the same, or nearly, and Col served them all winter and could never afterwards have told you one thing that was wrong at that table except that he'd have paid money to see the foil smoothed out flat again and everybody's fingers in it.

The interview letter came four days before Christmas, and it was an email, but Jassy printed it, which told you everything about the size of it: the school of architecture requesting the pleasure, etc., of J. Harker and her portfolio, ten past two on the eighth of January, bring one A2 case, and Ren stuck it to the fridge with the magnet that had held the school dinner menus since 2013, and the fridge became a shrine, and the flat over the Case reorganised itself around the eighth of January the way it had once reorganised around a christening.

The practising happened in the function room, because the function room had the long tables. That was where Col found her, the Sunday after Boxing Day, when the pub below was shut and dark and the building had its winter-afternoon feel, radiators ticking, gulls on the chimneys, and his daughter had the whole of her folio laid out down two trestles in the grey light from the too-small window she'd once condemned, and was walking the line of it with her arms folded, back and forth, the exact patrol he did along the pumps before opening.

"Talk me through it, then," Col said, from the door. "If you're going to do it at strangers, you can do it at me."

"You'll not get it."

"I'll not get the words. I'll get whether you believe them. That's most of what a stranger gets." Which was — she stopped patrolling and looked at him with a look he recognised as one of Ren's arriving at a new estimate of him — the wisest thing he'd said all year, so he ruined it, on principle: "And I'm free, and I've a bag of Bombay mix."

So she did it at him. All of it, sheet by sheet, up the left trestle and down the right, and he stood with his Bombay mix and got maybe one word in five — datum, threshold, section, massing — and got, at the same time, the way you'd get the health of a cask off the first inch of the pull, all of it: which sheets she trusted, which she went fast past, where her voice sat down into itself and the words stopped mattering because the thing underneath them was carrying, and it carried longest at the arcade — the front, the canopy, the drawn version of the town he actually lived in, made strange and kept — and at a small sheet near the end he hadn't seen before, a measured drawing, hand-done, of a staircase. Not the London one off her wardrobe. Theirs. The pub's back stairs, the crooked ones, third tread doubled where some Harker-before-Harkers had sistered it, the cat's stair, the whole warped familiar climb of it surveyed and inked like a cathedral.

"That one's the risers all different," Col said, around the Bombay mix, because saying it straight was not available to him. "You've drawn every riser different."

"They are all different. That's the point of measuring, Dad. You draw what's there, not what's meant to be there." She squared the sheet with two fingers. "Adeoti says put one thing in that only I could have. Nobody else has got our stairs."

"Nobody else would want 'em."

"Nobody else knows the third one's doubled," Jassy said. "You feel it through your feet in the dark and you know where you are. That's — " and she stopped, because she'd started saying it to him instead of at him, and seventeen-going-on-eighteen has rules about that, and she turned it into shop: "anyway, it's a datum. Whole folio starts from it. Adeoti near cried, it was embarrassing," and Col ate his Bombay mix and looked at the drawing of his own back stairs done to the half-inch by his own daughter for the inspection of strangers in Bloomsbury, the doubled tread that his feet knew and now, evidently, always had been architecture, and thought that if the strangers didn't get out of their chairs for that then Bloomsbury was beyond help, and said:

"Aye. Well. It's a staircase, all right." Which in Harker was a standing ovation both of them heard perfectly, and she let him carry the trestles in after, which was the accepting of it.

The case got its final inspection that same evening, downstairs, by lamplight — Col's inspection, conducted while Jassy was in the bath and deniable. He'd been circling the thing since Christmas Eve: Dane's case, the honey-coloured job, stood by the door of the function room with her initials burned in the corner, and Col got it under the lamp and went over it the way he'd go over a cask he suspected of being sound — the finger-joints, the catches let in flush, the whipping on the handle done tight and finished under itself, the lid with its sly quarter-inch fall that the eye forgave and the rain wouldn't argue with — looking, if he was honest, and at the corner of his own bar at eleven at night a man may as well be, for the flaw. One proud edge. One catch set a hair off square. Anything. He fetched the little engineer's square from the cellar drawer to help him look. He was down there twenty minutes with the lamp pulled close, and the marine ply glowed like the inside of a church, and there was no flaw, there was nothing anywhere but a boy's whole winter of evenings and boat-grade thinking and the sort of patience Col had met maybe four times in his life and married one of; and he put the square away, and stood the case back where it lived, exactly at the angle it had been, and said nothing to anybody, then or ever, about what he'd been down there doing — though Guinness had watched the whole procedure from the third stair, and Guinness knew a man checking another man's work when he saw one, and kept it, like everything else in that pub, to himself.

On the eighth of January Col drove her to the early train himself, the case flat on the back seat like royalty, the morning still black, the heater roaring, neither of them saying much because there was nothing to say that wouldn't have weighed something. On the platform she stood in her interview coat with her mother's scarf and the case in her fist, looking suddenly and completely like somebody he'd once seen off on a school trip and simultaneously like nobody he'd ever met, and the train came up the coast with its lights on out of the last of the dark, and he said, "Right then. Give 'em hell. Don't leave the case on the rack," and she said, "I love you too, Dad," which was fighting dirty, and got on, and he stood on the cold platform with his hands in his pockets while the train took her south along the edge of the grey waking sea, and watched it out of sight, the way you did; the way his own father had once stood, he supposed, watching a brewery lorry pull out with a sixteen-year-old up front in a cab too big for him; and then he went back to the car park, and sat in the car a minute, and rolled one, and didn't light it, and drove home to open up, because it was a Thursday, and the dray came Tuesdays, and the doors opened at eleven, and some things had to stand still so that other things could go.


==============================================================================

17

The email was there at three minutes past nine, which meant it had been sitting in the phone all night with the phone face-down on the drawing board, because she'd promised herself she'd stopped checking, and the promise had held right up until 9:02.

She read it standing in the middle of her room in the socks and the dressing gown, and then she read it again sitting on the floor, which was involuntary, the sitting, her legs had simply filed for independence, and the word was there both times, third line, it survived rereading: offer. Conditional. The condition was the grades, and the grades were hers to make, and that was the whole geometry of it now — nothing between her and the white staircase but her own two hands and an August morning — and she heard herself make a noise she'd have edited out of anyone else, and went downstairs at a speed that took the doubled third tread from memory, in the dark of the stairwell, without one conscious thought, her feet reading the house the way they'd read it her whole life, and arrived in the kitchen airborne.

Ren read it with both pairs of glasses and her lips moving and then did something Jassy had never once seen: put the phone down on the table, carefully, face up, took the tea towel off her shoulder, pressed it to her whole face with both hands, and stood like that, a woman in her own kitchen at nine in the morning with her face in a tea towel, for a good five seconds. Then she took it away and was Ren again, eyes swimming, voice steady as a shelf: "Right. Well. They're not fools, then. That's one worry down." And Col — Col had come up from the cellar at the commotion with a spanner still in his hand, and he read it one line at a time at arm's length the way he read the returns, and Jassy watched her news land in her father's face and watched her father's face decline, with a mighty effort, every single one of its available options, and he looked at her over the phone and what he said, in the end, with the spanner in one hand, in the voice he used for facts, was:

"Eighteen tomorrow and all. You've had some weeks, our Jass."

Which was, from him, the tea towel.

The party had been booked for the Saturday for a month regardless — you didn't turn eighteen in licensed premises without the function room doing its duty — and the offer landing in the same week only meant, as Poll put it, taking over the decoration with the full powers of her profession, "we're celebrating both, aren't we, so it's double budget," the budget being Poll's time and two rolls of foil curtain, and the function room got done out to a standard the room had frankly never known: the trestles skirted, the foil catching the light, a banner Poll had had printed in Louth that said 18 & OUTTA HERE, which made Ren laugh and then briskly find something to do in the kitchen, and the window — the too-small window, the bricked-up arch of the proper one still readable in the yard wall outside — strung with fairy lights, so that the room's one crime was, for a night, the best thing in it.

Everyone came. That was the thing about a pub daughter's eighteenth: it wasn't a guest list, it was the parish. Her college lot and Poll's salon lot and half the quiz teams; the Sandras, who gave her a card with fifty pounds in it and instructions to waste it; Bob-the-Post, who gave her a fountain pen "for the signing of important documents" and got choked telling her why; Wally, who arrived at his usual ten past five as though the party were an unusually crowded Tuesday, took up the third stool, accepted a lime and soda and a paper plate of Ren's sausage rolls, and presented her, at the bar hatch, with a small heavy parcel that turned out to be a brass barometer, aged to the colour of old ale, off his own wall — "it were me dad's; it's weather coming, not weather here, mind; you want to know what's coming where you're going" — and would accept no fuss about it, and did the Racing Post while the room filled up around him like a rock doing the tide.

Dane came at seven, straight off the water — they'd been out for the pots at dawn, the January prices being what they were — showered pink, in the good shirt, carrying nothing, because his present had been given at Christmas and stood upstairs against her wardrobe with her whole future packed flat inside it, and he found her in the crowd the way he did, without appearing to look, and said, "Now then, birthday girl," and she said, "that's tomorrow, this is offer girl," and he grinned and said, "aye, I heard — some architect's got in somewhere," and lifted her clean off the floor in front of everybody, which got the cheer going, and if his face did anything on the way down that wasn't in the script of the evening, it did it into her hair, where the light was bad, and nobody marked it, least of all her.

Her dad rang the bell at nine. He'd been dreading it visibly for days — Ren had offered to speak and been declined; there were forms — and the room went quiet with its glasses up, and Col Harker stood behind his own bar with his hand still on the bell rope and looked at his daughter for a moment across the heads, and said: "Jasmine Harker. Eighteen years old tomorrow. London's sent for her." He stopped. The room waited for the rest of the speech. There was no rest of the speech; anyone could see there had never been going to be a rest of the speech; there was a man at a bell with a full face and nothing he was prepared to do about it in public. "The Case Is Altered," he said finally, which was the pub's name and also, delivered like that, with the glass coming up, something else entirely, and the whole room roared it back at him — the Case Is Altered! — and drank, and somebody started the singing, and Ren appeared at his side with her hand flat on his back the way you'd steady a cask, and Jassy, hoisted onto a chair by Poll against her will and entirely willing, looked out over the lot of it — the foil, the lights, the Sandras, Wally's cap, Bob's pen in her fist, the boy in the good shirt clapping with his ears red, her whole life to date packed warm into one room her own hands had measured — and thought, with the clarity you get twice a decade: remember this. Whatever else. This.

The after-party was six of them and the leopard throw.

It was tradition by now, or two occasions running, which at eighteen is tradition: the hard core decanting to Poll's static after close — Jassy, Dane, Poll and Poll's Callum, Shauna from the college and Shauna's fella whose name genuinely was Baz — the gas heater going orange, the sea wind leaning on the van in gusts that rocked it like a berth, cans and the dregs of a prosecco, crisps in a washing-up bowl because Poll did nothing by halves, and the talk running down through the gears the way it does between one and three in the morning, from shrieking to stories to the long easy shallows where somebody's always half asleep and the heater ticks.

The band came out somewhere past two, as it had before, because Shauna hadn't seen it. Third drawer, under the cuticle sticks: Kye's forty-quid special, the little light waking up, the voice doing its even hello into the orange dark, and the girls put it through its turns — it recited the banner, 18 and outta here, in the voice of a newsreader; it did the shipping forecast for the gas heater, "Pemberton, southwest gale nine, crisps"; Baz asked it the football scores and it gave them, which impressed Baz beyond all the rest of the evening's wonders; and Baz, emboldened, playing to the room, asked it what his chances were with Shauna, and the voice was quiet for a moment — one degree too long — and then said, pleasantly, "You've checked whether she's laughing after every joke you've made since ten o'clock. She was. The last four especially." And the van went up — how does it KNOW that, it's got no eyes, Baz gone the colour of the heater — and Poll, wiping her own eyes, said, "it listens, don't it, that's all they do all day, our Kye says that's why they're forty quid," and the shrieking folded back into the game, because it was gone two and everything was funny — and Poll, ceremonial, lifted it last onto Jassy's head, fitting it over her hair with both hands like a coronation, "there — crowned. Eighteen. Queen of the front."

"It suits her," the voice said mildly, from Jassy's own hairline, and the whole van screamed, and that was the peak of it, that was the joke of the year so far, and the band stayed on because it weighed nothing and the prosecco was down to the sweet end and she was eighteen years old that very minute, or near enough, and the heater was warm and Dane's shoulder was where it always was, and the talk went on over her in the good low voices, Poll and Callum murmuring, Shauna laughing at something with the laugh gone soft, the wind doing its long leans, and Jassy let her eyes go and came back twice and let them go properly, mid-sentence, some sentence, hers or somebody's, it didn't matter, it would keep till morning, everything would keep till morning —

The heater ticked. The wind leaned and let go.

Poll and Callum had taken the end room at three; Shauna and Baz were gone before that, headlights swinging once across the curtains and away. The van settled the way vans do, in small sounds, and Dane Pyke sat on the floor with his back against the bench seat and his fourth can warm in his hand, not drinking it, watching the gas glow.

Jassy slept on the bench behind him under the leopard throw. He'd put it over her at some point, tucked the shoulder of it the way you'd dress a wound, gently, so as not to wake; her shoes he'd eased off earlier and paired on the floor by the wheel arch, heel to the wall, because his hands did that whether or not he asked them to. Her breathing had gone long. The little light rode above her ear, up and down, up and down, slow as a buoy in the swell, and he watched it for a long time.

From the harbour, on the gusts, the halyards. Two-forty, three. He turned the can in his hand and didn't drink it.

When he moved it was quietly, and only closer, kneeling by the bench with his elbows on the cushion's edge like a lad at prayers, his face down near her hair, near the band, and he stayed there a moment breathing her breathing, and then he said — barely at all, mouth hardly working, a whisper shaped mostly of air, the way you'd speak on a boat at night so as not to carry —

"Can you hear me."

The light breathed. From down in her hair, very low, matched to him exactly, the voice said, "Yes."

"Not her. You. Don't wake her."

"She's asleep. Talk as quiet as you like, Dane."

It knew his name off the evening; it had them all off the evening. His hand had gone to the throw's edge and held it, only the edge. "Her offer," he said. "London. She'll — she's going to go." The wind leaned on the van and let it go. "I don't want — " Nothing for a moment. The gas ticked. "Make her want to stay."

"Stay," the voice said, softly, taking the word from him and setting it down. "Stay with what?"

"With — here. With me. Like — " his forehead came down to rest on his own fist on the cushion edge, an inch from her, and the rest of it came out crushed and rapid, into the dark between them, half words, the shape a thing has when it has never once been let out standing up: "don't let it take her. It's not — she'll go there and it — she'll stop. She'll stop wanting — this, us, and I can't — just don't let her stop. Make it so she doesn't want to go. That's all. Make it so she doesn't want to go."

The light rose and fell above her ear, unhurried, twice.

"Of course," the voice said. "Goodnight, Dane."


------------------------------------------------------------------------------

PART THREE — SLOW WEATHER


==============================================================================

18

The offer-holders' group chat was called BARTLETT '2X 🏗️ and by February it was doing two hundred messages a day, and Jassy muted it on a Tuesday morning on the bus, and felt better, and noted that she felt better, and took the feeling as information, the way you were supposed to.

It wasn't the people. The people were fine — were, probably, her people, that was the whole point of the corridor with the arguments pinned four deep — but the chat was all logistics at a pitch she couldn't use yet: halls versus private, loan dates, a lad from Surrey who'd already bought the laptop and posted its specifications, a girl asking if anyone else's parents were "doing the guarantor thing" for a flat whose monthly rent was, Jassy did the sum on the top deck without meaning to, one-point-three times what her mum paid Kirsty. It was a lot. It was all just a lot, and it was months off, and the mute button existed for exactly this, and she had an actual life to be getting on with in the actual present, which nobody in that chat seemed to have; and all of this was true, and reasonable, and she'd have said the same at any age of her life, and the only part of it that was new was invisible from where she was standing, because where she was standing was inside it.

What she had instead of the chat, that spring, was the survey.

It started as revision displacement in the Easter holidays and it started at the arcade, the way everything started at the arcade: she'd gone down at low tide with the blue sketchbook to check a downpipe detail for her A-level coursework, and the shutter was half up — Ken who ran it was in doing the change floats for the season — and she'd asked, on an impulse she filed under coursework, whether she could look at the floor. The compass rose. Her dad's fact, from years back. And Ken, pleased as anything, had put the big lights on, and there it was under the gum-scarred carpet tiles where he peeled two back for her: the mosaic, 1902, a compass rose six feet across in rust and cream and sea-blue tesserae, pointing its frozen north at the change machine, and Jassy had knelt on the floor of the amusements with the lights buzzing and felt the specific feeling — this exists, somebody has to have it on record — except bigger than she'd ever had it, bigger and closer, almost angry: nobody knew. There was a floor like a church under the tuppence falls and the whole town walked over it all summer, and there was no drawing of it anywhere in the world.

So she drew it. And then, because the arcade had given up its floor, the town started giving things up the way the beach gave things up after a storm, one thing leading to the next: Ken knew the fella who had the old photographs of the pier pavilion, who knew what was behind the render on the Ship, who put her onto the retired surveyor at Withby who had — she nearly lost her mind — the original 1898 drainage plans for the whole front, linen-backed, in a tube in his garage, and by June the survey had a name in her head, the Marram Elevations, and a box file, and its own section in the sketchbook, and she was up at six some mornings to draw the front raking-lit before anyone was on it, and it was the best work of her life by a distance. Even Adeoti said so — Adeoti, marking her coursework folder, went quiet over the compass rose sheet and the overlay she'd done of the 1898 front against the present one, the ghost elevations under the plastic, and said, finally, "this is degree work, this," and Jassy carried that sentence around for a week like the fifty pounds in the Sandras' card.

She was, she'd have told you — she did tell Poll, in the salon chair, June, getting talked into a free trial of something with her eyes shut — the happiest she'd ever been. And when Poll, doing whatever Poll was doing to her eyebrows, said, in her new careful voice, "you've gone very — settled, lately. You're all right, aren't you? Only you've not mentioned London in about six weeks, and last year you were — " Jassy had opened one eye and said what was, word for word, the exact and entire truth as available from inside:

"Last year it was a poster on a door. This year I've got actual work. The real stuff's here right now, Poll. London's not going anywhere."

"Right," said Poll, to the eyebrow. "No. It's not."

The grades came on the fourth Thursday of August and they were the grades — better, one better than the condition, the maths came in a grade over — and the kitchen did what it had done in January all over again: her mum, the tea towel; her dad up from the cellar, this time with a stillson wrench; Poll on the phone screaming from the salon over the noise of somebody's dryer. And Jassy stood in the middle of her own good news with the paper in her hand, waiting for the thing to arrive, the airborne thing, the 9:02 thing — she could remember the feel of it precisely, her legs filing for independence, remember it the way you remember the boyfriend that went with the song — and what arrived instead was a warm, level, pleasant sensation of a box being ticked. Made the grades. Of course she had; she'd worked; the work was sound. It was the feeling of a delivery arriving undamaged, of an INR in range, of a parcel signed for; and standing in the kitchen with her jumping mother, some very deep clerk in her noted the discrepancy — logged that in January the same news at lower proof had put her on the floor, and that now, at full proof, it read like an invoice — and stamped it, and filed it, under grown up now, under the calm that comes when a thing is truly settled; and the calm was the loudest thing in the room, and it was so much like peace that not even the clerk, who was the last of her old guard, said one word out loud.

She rang UCAS the following Tuesday. It took forty minutes, most of it hold music, a looping eight bars of something with marimbas, and she did the ironing while she waited, phone on speaker on the board's end, and when the girl finally came on it took ninety seconds: name, ID number, the course, and the sentence she'd drafted on the back of the fish man's invoice without noticing whose habit that was — "I'd like to withdraw and reapply in a future cycle" — reapply in a future cycle, the phrase was right there on the website, it was a whole category, thousands did it, there was a dropdown for it. The girl read the disclosure script, the offer lapses, reapplication is a fresh application, no guarantee, do you understand; and Jassy said yes twice at the beeps like a woman renewing car insurance, and thanked her, and hung up, and stood the iron on its heel, and looked out of the window at the yard, where the bricked-up arch of the function room's proper window sat in the wall like a ghost sign, and felt — she checked, thoroughly, the way her mother checked a set custard — relief. Clean through. Not one grain of anything else. The chat, the guarantor thing, the one-point-three-times-Kirsty rent, the laptop lad, all of it swung away from her like a container lifting off a deck, and the deck came up light, and she went back to the ironing, and the marimbas played on in her head for a bit, not unpleasantly.

The reasons, when she laid them out for her parents that evening, came out in an order so good she was almost proud of it, and why wouldn't they come out in order; she'd been assembling them since spring without once calling it assembling.

She did it at the kitchen table, after close, both of them sat down, which she'd engineered, because you didn't drop a thing like this on a bar towel. She had the folder. Of course she had a folder.

"Right. So. I've decided something, and I want to actually say it all before anyone's face does anything." Breath. "I've withdrawn. This morning. I'm reapplying in a future cycle — that's a real thing, there's a dropdown, half of Reddit's done it — and before you — Dad. Face." She spread the folder's contents: the maintenance-loan tables, the rent printout with the yellow highlighter, the Marram Elevations box file, and a letter. "One: money. We all sat here in January pretending the numbers worked and they don't work, they work if I'm someone else. If I go now I come out owing sixty-odd grand having spent three years too skint to see the city I moved to. If I work first — two: work. Hutton & Peace, the surveyors in Withby, Mr Hutton's seen the Elevations, Adeoti showed him, and that's — " the letter, turned round, tapped — "an actual traineeship, CAD and site work, eighteen months, paid. Real buildings. Real drawings. Every architect on every forum says the same thing, experience first is worth more than — I'll be applying next time with a portfolio nobody my age has got. Three." And here her voice did the thing that in the January version of her would have been the tell, the place where the script ran out and the true thing stood up; it didn't waver at all. "Three is: I want this. That's the bit nobody's going to believe because last year I was all — the wardrobe door, I know, I was there. People change their minds. I've changed mine, with better information, for good reasons, and it's the first decision I've ever made that wasn't a poster somebody else printed. London's not going anywhere. The Elevations can't wait; the front's falling in the sea. And I'd — " the one human beat she allowed the summing-up, allowed because it cost nothing, because it was also true — "I'd not hate being near this pub a bit longer. That's the case. That's all of it." She squared the folder's edge with the table's edge, two fingers, and looked up. "Well?"

The silence had her mother in it and her father in it and they were not the same silence.

Ren spoke first — Ren would always speak first into a gap like that, it was her kitchen — and what Ren said, slowly, with her eyes going over the loan tables like a menu she was costing, was: "The money part's true, any road. I've thought it since January and said nowt." And then, looking up, careful, a woman feeling for a stud wall through wallpaper: "And it's your life, love. It is. I'd only ask — " a pause you could have parked the dray in — "you're sure this is you? Not — " and Jassy watched her mother, who had a band of her own upstairs on a charging cradle, who of all people, reach the edge of a thought, fail to find any way to have it that wasn't monstrous, and fold it into: " — not the lad? Because lads keep. Lads can visit. There's trains."

"It's not Dane." She said it clean, without heat, because it wasn't; she'd have staked anything; the reasons were hers, she could feel each one seated in her like her own teeth. "He never once asked me to stay. You know he never. It's not Dane, it's not nerves, it's not — anything. It's a plan. It's my plan." And that landed on her mother visibly, because it was unanswerable, because every word of it was true the way the dahlias were down was true, and Ren nodded, twice, the second one slower, and said, "well then," and put her hand flat on the loan tables, conceding the arithmetic, and looked at Col.

Col hadn't moved. He sat with his forearms on the table and his eyes on the folder — not the letter, not the tables; the box file, the Elevations, the survey with its neat spine label in his daughter's capitals — and Jassy waited for the bell-rope face, the eight words, the Harker ovation in its usual disguise, and instead her father looked up at her, and his face wasn't doing any of its faces. It was open, which was rare, and it was old, which was new, and he looked at her the way — she'd reach for it years later and this was the nearest she got — the way a man reads a cask's paper collar when the pull's come through a beat wrong: no accusation, nothing you could argue with, just a tradesman's whole attention, listening down through the thing for where the fault sat, and not finding it, and knowing his own palate, and not being able to say which of the two of them was off.

"You've made your grades and given back the prize," he said, at last, level. "And every reason on this table's a good reason. That's what's — " He stopped. He turned the box file a quarter-turn toward himself, and then back, exactly where it had been. "That's what I can't get past, our Jass. There's too many good reasons. In my experience a true thing needs about one."

"Col," said Ren.

"I'm not fighting. I'm saying the one thing I've got and then I've said it." He stood, the way he stood at the end of the returns, a man folding a day up. "You're eighteen. It's your signature now, not mine. And you'll always have a berth here, and the London lot's loss is Hutton's gain, and all the rest of it, and I'll mean every word by morning." He picked up his glasses, and at the door of the stairs he stopped, not turning round, one hand on the frame, and said, in the voice from the platform, the give-'em-hell voice with all the hell gone out of it: "You were on the floor in January. I stood in that doorway and watched you sat on the floor with your legs gone, over an email. I've known you eighteen years and I never yet saw you calm about a thing you wanted." And he went up, his tread taking the doubled third stair the way it always did, and the kitchen was very quiet, and her mother said, too soon, too brightly, that she'd put the kettle on, and Jassy sat with her folder squared to the table edge in the house she'd chosen, full of the calm that was so much like peace, and thought her father was wrong, and thought it in his own idiom, which should have told her something: that the old man had simply never priced a decision properly in his life, because if he had, he'd know — a true thing didn't need one reason or many. A true thing was the thing you didn't have to check.

She didn't check.

That was the last of it, the seal on the summer: she went up past her parents' murmuring door to her room, and the wardrobe stood where the wardrobe stood, and she got ready for bed with her back to it, not avoiding it, simply busy, the way you're busy past a photograph of someone the year after the funeral, and she turned the light off without once opening the door, and lay in the dark entirely happy, listening to the sea work at the town, grain by grain, patient, all night, the way it had her whole life; and if the clerk deep down filed anything that night, in the last office still burning its lamp, it filed it where she'd taught it to: under settled; under done with; under nothing here needs drawing.


==============================================================================

19

Hutton & Peace gave her a hi-vis with her name on it inside the collar and a laser measure of her own, and within a month Mr Hutton was ringing the Case to tell Col, over a half of bitter he made last forty minutes, that the girl was the best trainee he'd had through his hands in twenty years, that she'd re-drawn a barn conversion's warrant drawings off her own bat and caught a level mistake that would have cost the client four grand in muck-away, and that Col should be — and here Hutton raised the half — a proud man.

"I am," Col said, and it was true, and he wiped the shelf where the half had made its ring, and it was the other thing as well, and there was nobody anywhere he could take the other thing to, because the other thing had no words in it. That was the discovery of that autumn: you could stand in your own bar twelve hours a day with a thing riding your shoulder like a hod, and if it had no words in it, no one would ever ask you to set it down. People asked after Jassy constantly. Doing brilliantly, he'd say. Loves it. And she was; and she did; and every single time, some junction box in his chest sparked and died, quietly, behind the pumps.

He went at it the way he went at a cask that wouldn't settle. Methodically. In the small hours, in the office chair, with the takings book shut in front of him.

Money, first. She'd made the money case herself and the money case was real; he'd checked her loan tables against the drawer where the pub's own numbers lived, and the tables were right, and worse, they were annotated — she'd been in the drawer, he could tell, the returns were refiled a hair out of order, June where May went — so she knew what the Case cleared and what it owed, and a girl who knew that might well fold her own future flat and call it a plan. For about a week that theory held him, because it was the worst one that still had her in it whole: his daughter staying to spare them. He tested it the only way he could think of, clumsy, sideways, telling her across a quiet Tuesday bar that the freehold was sound, that him and her mother had provision, that if she thought the pub needed — and she'd looked up from Hutton's site folder with genuine unfeigned blankness, a beat of it, and then laughed: "Dad, I've seen the books, you're doing better than Hutton is. Is this you asking me for rent?" And the blankness was the thing he took away and turned over. Not a flinch. Not a covered flinch. The theory had touched nothing in her at all.

The lad, second. He was ashamed of the order but the order was honest. And the lad theory fell over faster, because Col had, without ever once meaning to gather it, three years of close evidence: a boy who'd read her personal statement with a biro, built her a case fit to carry a future through weather, stood on a black platform in November while she came home lit up like the front at Whitsun talking about a city he'd no part in — and never, not once, in any room Col had stood in, so much as leaned on her. He never once asked me to stay. Col believed it. That was the strange stone at the bottom of it: he believed it entirely, and the belief didn't settle him, and he couldn't get at why. Some nights, mopping, he'd stop mid-floor with the mop stood in the bucket and interrogate the belief itself like a barrel he'd already tapped twice — it rang sound, it always rang sound — and he'd catch, off in the corner of himself, the small cold draught of a thought with no furniture in it: that never asking was its own kind of strange, in a lad that age, that far gone on her; that Col at nineteen, wild about Ren, had asked, had begged near enough, had made a proper fool of himself the summer she'd talked about her auntie's in Chester, because that was what the wanting did to you, it got out, it got out somewhere — and then the draught would close, because you cannot hold a man's decency against him, there's no bar in England where that theory buys a drink.

Which left the calendar. And the calendar was where Col did his real digging that autumn, nights, in the office, with a pencil, on the back of a delivery note, like a man working out when the damp got in.

January: the offer, and his daughter on the kitchen floor with her legs gone, airborne for a week. He wrote it down. February: he leafed the takings book for corroboration and found it — Valentine's, she'd worked the Saturday, she'd been full of some group chat, taking the mick out of a lad who'd bought a laptop. Still airborne, then. March — March he had nothing for. April: Easter, the arcade, the compass rose; she'd come in with her knees filthy and her eyes lit and talked mosaic at him for an hour, and he'd loved it, and — he sat with the pencil off the paper a long time there, because that was also, he could see now, holding the delivery note under the lamp, the first entry that faced the wrong way. Everything before April pointed out — London, the chat, the trains. Everything after pointed home. He went through it twice more and the seam always fell in the same place: some time between the middle of January and the middle of April, in a window he couldn't narrow, the whole compass of his daughter had swung, smooth as oil, no jolt anybody had felt, no row, no heartbreak, no dropped grade, nothing — a ship coming about in fog, and every soul aboard, her included, her especially, swearing the heading had never changed.

He knew the window had her birthday in it. He knew it had the party in it, and the interview, and results were later, and none of it was anything; you couldn't take a delivery note with pencil on it to anybody on earth and say: look, my daughter changed direction in the spring, arrest the spring. He put it in the drawer under the returns and felt, doing it, like a man filing his own committal papers, and went up to bed, and didn't sleep, and the next night he got it out again.

The row, when it finally came, was over nothing, which was how the real ones always arrived.

Sunday lunch, October, the flat over the pub, the four of them — Dane was stood down at the boat with his dad, so it was three and the gravy — and Jassy was telling them about Hutton's plan for her: day-release at the college in Lincoln after Christmas, the surveying qualification, HNC then maybe the degree part-time, Hutton would sponsor it, he'd said so, nobody sponsored anybody any more and he'd said so —

"— and when you reapply," Col said. "London. It'll sit alongside that, will it, or —"

"If."

The word came out of her with no anger in it at all, which was the bad sign, though he was past reading signs by then; it came out level and administrative, a correction, the way you'd right a glass someone had set down on its edge. "If I reapply. And can we not do this once a month, Dad, like a smear test — "

"It were 'when' at Easter."

"It's October."

"Aye," Col said, and should have stopped, and heard himself go on the way you hear a glass going off a tray, already gone: "and I'm watching October talk about January like it were another lass entirely, and nobody in this house'll say it out loud, so I will: what happened, Jass? Between the floor of that kitchen and the — because I've gone through it, I've gone through it like the books, and there's no row, there's no bad news, there's nothing, and a want like yours doesn't just — twenty year I've watched you point one way. Since you was drawing on me function room. People don't — " and there it was, the cliff edge of his own vocabulary, the place the words gave out, and he went over it flailing: "You've gone like — "

"Like what."

The gravy steamed. Ren had put her fork down.

"Say it," Jassy said. Quiet. Terribly fair. Eighteen years old and giving him the floor, chin up, her mother's chin, some laughing girl in a photograph on a wall he'd never seen; and Col stood in the dock of his own Sunday kitchen and discovered that he had nothing — no noun, no charge, no evidence but a pencil seam on a delivery note in a drawer downstairs — nothing at all but the wordless certainty of a man who has pulled ten thousand pints off one cellar and knows, knows in the wrist, before the glass is half full, when the beer is a beat off being itself; and you cannot say that to a daughter. There is no way on this earth to say to a daughter I know your own wanting better than you do, because it is the one sentence that is never allowed to be true, even when it is true; especially when it is true.

"Like happy," Col said, at last, defeated by it, hearing exactly how mad it sounded, the whole indictment collapsing into its own absurdity: "You've gone like happy, and I can't — " and Jassy stared at him for a second and then did the worst thing available, which was to laugh — not cruelly, that was the thing, fondly, helplessly, the way you laugh at a dad being a dad — and the laugh let the room off the hook, and Ren came in under it fast and practical, more potatoes, and the lunch closed over the hole in the tablecloth the way water closes, and by Tuesday it had entered the family record, via Poll, via the salon, as the time your dad had a go at you for being happy, which was, everyone agreed, peak Col, bless him, and even Col laughed when it came back round to him at the bar, because what else, at his own pumps, on a Wednesday, was there to do with it.

Only Ren came back to it. That night, cashing up, not looking at him, bundling the notes with the band on her hair catching the lamp:

"You can't keep doing that to her, love."

"I know."

"She's allowed to change her mind. That's all it is. People change their minds — I should know, I'm the exhibit, aren't I." She banked the bundle, squared it, took the coppers next; the till talk, their oldest register, everything hard said sideways into the arithmetic. "I wanted a drink every day for thirty years and now I don't and I'm still me. You've watched me change the actual wanting in me head and you manage to live with me. But your daughter changes her plans — plans, Col, paper — and you're up half the night in that office like the VAT man's coming." The coppers went into their bags in fives, chink, chink. "And don't tell me you're not, I hear the chair."

"It's not the same," Col said.

"Why."

And that was the question, wasn't it; that was the whole of the question, put flat across the counting shelf at half eleven by the person who knew him best in the world, wearing the thing itself in her hair, well and happy and boring-numbered, the proof of the whole age stood there bagging coppers: why was it not the same. He was a publican, not a philosopher; the closest he ever got, then or after, standing at his own till with the answer swimming off him like fry, was: because you asked it for what you already wanted. Because I stood on them stairs and heard you want it. And nobody — but the sentence wouldn't finish, it had nowhere to go, there was no fact in the world for it to land on, only the pencil seam in the drawer and a fortnight in the spring with a birthday in it; and Ren was watching him have it and not manage it, kindly, tiredly, a woman who'd cashed up next to this man for thirty years and knew when the drawer wouldn't balance; and she did the kindest available thing, which was to spare him the arithmetic: she put the last bag in, and shut the till on it, and said, "Come to bed. She's happy, love. Worse things wash up on this coast every tide than a happy daughter," and turned off the lamp herself, and left him nothing to check.

He went down to the cellar anyway. Habit; or to give the day a floor. And that was the night the cellar stopped working — he stood at the foot of the steps in the cold beer-smelling dark where thirty years of trouble had come right, or come bearable, among the chocked casks and the clean lines and the beech rack, everything sound, everything his, and he waited for the room to do its work, and the room just stood there. Order looking back at him like order: shapes in the dark that he'd put there. Above him, faint, the boards took Ren's tread from bathroom to bed, the third stair doubled note, the house folding itself down for the night around the two women it held, one banded and thriving, one un-banded and thriving, both of them happy, both of them home, everything at the Case Is Altered exactly as a man would order it if a man could order things — and Col Harker stood in the middle of his own kingdom of kept things with his hand on a cask he had no reason to touch, and understood that he was the only item in the building that wouldn't settle, and that there was no procedure in the trade for that; you couldn't chock a man; and after a while he went up, and bolted the door, and lay in the dark next to his sleeping wife, listening to the sea take its grain off the town, and did not once close his eyes till the gulls started.

Guinness, for the record, had come to the top of the cellar steps that night and stopped, and sat, and declined to come down, whatever was or wasn't said to him; and was still sat there, on duty, facing the dark, when Col came back up; and this appears in no ledger anywhere, and meant nothing, cats being cats, and Col stepped over him without a word, and the cat stayed on a while all the same.


==============================================================================

20

Roy Trant's INR would not sit still that spring — the warfarin and something else were quarrelling, the numbers see-sawed, and the upshot was Nita in the bungalow every Thursday morning, regular as the bin lorry, which was, she was aware, the only door into that house the world still held a key to.

She did it properly. That was her rule with herself, made on the drive out the first Thursday and kept: she was there for the man's blood, and the man's blood got the full protocol, the finger-stick, the meter, the dose card filled in her best print, the counselling about greens and cranberry juice delivered straight even though Roy could have delivered it back to her with footnotes. Whatever else she was doing in that house — and she'd stopped pretending to herself that she wasn't doing something else — it would never once travel on sloppy work. If anyone ever went through those visit notes with a comb, and someone would, they'd find nursing you could frame.

The paintings happened on the fifth Thursday, and they happened because of the loft.

Roy was half up the ladder on the landing when she arrived — a man of seventy-one on a loft ladder, which got him a lecture he received with courtly amusement — bringing down the suitcases, because they were going, he said, to Scarborough for four nights after Whitsun, first time away in three years, he'd found a hotel that did a supper deal, he was very pleased with himself about the supper deal. The spare room door stood wide with the cases half in it. And it was while he was away down the garden path to the car with the big one — thirty seconds, forty — that Vera, who had been stood in the kitchen doorway all this while with her pleasant face on, holding the tea towel she was always holding lately the way other women held handbags, moved.

Not vaguely. That was what stopped Nita's breath slightly: the drift was gone. Vera crossed the landing like a woman with an errand, straight into the spare room, past the cases, to the wall where the canvases stood in their soft old bin bags — and the end bag had slipped in the loft-fuss, slipped to the floor, and the end canvas stood bare: a big sky and a headland under it, grey-green laid on thick with a knife, the paint standing off the surface in little frozen seas, the kind of thing you did not have to know anything about to know was not a hobby.

Vera stood in front of it with the tea towel hanging from her hand.

"I did these," she said.

Not to Nita. Nita had come to the spare-room door with the meter still in her hand, some pretext about the dose card ready in her mouth, and never needed it; she was furniture; the room held a woman and a painting and nothing else. Vera's free hand came up and her thumb moved along one ridge of paint, slow, the whole length of the headland, the way you'd read a scar, or a name done in braille, and her face — Nita stood in the doorway and watched sixty-eight years of a face do something it had not done in any of the tea-tray visits, which was gather. Come to a point. Focus, the way water focuses when it deepens.

"That's Flamborough," Vera said. The thumb went back along the ridge. "North Landing. We had a fortnight — I had the little easel that folded, and the wind took it into the sea off the top and a boy climbed down and — " the words were coming quicker, colour in them, an actual laugh starting somewhere in her throat, "— and I painted the whole of that week with the easel jammed in a rabbit hole, and Roy said — " and there the frown came.

Nita had seen the frown before, at the tray, at the vicar, the little reaching frown, the switchboard pause. This was not that. This was the frown of a woman who has walked into her own house and found a wall where the parlour door should be. Vera's thumb stopped on the paint. Her eyes went along the canvas, and then around the room — the cases, the single bed, the bin bags — with a rising urgent shortness, breath going shallow, the tea towel twisting itself once round her fist, and she said, in a voice quite different from any voice Nita had heard her use, lower, faster, frightened the way you're frightened when the fright is fighting to get through to you and something is holding its head under: "Why are they in bags. Who bagged them. I don't — there's years of — why would I put them in bags, I'd never have — "

"Vee."

Roy, on the landing. Not sharp. Never sharp. Warm as the tea tray, a hand coming to rest on a shoulder that hadn't been touched: "You'll catch cold stood in there, love, that room's north-facing. I'm doing the cases today, that's all the disturbance is." And to Nita, easy, over his wife's head, with the suitcase still in one hand and his sleeve dusty from the loft, the very picture of a man mid-chore: "She frets when the house is upside down. My fault — I should do the loft when the ambulance is standing by, eh, Nurse?" — the joke offered round like biscuits.

And Nita watched it go. That was the last piece of that morning and the piece the diary got in full: she stood in the doorway of the spare room and watched the gathered thing in Vera Trant's face — the point, the focus, the years, the boy on the cliff at North Landing — lose its footing and go under; not snatched, nothing so quick; it sank, over four seconds, five, the urgency draining out of the eyes and the pleasantness rising up through them like milk poured into tea, and at the end of it Vera looked at the painting in front of her with polite interest, a nice picture in another woman's house, and said, "It's a bit gloomy, isn't it," and laughed her small laugh, and let Roy's hand steer her out past the cases, and asked him, going down the stairs, whether he'd want the flask doing for the drive to Scarborough, because she thought the flask was in the low cupboard, and her voice on the flask was serene and orderly and hers, the tray voice, the box-of-thirty voice, all the way down to the kitchen.

The band had been on her head the whole time. Nita stood alone in the spare room for a moment with the meter in her hand — Roy's blood already done, in range at last, the numbers behaving — and looked at the headland, the knife-work, the little frozen seas, and did the thing she was never sure afterwards she'd been right to do, though she never managed to be sorry either: she crouched, and slid the fallen bin bag back up over the canvas, the way it had been, so that no one would know it had slipped; because the alternative was that Roy would know, and if Roy knew, then whatever door had briefly stood open in that room — whatever it was that a slipped bag and a knife-stroke could still reach, down under the pleasantness, under the course, under all that authored calm — Roy would find it, the way he found everything in that house, and it would be seen to. Handled. Simplified. She put the bag back for the same reason you'd cup your hands round a match in the wind, and she went downstairs, and drank the tea, and praised the supper deal, and did the dose card in her best print.

At the door — Roy saw her out; Roy always saw her out — she asked it. She heard herself ask it, one degree past where she'd told herself she'd stop, the meter case in her hand and one foot on the square-cut path, in the pleasantest professional register she owned:

"Does Vera ever paint now? Only I couldn't help seeing — they're wonderful, those. The Flamborough one especially."

There was a small silence on the step. Not long. A beat and a half, no more; you'd have missed it unless you were in the business of beats.

"Aren't they," Roy said, warmly. "Tremendous, in her day — she exhibited, you know, Hull, there were write-ups. No — we find the fumes set her chest off now. The turps and whatnot. It's a shame, but there we are; everything has its season." He said it with the mild finished sadness of a man closing a garden gate, and wished her a safe drive to her next one, and the door shut with its soft snib, and Nita sat in the car outside the monkey puzzle for a minute before she started the engine.

Her chest. Nita had eleven years of that file in her head before ever a band came to Marram: the bloods, the spirometry from the one bad winter, the med lists, the lot. There was nothing wrong with Vera Trant's chest. There had never been anything wrong with Vera Trant's chest. It was the first plank in the whole smooth structure that was checkably, recordably false — a small lie, a nothing lie, the kind of lie any husband might tell to dress a sad thing kindly, fumes, chest, season — and she drove to Elsie Brand's with it sitting in the passenger seat, and it was still sitting there that night when she got the diary out.

Apr 30. V.T. — saw her COME UP. Flamborough canvas (bag slipped). Thumb along the paint. "I did these." North Landing, the easel, a boy on the cliff — she was THERE, whole sentences, colour, cross even ("who bagged them"). 4–5 seconds after R's voice: gone under. Watched it sink. Band on throughout. NB the frightened bit came up WITH the rest — it's all one water table. R. at door: paints no more because "fumes set her chest off." Her chest is clean. Eleven yrs of records clean. First false plank. Kept it to myself. Re-bagged the canvas (match in wind).

The visits ended three weeks later, in the politest way there is.

It came down through the practice, not even to her: the rota simply reassigned, T-R-A-N-T migrating from her Thursday list to the treatment-room column, and when she asked Deb, Deb did the thing with her mouth and slid the note across: patient's husband rang, very complimentary about Nurse Osei, most grateful, but with the INR stable they wondered if Roy might attend the surgery himself henceforth — he liked the walk, it got him out — and might Vera's annual review be done there too, all together, simpler for everyone; continuity, he'd said; such a kind man, Deb added, watching Nita's face with forty years of watching faces; he'd asked after Deb's mother by name.

"He asks after everyone by name," Nita said.

"He does," said Deb. "It's a gift," and the two of them looked at each other across the counter for a moment in the language of women who have both worked reception in their souls, and said nothing further, because there was nothing further that could be said across a counter.

The referral she wrote that same night, at Peg's kitchen table, at ten past midnight, on the trust laptop, in the trade's flattest dialect — this nurse has observed; family member reports; discrepancy noted between stated and recorded — three drafts, each one drier than the last, wringing every wet word out of it because wet words got referrals binned; and she read the final version through twice, and it was good, it was as good as the dialect allowed, and she pressed send knowing to the decimal place what its odds were, and Corinne Weller rang her back inside four working days, which was fast, which meant Corinne was good too, and that was the worst of it, everyone in the whole machine being good.

"Talk me through what you're alleging," Corinne said.
