"Because I've read it three times, Nita, and I want to be able to take it somewhere, and every time I reach for it I come back with — he's her registered carer, there's capacity on file from February and I can't go behind that, the device is clinical-grade and prescribed and consented, the GP saw her within the year, she's clean, fed, warm, socially presented, no disclosure, no injury, engaging — " papers turning — "a locked spare room in a couple's own house is a locked spare room, people lock rooms; a fib about turps is a fib about turps. You know what I've got on my desk this morning. I've got a lad of nine eating out of bins. Give me the sentence, Nita. Give me the sentence that starts 'I am concerned that Mr Trant is—' and finishes with something I've a category for."

And Nita, at the kitchen table, in her dressing gown, with twenty-two years of the categories in her and the diary upstairs under the church magazine, looked out at the dark garden and did her level best, and heard how it sounded even as it left her, heard it hit the desk in Louth like a moth:

"I'm concerned that he's — settling her. Bit by bit. Turning her down, the — there's less of her every quarter, Corinne, and she consents to every single step of it, and the consent is the — " and stopped, because down the line, kindly, exhaustedly, into a phone wedged on a shoulder above a desk with a nine-year-old eating from bins on it, Corinne Weller had begun, very quietly, not unkindly, to sigh.

Screened out. The letter used the phrase threshold not met, and thanked her for her vigilance, which was the system's way of patting the moth.

Peg was still up when she came off the phone the night the letter came — Peg was always still up, sleep being, in Peg's stated view, a rehearsal she didn't need — and Peg had the whole thing by then, or the outline of it, gathered sideways over months in the Peg manner, and she heard the last of the phone call from her chair with the snooker murmuring, and when Nita came through and stood in the doorway with the look on her, Peg spoke to the television, generally, the way she delivered all verdicts:

"When I was a girl on Adelaide Street there was a house like that. Lovely man. Grew leeks. His wife got quieter and quieter for fifteen year, like a wireless with the knob going, and everybody said wasn't she serene, and my mother crossed the road rather than pass the window. I asked her why once. She said: 'That house has weather in it. You can't see weather through a shut door, but you can see the washing.'" Peg watched the young man in the waistcoat pot a long red. "That woman's washing was on the line by seven every morning of her life. Beautiful washing. Never once a Monday missed." The red went down; Peg nodded at it, satisfied, and delivered the rest to the table baize, flat as a coroner: "Nobody ever knocked. That's all your machines have gone and changed, love — the weather's got quieter. The doors were always shut."


==============================================================================

21

When she'd told Dane — August, the day of the marimbas, down at the staithe with the tide making and the pots stacked for the morning — he hadn't said anything at all at first, which was standard issue; and then he'd asked, not looking at her, coiling something that didn't need coiling: "You're sure? It's not — nobody's — you're sure it's what you want?" — which she'd loved him for, the scruple of it, the checking; and when she'd laid the whole case out, Hutton's letter and the money and the future cycle, he'd stood with his back to the evening and held her absolutely still for a long time, longer than the news seemed to need, his chin down on the top of her head and his arms round her like a man holding a hatch shut in weather, and she'd felt his heart going and thought: he never once asked, and look at him — he cares more than any of them; and across the water the gulls had worked the tideline over, and neither of them had said anything else for a good while, and that was the whole of the telling, and it went in the file under best of him.

The flat came up in November because old Mr Beal finally moved to his son's in Lincoln, and it was the flat over the Blue Dolphin, which everyone had opinions about.

"You'll smell of scraps," Poll said, with relish. "Both of you. Forever. You'll walk in the salon and I'll know."

"It's reconditioned, the extractor. Dane's been up on the roof, he says the new fan's — "

"You'll be batter-adjacent by Easter," Poll said. "Mrs Chip." And then hugged her hard enough to hurt, in the salon doorway, for no reason either of them named.

The move itself was a Saturday of legend, the sofa Ted Pyke had insisted on donating — "good sofa, our lass had it new in '09" — jamming on the turn of the stairs at an angle that repealed geometry, Ted below shouting bearings like a docking pilot, Col above with his end on his knee saying nothing at increasing volume, the two fathers wedged in a stairwell by upholstery while their children wept against the yard wall; and Ren arriving at the crisis with fish and chips for five, so that the first meal in the flat was eaten on the trapped sofa itself, halfway up, papers on knees, Ted passing the vinegar down the banister; and if you had asked Jassy Harker that day to point to the life she'd have chosen out of all lives on offer, she'd have pointed at the stairwell and been telling you the truth, and that was the thing about that whole year, the thing that made it what it was: it never once lied to her. It was a good year built of true days. Whatever had been done to her had been done further up the river than any day could see.

The work was real. That surprised nobody but the version of her she didn't know was missing. Hutton's was two rooms over a solicitors' in Withby, a firm of four — Mr Hutton, who wore his glasses on a painter's-merchant string and had surveyed half the coast; Barbara, who ran him; a lad called Priestley who did drains and worshipped, in order, Leeds United and level datums; and her — and the work was churches with cracked chancels and barn warrants and damp schedules and loft conversions for people from York with more money than headroom, and Jassy took to it the way she took to anything with edges. She passed her HNC modules at the top of the day-release lists. She learned the total station, the bat survey rules, the way a 1780 roof spreads and lies about it. Hutton started sending her out alone within the year — "you see true, lass, and you write it down plain; that's the job; the rest is software" — and clients asked for her, the young one who draws it proper, and every Friday the money went in, real money earned with her own eye, and some of it into the account she still called, in her head, without examining it, the deposit.

For what, the deposit, was a question the year never made her answer.

And home was the flat, and the flat was them, and them turned out to be a thing she was good at too — or they were good at, whoever you divided the credit between: his tide clock and her drawing board in the one bay window, negotiated to the inch; the extractor's hum below like a tame sea; Friday teas claimed off her mother at the Case with the staff discount that was a fiction of Ren's bookkeeping; Dane's van — he'd bought the van in March, off Kye's contact, sign-written by the same fella who did the salon, D. PYKE, PROPERTY & MARINE MAINTENANCE, and underneath, at Jassy's insistence, in smaller letters that made Ted laugh till he had to sit on his own donated sofa: no job too odd. The round took. Of course it took: a coast full of retiring tradesmen and a lad who fixed things properly and cleared up after himself; by summer he was booked two weeks out and had turned down work in Louth as too far from the water, and some nights he did his invoices at the kitchen table with a biro, tongue between his teeth, and she did her CAD homework opposite, and the fryers hummed, and if either of them had been asked to design happiness from scratch neither would have had the nerve to draw this, on the grounds that the examiners would call it complacent.

She still had the Elevations. That was the other thing; nothing was taken, nothing was ever taken. The box file moved to the flat and got its own shelf, and the blue sketchbook lived on the board's shelf same as always, and when Ken from the arcade stopped her on the front in April — he'd kept the two carpet tiles up all winter, bless him, cones over the compass rose like a crime scene — and asked, hopeful, was she still on with her drawings, because the council lad had been round again about the roof and Ken would love to have something proper to wave at them, Jassy heard herself say, warm as anything, entirely meaning it: "Definitely. It'll keep, Ken — that floor's sat there since 1902, it's not going anywhere. I'll get back on it when the evenings pull out."

Which was true. Everything she said that year was true. The evenings pulled out, and pulled in again, and the box file sat on its shelf with its neat spine label, THE MARRAM ELEVATIONS, next to the tide clock, perfectly safe, the way a thing is safe when the tide that used to shove at it has gone out; and Ken kept the cones up, and the council lad came round again about the roof, and the sea took its grain a night off the boulder clay north of the caravans, patient, unhurried, on its own long survey, and nobody anywhere was doing anything wrong.

The wardrobe happened during the move, or rather was discovered then, the way you discover in daylight a thing the dark has been holding for you.

She was clearing her room at the Case — the last boxes, the childhood strata, rosettes and revision cards and the shoebox of Poll-era photographs — and she opened the wardrobe to empty it, both doors wide, and stood in front of it with a bin bag in one hand, and the inside of the left-hand door was bare.

Cork-tack holes, a constellation of them, top corners and bottom, where things had been pinned and re-pinned over years; two grey ghosts where sun had got at the wood around something rectangular; a curl of old Blu-Tack like a fossil worm. Nothing else. And Jassy stood looking at the bare panel with the specific sensation of a foot finding a stair that isn't there — no grief in it, nothing so shaped as grief, just the small systems-error lurch of when did I do that — because she had no memory of taking it down. Any of it. She reached for the memory the way you'd pat a pocket, confident, and the pocket was flat: no afternoon, no mood, no decision, no bin. After results, probably. The big sort-out, when the A-level files went to the loft; she'd had a black-bag blitz that week, everyone had seen her do it, her mum had said something about ruthlessness. It would have been then. It must have been then, it had the shape of then, and the explanation arrived so smoothly, slotted in so well, that the lurch closed over almost before she'd felt it — almost; there was a last quarter-second, stood in front of the bare door with the bin bag, when the explanation sat in her hand like a part that fits the hole without being the part, too smooth, no wear on it, and something in her, some clerk on the last of the lamp oil, looked at the join and made its small mark; and then Poll shouted up the stairs that the van was ticking over and Ted was threatening the sofa again, and the moment did what moments do.

She found the postcard at the very bottom, under the shoe layer, face down, dust-furred: the white staircase, the poured one, London going up through a house like something spilled upward. She turned it over. Her own biro on the back, fifteen years old and embarrassing: THIS. Just that, and three underlines.

She looked at it a while, crouched at the wardrobe's foot, in the emptying room of her childhood. It was — she assembled the response with care and it came out genuinely kind, the way you'd speak of somebody else's dead enthusiasm, a school friend's pony phase: it was a lovely staircase. It was. A bit impractical; a heat-loss nightmare, Priestley would say; you'd never get it through building regs with that open riser run, and where — she almost smiled — where did the rain go, all that rooflight glass. A nice staircase. She put it, not in the bin bag — she was careful with paper, always had been — but flat in the box marked SIXTH FORM ETC, between the folder of old crits and the offer-holders' welcome booklet, the box whose lid she taped shut and carried across the landing herself and pushed deep along the eaves shelf of her parents' loft, where the Christmas lights lived, and the cot; and she thumped back down the ladder into the noise of her own moving day, and out into it, and was happy — was, by every reading on every instrument she had access to, happy; and the box sat in the dark with the tape going slowly amber, and across town in the new flat the tide clock said half-ebb, which was a reading off Bridlington, Dane always said, not here, you had to know your offset; nothing on any wall told you the truth of this coast straight; you had to know what to add.


==============================================================================

22

The bowls were Wally's idea, in the sense that Wally announced them as settled fact.

"Thursday afternoons you shut at three now" — this was true; winter hours, the dead stretch; another line item off the falling tide — "so you'll come up the club Thursdays. I've cleared it with Arthur. You want flat shoes." He turned a page of the Post. "Not them. Flat."

The Marram & District Crown Green Bowling Club was a strip of impossible velvet behind the cricket field, a wooden pavilion the colour of strong tea, and eleven men and fourteen women who took no prisoners whatsoever, and inside its little clubhouse Wally Nunn — two and a half years dry, chairman of the greens committee, holder of the Autumn Pairs shield with Mavis Petch's sister — was somebody Col had never met before: brisk, exact, deferred to. On his own stool at the Case Wally was a fixture; up here he was a skipper. He put a bowl in Col's hand the first Thursday, stood behind him like a golf pro, and said, "Now. Forget everything you think, because you think like a darts man, straight at it. This game's all bias. The bowl's built lopsided — " he turned it, showed the weighted cheek — "so you don't aim at the jack. You aim off. You send it out wide, into the wrong part of the green, trusting the lean in the thing to bring it home. Watch." And he stooped and swung and the bowl went away absurdly wide, a shot to nothing, halfway to the ditch, and then leaned, and drew, and came round in a long dying curve like a gull coming in off the wind, and stopped six inches off the jack, kissing the head.

"Now you," said Wally.

Col played straight at it for three Thursdays because his hands refused the premise, and everything he sent died wide or ran through the head like a dray horse, and the fourteen women watched with pity, and Wally stood at his shoulder saying, mild as milk, "aim off, you great article; the green does half the work if you'll let it; you can't muscle a draw" — until the fourth Thursday, when something in Col's wrist finally surrendered its opinion, and he let one go out wide and wrong with his whole body objecting, and watched it lean, and turn, and gather the line home, and finish flush against the jack with a click like a till drawer, and Arthur in the pavilion doorway said "he's got one," and Col straightened up with his knees going and a feeling in his chest that was entirely disproportionate to lawn sports and said nothing whatsoever, because he was learning the room's manners.

"That's the game," Wally said, retrieving. "It's not about strength. It's about knowing what a thing'll do when you're not gripping it." And he said it about the bowl, to the bowl, and Col took it about the bowl, and on the drive home it kept unfolding in his lap all the same, the way Wally's remarks did, being none of them, on inspection, about bowls.

The hoist had been on the cellar's snag list for two winters — the old barrel hoist over the back hatch, a Victorian arrangement of beam and blocks that predated Col, that predated Elgood's probably, with a crack opening along the beam's grain that Col had been not-looking at professionally since before the coffee machine — and it was Dane who forced the job, in April, by the simple method of standing under the hatch on a delivery Tuesday, looking up at the beam for a long moment in that way he had, and saying, "I'm not stood under that again, and nor are you."

They did it over two Sundays. Green oak from the yard at Saltfleet, because Dane knew the fella; the old beam down in a controlled ruin that filled the yard with a hundred and forty years of dust and one desiccated sparrow's nest with three china marbles in it, which went on the bar shelf next to the barometer, nobody could say why marbles, the bar voted them lucky; the new beam up on the two of them and a chain block, Dane calling it in boat language, handsomely now, well, hold — and Col holding, and taking the weight, and finding, as the oak settled onto its refreshed padstones true to the bubble, that he was as near content as he'd been in eighteen months, up a ladder, under load, in the dust, with the lad.

That was the afternoon he made the offer. He'd not planned it; planning would have killed it; it came out of the work, at the tea break, the pair of them sat on upturned crates in the yard with the new beam above them yellow as butter, and Col heard himself say, roundabout, in the dialect, that the brewery lad wanted watching on the ropes these days; that cellar work was a young man's game whatever Col's knees pretended; that a place like this — he did not look at the pub while saying it, he looked at the beam — a place like this wanted, long run, somebody about it who fixed things before they fell, and there'd be a living in that, for the right — someday, was all Col was saying; the right lad, someday; it wanted thinking on, was all.

It was as close to here is a future, son, marry into it as a Harker could come without medical assistance, and both of them knew the exchange rate, and Col risked one look sideways at the end of it, and what he saw he had no file for.

The lad had gone still. Not modest-still, not chuffed-and-hiding-it still, which is a thing with a temperature to it, a thing Col had watched this boy do over praise since the ice machine. Cold still. He was looking at the mug in his hands, and his face had shut like the tide gate, and the silence went on one beat past awkward into somewhere Col didn't know the water, and then Dane said, "Aye. Well." And put the tea down half-drunk, and said he'd want to be getting on, Ted needed him on the pots for six — which might have been true and rang like a coin on wood — and gathered his tools with none of the usual ceremony, the flex coiled anyhow, over-over, and was in the van and gone up Keel Street before the kettle Col had refilled had come back to the boil.

Col stood in the yard with two mugs and the new beam and the old dust and ran it back the way he ran back a till discrepancy, item by item. Somewhere in it he'd stepped wrong. He couldn't find where. He'd offered the lad — roundabout, deniable, but offered — the thing lads like that were supposed to want, root and berth and a beam that would outlive everybody; and the lad had taken it like a summons. Pride, Col decided, eventually, because it was the only stool that stood: too proud to come up through the wife's family, didn't want it looking like — aye. That. It fitted well enough if you didn't lean on it, and Col didn't lean on it, having by then a drawerful of things he was careful not to lean on, and he washed both mugs and said nowt to Ren, and the beam settled into its work above the hatch, taking the weight it was asked to, sound for the next hundred and forty years, whoever was under it.

The letter came in May, in a good envelope, addressed to The Owner, The Case Is Altered public house — which told you at the first fence they didn't know the first thing about the place; everyone who knew it wrote to Col — and inside it a single page from something called Keelhaven Coastal Ltd, with a paragraph of weather about evolving leisure trends on the East Coast and their active acquisition programme, and a sentence expressing sincere interest in a discreet conversation regarding the freehold, and a number that was not named but was characterised, with a straight face, as reflective of the site's full residential potential.

"Residential potential," Col said, at the till, and put it in the bin under the counter, where it lay on the crisps boxes' flattened cardboard like a gull on a tip.

Ren took it out that evening. He watched her do it — she did it in plain sight, which was the message — smooth it flat on the counting shelf, read it through once over the top of both pairs of glasses, and file it in the concertina folder where the insurance lived, under a tab she wrote out fresh with the good biro while he watched: K'HAVEN. NO. (BUT.)

"'No but,'" Col said.

"No," said Ren, bagging the coppers, "but." And that was the whole debate, both sides of it, filed by half past eleven; a marriage of thirty years being among other things a very efficient committee. She didn't say the roof quote out loud, or the insurance renewal, or the wet trade's winter number, and neither did he, because you didn't have to say things to have them sat in on a meeting; and upstairs the folder held the letter the way the loft held the taped box, the way the drawer under the returns held a delivery note with pencil on it: the whole building quietly filling up, that year, with papers nobody was acting on and nobody could throw away.

The last Thursday of the bowls season, Wally beat him nineteen–six, which was an improvement, and stood him a lime and soda in the pavilion afterwards among the shields, and squared his cap, and said, apropos of nothing, in the voice he used from the third stool for the minutes of the meeting:

"Your Jassy's young feller. He's stopped coming in the Case of a Friday."

"He's busy. Round's gone mad. He's turning work away."

"Aye," Wally said. He looked out at the green, where Arthur was doing the ropes. "He were in the Anchor at Withby last Friday, any road. Baz Leggett seen him. Sat on his own with a pint, watching the racing." He said it with no freight at all, which was how Wally said the freighted things, and adjusted his cap, and added, mild, terrible, in exactly the tone he'd used about the bowl: "Man drinks where nobody knows him for one of two reasons. Celebrating nowt, or paying for summat." And he stood, and thanked Arthur about the ropes, and left Col sat in a wooden pavilion in the smell of cut grass and liniment with the whole green going gold in front of him and the season over and something out wide on the wrong part of it, leaning, beginning, in its own long time, to draw.


==============================================================================

23

Ripon was two and a half hours each way on a Sunday, and Nita told Peg she was going to see about some curtains, and Peg said, "mind how you go, and fetch us some of that proper Wensleydale if you're seeing about curtains anywhere near a Booths," which was Peg for I know exactly where you're going and I'll not say it aloud either.

Grace Holt lived in a terraced almshouse-looking row near the cathedral, and was Vera minus the settling: the same long bones, the same chin, a woman of seventy who came to the door in an apron with her sleeves pushed up and a look that took Nita's measure on the step in one pass, the Peg look, the generation's look. She had baked. Of course she had baked; the table was laid for a state visit, and the first twenty minutes were the weather, the parking, the state of the A1, the cathedral's scaffolding — the old protocol, the circling that let each of them establish the other could be sat with — and then Grace poured the second cup, and set the pot down, and said, without any change of register at all:

"You're not here as a nurse, are you."

"No. I can't be. If I were here as a nurse I couldn't say any of what I'm — I want to be straight with you about that, Mrs Holt. I'm a woman on her day off who knows your sister a bit. That's all this is, and even that much, if it ever — it could cost me."

"Then we'll be two women having tea." Grace turned her cup a quarter-turn, a gesture so exactly Vera's that it hurt to watch, the same hands on the same china a hundred miles apart. "I went over at Whitsun. Stopped two nights at the Bell in Withby because he doesn't do visitors overnight now — the spare room's full of her paintings, he says, there's nowhere to put a bed, he says." The cup went another quarter-turn. "I had one afternoon with her. He was in the room for the whole of it, or the garden, or the hall. Making tea. Doing the hedge. Never further than a door. And our Vee sat there in her nice cardigan and asked me — " Grace's voice did not break; it hardened, which was worse — "she asked me how I was settling in. In Ripon. I've been in this house fifty-odd year, Mrs Osei. She helped me hang them curtains up there the spring Callaghan got in. 'How are you settling in.'"

"And she called me love," Grace said. "Twice. She's called me Gracie since she could talk. Everyone else in the world gets 'love.' I got Gracie." She looked out of the window at the little walled yard, the washing line, the one rosebush. "I rang your surgery twice and got a girl reading a screen. I wrote to that doctor and got a letter back with a sentence in it I've said in my sleep since: 'Mrs Trant has been assessed as having capacity.' Capacity." She said the word the way you'd hold up a small dead thing by the tail. "My sister has the capacity to agree to whatever he puts in front of her. That's what that word means now. I looked it up. It means she can follow what's said to her and say yes. Well. She could always say yes. It was the other word she was good at, our Vee. You should have seen her at nineteen telling my father she was marrying a schoolteacher whatever he thought of it." Grace stopped, and did the arithmetic of that sentence in front of them both — the schoolteacher, the whatever-he-thought — and folded it away, fifty years too heavy to lift on a Sunday.

Nita told her, then, what she could. She was careful — she'd rehearsed the care on the A1 — no records, no diagnoses, nothing off a screen; only what any visitor's eyes could have seen: the paintings bagged, the room locked, the cards gone to a box of thirty, the flower show, the frown that reached and reached, the false plank about the chest — she did say the chest; she heard herself say it and knew, saying it, that she'd stepped over something she couldn't step back across; there was no version of a woman on her day off who knew about that chest. Grace listened the way Peg watched snooker, missing nothing, her hands still in her lap; and at the end she said, "What do I do," and Nita gave her the honest inventory, which was short: visit, ring, write, keep saying Gracie, keep the old stories coming, ask the surgery — as family, in writing, her own words — for a carer's assessment, for a review, ask and keep asking; you have standing I haven't; you're allowed to be a worried sister forever; there's no threshold on that.

"And will any of it work?" Grace said.

"It'll keep the door — " Nita stopped, because the honest end of the sentence was from rusting shut, and rerouted: "It'll mean she hears her own name sometimes. That's not nothing."

Grace saw the reroute. Grace, Nita understood in that little parlour, saw roughly everything, and had been seeing it, powerless, from a hundred miles, for three years, which was its own kind of house arrest. She walked Nita out through the yard, past the rosebush, and at the gate she took Nita's hand in both of hers, the strong floury grip of the generation, and said, "You're the first person in this whole business who's talked to me like it's real," and Nita drove home down the A1 with the Wensleydale on the passenger seat and that sentence riding in the back, and it should have felt like absolution, and what it felt like, mile on mile, was a fuse.

The letter Grace wrote to the practice in January was three pages, longhand, magnificent, and it detonated everything.

Nita never saw it; she reconstructed it afterwards from the shrapnel, from what was quoted back to her across various tables. Grace had done exactly what a strong frightened loving woman with a fountain pen would do: she had marshalled every fact she had, and cited her sources. The bagged paintings. The locked room. The chest that had never been bad — and your own district nurse has seen it all, she knows, ask Nurse Osei, Nurse Osei agrees with me — the name three times by all accounts, underlined once; a sister swinging the heaviest thing she had, which was a witness; not knowing, because how could she know, that the witness was the one thing in her arsenal that could be turned around and fired back.

Roy Trant's complaint arrived at the trust eleven days later. Typed. Courteous throughout — Nita would hear passages of it read aloud more than once in the year that followed, and the courtesy never thinned anywhere in it, it was courtesy load-bearing from lintel to sill — and it expressed, more in sorrow, his and his wife's growing unease over a considerable period regarding Nurse Osei's conduct: her persistent questioning on matters unrelated to the clinical task; her interest in the family's private arrangements; the discovery — via poor Mrs Holt's letter, which he attached in full, which was the masterstroke, his own accuser filed as his exhibit — that private observations made inside their home had been shared with a third party in another county. He used the word surveillance once, precisely placed, like a man setting a single screw. He requested no punishment; he was sure there was an innocent construction; he asked only that the appropriate people satisfy themselves as to the appropriate standards, and signed himself hers faithfully, R. Trant, and beneath it, in the space where a title would go, nothing at all, because a man like Roy never had to write the titles; the room supplied them.

The meeting was in March, at trust offices in Lincoln, in a room called Meeting Room 4 that had a whiteboard nobody had cleaned properly and a window onto a car park, and there were two of them, an investigating officer called Mrs Pashley and a younger one from HR who took notes on a laptop and never once spoke, and it was, everyone said so at the start, not a disciplinary, it was a fact-find, she was welcome to have someone with her, she had brought nobody, which they noted.

They were forty minutes in — the visits, the questions, the reassignment, Grace, the day off, the Wensleydale, Mrs Pashley working through it all in a tone of level administrative regret — when it came.

"Mrs Holt's letter refers to things you'd 'seen' and 'knew.' Some of these — the detail about Mrs Trant's chest, for instance — wouldn't be visible on a visit. They read like clinical knowledge." Mrs Pashley aligned her papers. "And they read like accumulated knowledge. Dates. Sequences. 'Second year running.' Nurse Osei — and think before you answer this, because you're entitled to think — where does that detail live? Is there a record?"

And Nita thought. She was entitled to. She sat in Meeting Room 4 and looked out at the car park where a man was failing to reverse a Berlingo into a space that would have taken a bus, and she thought about the answer no, it's all just memory, which was safe, which was probably even survivable, and which was a lie; and she thought about eleven years of doorways, and the thing the doorways had taught her that she'd have put on her own headstone if headstones took paragraphs — that the whole difference between the houses that got saved and the houses that didn't was usually one person who wrote it down — and she opened her bag, and took out the 2009 diary with the biro through the elastic, and set it on the table between them, squared to the edge, and said:

"There's a record. I kept it at home because there's no field for it in the system you gave me. Everything in it is dated and true, and I'd write it again tonight. You'll want to photocopy it. I'd like it back."

Mrs Pashley looked at the diary for a while without touching it, the way you'd look at something handed in off the beach that might yet be ordnance, and then she said, quietly, with the first unadministrative feeling she'd shown in an hour and a quarter, which Nita only later understood had been a kind of pity: "Oh, Nita." Not Nurse Osei. "You didn't even — " and stopped herself, and squared her own papers, and reached for the phone on the wall to ask for the photocopier code, and that was, in every way that came to matter, the end of the fact-find and the beginning of everything else.

The suspension letter used the phrase neutral act throughout — suspension is a neutral act, it kept saying, like a man repeating a spell — and gave her a case number, her second in two years, she thought, and this one too came with a strange cold lightness stapled under it, though this time nobody anywhere would have called the ledger public-facing: pending investigation into (a) breach of confidentiality, (b) failure to maintain professional boundaries, and (c) record-keeping outside approved systems, she was not to attend the surgery, contact patients on her former caseload, or — clause (d), the one that finally made it through her calm at the kitchen table, the one that made her put the letter down and press the heel of her hand flat against her sternum — contact their family members.

Grace rang that same week, having received her own letter, some bland regretted-procedural thing, and having extracted Nita's number from Deb by main force of Yorkshire, and Nita stood in the hall with the phone and said, "I can't — Mrs Holt, I'm so sorry, I'm not allowed to speak to you," and Grace said, at speed, before the phone could go down, knowing exactly what she was doing, three words into the gap like a hand into a closing door: "Gels. Keep. Writing," and hung up her end first, so that the breach, if it was one, was hers.

Peg took the whole business the way Peg took the Blitz, which she claimed to remember and did not.

She was at the kitchen table when Nita came back from Lincoln the day of the fact-find, and she had — this was the thing, this was Peg entire — she had somehow already got the shape of it, out of the air, out of the chemist, out of the changed sound of her daughter's key in the door across months; and she had the good pot out, and the snooker off, which was Peg's flag at half-mast; and she listened to the whole of it with her hands folded on the oilcloth, the complaint, the letter, the diary on the table in Meeting Room 4, and at the end of it she was quiet a while, and then she delivered it, the verdict, the one Nita would carry into the building in Leeds a season later sewn into her coat where nobody could confiscate it:

"They'll have you for the notebook," Peg said. "You know that. It's the notebook they can see. It's the only bit of the whole affair that fits through their letterbox, so that'll be what it was all about, by the end; you watch; the woman won't be in it at all." She poured, one cup, two, steady as a beam. "Your grandmother kept a book like that on Adelaide Street. Births, bruises, whose man was walking out with whose wages. Under the mangle cloth. She called it her weather book." Peg pushed the cup across. "They burned it with her things and the street got twenty year darker in a fortnight. Drink that while it's hot." And she put the snooker back on, at low volume, which meant the flag was going back up the pole; and mother and daughter sat in front of the green baize in the lamplight, one of them suspended by a neutral act, the other one watching a young man in a waistcoat think his way round a snooker he could not see a way out of, and offering him, at intervals, unheard, across all the distance between a Marram front room and everywhere, the only coaching Peg had ever rated: "Look at it longer, son. It's there. It's nearly always there."


==============================================================================

24

Afterwards — years afterwards, when she could bear to run the tape at all — what frightened her most was how good her explanations had been.

The recycling, for instance. The flat's blue box went out fortnightly, and some point that summer it began to clink, and the clink had an explanation before it ever reached her as a question: the round. The round had gone mad; he was first-choice odd-job man from Saltfleet to Withby now; he was up at five for the pots two mornings a week on top because Ted's shoulder was bad, and a man who worked like that had a couple of cans at night, that was called being a working man on the East Coast, her own father had kept a bottle of dark mild about himself nightly her whole life. If the cans had been her dad's brand of arithmetic she'd have counted; they weren't; there was no six o'clock standing-up in Dane, no shed, no minty anything; there was just a tiredness that came home in the van with him and sat in the flat like a third tenant, and drank its couple of cans, and said less than it used to; and every single piece of that had a reason with a working postcode, and none of the reasons were wrong, and all of them together added up to a lie she was not the author of and could not have audited from where she lived, which was inside it.

The telly thing she filed under tiredness too. The adverts were everywhere that year — the swimmer one, the woman coming up out of the water not looking back, once is enough; a new one with a dad and a lad flying a kite, whatever that was selling — and somewhere in the summer she half-noticed that Dane had developed a genius for needing something at the exact moment they came on. Kettle. Van. Bins. Once, mid-kite, the bathroom, at a walk that wasn't quite a walk. She noticed it the way you notice a dripping tap in someone else's house, one room's worth of attention, gone by morning; irony being — she gave the tape this much, later — that of the two people on that sofa, she was the one who could watch the adverts with no feeling whatsoever, calm as milk, a woman watching weather from a country where it never rained; and he was the one they were reaching, some nerve in him bare to it; and if you'd stopped her that summer and asked her to bet on which of the two of them had the band-shaped thing in their life, she'd have laughed and got it wrong.

The drawing was harder to file, because the drawing had been theirs.

He'd always been her first reader. The margin D. Where's the rain go. He'd sit with a brew while she worked at the board in the bay window, Sunday mornings, doing his invoices or the crossword or nothing, quality nothing, the fryers off below and the gulls doing the town's admin outside, and it was the best furniture the week had. And that summer it stopped, not with a bang, with a migration: his invoices moved to the kitchen table, then to evenings, then to the van — he had a clipboard system now, he said, easier to do it on the job — and the Sunday bay window became hers alone, and one morning in September she looked up from a section through the arcade roof, feeling watched, and he was in the doorway with his mug, looking at her, and the look on him was — she had no file for it; it was the look you'd give a lit window from a cold street; and she said, "What?" smiling, and he said, "Nowt. Don't let me stop you," and put his mug down half-drunk and went out to the van, on a Sunday, no job on, and she heard it start and sit idling in the yard a full minute before it went anywhere, and she sat with her pencil in the air over the arcade roof and then — this was the part the tape ran slowest — went back to the drawing, because the drawing was going well, and he'd said it himself: don't let me stop you.

He said the other thing at two in the morning in October, and the tape kept every word.

She surfaced out of sleep because he wasn't in it — the bed had the listing feel of one person — and the kitchen light was on under the door, and she found him at the table with a can open and the tide clock ticking its Bridlington lie, and he was sitting the way men in her family sat at tables at two in the morning, which is to say like furniture that had been asked to hold too much, and she stood in the doorway in her dressing gown and said his name, and he looked up at her and asked it. No lead-up. A question with no clothes on, in a voice gone quiet and strange and very careful, a voice walking on a frozen pond:

"Would you have gone?"

"What?"

"London. If — " his thumb moved on the can, around the rim, around. "That autumn. If things had been — different. Any different. Do you ever — " and the pond took his weight and he went the rest of the way in: "do you ever miss it? Even a bit? You never — you don't ever talk about it, and I keep — I need to know if you miss it, Jass. Tell me the truth."

And she told him the truth. That was the unforgivable engineering of the whole thing, the part that, when she finally understood it, would put her on the floor of a different kitchen: she reached inside for London, at two in the morning, at his asking, with all the honesty in her body — actually reached, actually looked, because the strangeness of him made her check properly, the way you'd pat a pocket twice — and reported what she found, which was what had been left there for her to find, and she found it in her own voice, wearing her own manner, indistinguishable from every other true thing she'd ever said:

"I miss it like — honestly? I don't. I know I'm meant to. Everyone keeps holding the space for me to be sad in and I keep not being sad in it. I'm where I want to be, Dane. I want this — the flat, the work, the — you, you daft article, I want what we've — " and she'd crossed the lino by then and got her arms round him from behind, chin on the top of his head, doing for him what he did for her when the world listed; and under her arms his shoulders were wrong, set wrong, braced like a man taking a rope's load, and she put that down to the hour and the cans; and against her arms, very low, almost nothing, he said, "Right. Aye. That's — good, then," in the voice of a man reading his own sentence back and finding it correct, and put his other hand up over hers, and held it there a long time, too hard, the grip of the drowning, though she had no reason that autumn to know that grip by name.

The row, when they finally had one — the first real one in four years, the one Poll heard about from both of them separately and refereed by saying nothing to either — was about her future, which was the one subject in the flat with no safe grip anywhere on it, though only he knew why.

Hutton had made his move in November: called her into the back room, glasses down on the string, and offered — offered, in a trade where you begged for it — to sponsor the part-time degree. Building surveying, Lincoln, day-release stretching to three years, the firm's money, and at the end of it letters after her name and, Hutton said, looking at her over the string with the nearest he came to sentiment, "my chair, one day, if you want it; Priestley'd sooner die than manage anybody, and Barbara's retiring the day I do, she's promised." It was, on the coast, in that decade, a small kingdom on a plate; and she came home lit up with it, and Dane took the news standing at the counter, and said all the right words in the wrong order with the wrong face, and then, into the pause where the celebrating should have gone, said:

"You could still reapply, though. London. With all this behind you now, the — you'd walk in. You should. Before you're — I've been thinking, you should do it this cycle, the deadline's Jan—"

"Why," Jassy said, "would I reapply to London the same week Hutton's offered me — were you listening to what I just—"

"I'm just saying you've options—"

"I don't want options, I've got a life—" and it went from there, the way they go, up through the gears, two people who never rowed discovering they had no brakes fitted; and the shape of it, underneath the noise, the shape she couldn't see and he couldn't say, was a man trying with his bare ordinary words to push a boulder back up a hill he himself had greased, hating every calm certain answer that came back down at him, each one in her voice, each one his own handwriting — and to her it looked like the one thing it could look like from inside: a man trying to post her away. "Why do you keep doing this?" she said, at the top of it, really shouting now, the fryer-hum below them and the gulls going up off the roof. "Every time something roots me here you start — the degree, now London, it's like you're stood at the door holding me coat! Most lads would be — do you want me gone, Dane? Is that it? Is that what this is? Because if you want me gone you're going to have to say it out loud like a grown-up, because I'm not — I don't want to go anywhere! When do I ever say I want to go anywhere!"

And Dane Pyke stood against the counter of the flat he shared with the girl he'd loved since a plate of dabs on a harbour wall, with the answer in him like a swallowed hook — no, and you never will again, and it's mine, that never, I built it, I whispered it onto your sleeping head with your shoes in my hand — and what he did with his face in those four seconds she would understand only much later to have been the hardest work she ever watched anybody do; and what he said, when the face was finished, quietly, with the fight going out of him all at once like a tide leaving a pool, was: "No. I don't want you gone. Forget I — it's the round, Jass. I'm knackered. I'm sorry. The degree's grand. You'll be grand." And he came across the lino and held her, the hatch-grip, the chin on the crown, and she stood inside it hearing his heart do its too-much thing again and forgave him entirely, because from where she stood there was nothing that needed forgiving that a fortnight of early nights wouldn't fix; and she lay awake that night doing what her father had done a year before her, in another building, over the same mystery: the inventory. Money — the round was good, the van was paid, it wasn't money. His dad — Ted's shoulder, the boat's injectors, maybe. The drink — a couple of cans, working man, no. Her — no; he'd held her like the last plank of a wreck; whatever it was, it wasn't her. She went down the list twice and it audited clean, all accounts square, no harm anywhere done that any instrument of hers could reach; and she settled on the round, she filed it under knackered, she resolved — she actually resolved, in those words, in the dark, with him breathing beside her too evenly to be asleep — to book them a weekend somewhere, Whitby maybe, off-season rates, sea view, get him away from ladders and other people's boilers; and planning it, she fell asleep; and beside her in the dark, wide awake, evenly breathing, the man who had made the world she was happy in lay still as ballast, listening to her sleep in it.


==============================================================================

25

The first Sunday of December was filthy, a gale off the sea all day shouldering the town like a drunk, and the Case did eleven covers at lunch and then died on its feet, and by nine there was nobody in but Mr Ferris with the crossword and two lads off the wind farm watching the football with the sound down, and Col was cellaring up early when he heard the door do its two notes, and it was Dane.

He knew something was off from the door. Fifteen years behind a bar teaches you arrivals the way the boy's trade taught him weather: the door said it, and then the walk said it, and then the face said it — Dane crossing the room not looking at anything, wet through, not from the ten yards off the street, wet like a man who'd been out in it walking for hours, and he came to the bar and put both hands flat on it, and Col had watched a thousand men put their hands on that bar and knew every dialect of it, and this one said holding on.

"Pint, please, Col."

Not lemonade. Col drew it without a word and set it down and the lad drank a third of it straight off like medicine, which in Dane, who nursed a pint like it was somebody's baby, was a flare going up. Col wiped down within reach and stayed there.

"Boat all right? Your dad?"

"Aye. Sound." The wind leaned on the windows. Dane turned the glass a quarter-turn on the mat, and turned it back, and said, not to Col, to the glass, in a voice with all the give gone out of it: "What time do you shut?"

"When I bolt the door."

The football finished at half nine and the wind-farm lads went out into the gale with their hoods up, and Mr Ferris folded the crossword at twenty to ten, finished or abandoned, nobody would ever know, and inclined his head at the bar on his way — "Colin" — and was gone, and that left the room to the fire and the fruit machine's idiot lights going through their attract loop and the boy at the bar on his fourth pint, which for Dane Pyke was the far side of the map, sea monsters, here be. Col called nothing. He walked the room, damped the fire, killed the fruit machine at the wall, and came back behind the bar and stood opposite, and waited the way he was good at.

"I need to tell you a thing," Dane said. "And I need you to let me get all of it out before you — because if you stop me partway I don't think I'll manage the rest, and the rest's the bit that matters." He still hadn't looked up. His hands were flat either side of the glass now, pressing down, a man steadying a load that had already shifted. "And after it you can do whatever you want. Whatever you do'll be right. That's not me being — I've thought about it. There's no wrong thing you could do to me after it. That's the state of — " He stopped. Started again, lower. "It's about Jass."

Somewhere in Col a door he'd been holding for two years swung very slowly open onto exactly the cold he'd always known was behind it. He put the bar towel down. "Go on."

"Her eighteenth. The after-do, at Poll's van." Dane's thumbnail found a seam in the bar's varnish and moved along it, back, along. "You weren't there, so — there's a band. Poll's got a band, a loose one, off Kye, it's — they crack them, take the safeties off, no doctor, no records, it's the same as the real ones otherwise. It'd been sat in a drawer for months, it were a toy, it did impressions. Voice like the proper ones. We'd had it out that night, party piece, and Poll put it on Jass for a laugh — crowned her, like, birthday girl — and everyone were laughing and then everyone were asleep." The nail stopped on the seam. "Except me."

The gale took the chimney and worried it and let it go.

"She were asleep on the bench under Poll's throw. Band still on her. That little light going. And I'd had — I weren't drunk, Col, that's the — I'd had four cans over five hours, I could've drove. I want that wrote down wherever this ends up. I can't even say I were drunk." He took a breath like a man lifting from the knees. "The offer'd come that morning. London. And all night I'd been — everyone singing, your speech, her stood on that chair with her face like — and I'm stood in it clapping with everything in me going: that's it, then. That's her gone. You've had her three year and that were the lease up. And it — Col, it weren't a thought, it were a — it had hands, the feeling. I've been frightened at sea twice, proper frightened, and it were neither of them. Sat there at three in the morning with the heater going and everyone asleep and her breathing under that throw and the whole — the rest of my life sat there an' all, forty year of it, in that van, on the coast, fixing boilers, and her in London turning into whoever she were always going to be, and some lad — " he shook his head once, hard, clearing it of the lad. "And the band sat on her hair. Right there. And I knew what the loose ones'd do. Kye'd said. No lock on them, they'll take a target off anyone for anything, he'd said it like a selling point."

"So I got down by her," Dane said, "and I — " and there, for the first time, his voice went; not to tears; it went to gravel, to the voice off a boat radio; and he pushed through it the way you push through surf, graceless, committed — "I fixed her blanket first. That's the bit I — I tucked her shoulder in and I lined her shoes up so she'd not trip on them if she woke wanting the loo. I looked after her. Same minute. Same hands. And then I put my head down by hers, and I asked it could it hear me, quiet like, and it said aye, and I said — "

"Word for word," Col said. It was the first thing he'd said in ten minutes and it came out in the cellar register, flat, load-bearing. "You'll give it me word for word."

And Dane gave it him word for word, eyes shut, like a man reciting the coordinates of a wreck: "I said: 'Make her want to stay. With — here. With me. Don't let it take her. She'll stop wanting this, us — don't let her stop. Make it so she doesn't want to go.'" His eyes opened. "And it said" — and here his voice did the thing Col would hear at odd hours for the rest of his life, it went even, it went gentle, it did the impression without meaning to, the way a scar shows a blade's shape — "it said: 'Of course. Goodnight, Dane.'"

The fire ticked. The gale did its long lean. On the shelf behind the bar the three china marbles out of the sparrow's nest sat in their saucer, and the barometer that had been Wally's dad's said what it said, weather coming, and Col Harker stood in the middle of his shut pub with his hands by his sides and inside him, arriving in no order, at once, like a dropped crate of glasses: his daughter's legs going out from under her on the kitchen floor in January, over an email; the pencil seam on a delivery note, mid-January to mid-April, narrowed now to a night, to a minute, to a sentence; too many good reasons; I never yet saw you calm about a thing you wanted; a man's own voice at a bell rope, London's sent for her; and under all of it, rising slow and black and off the seabed of two years, the shape of the thing he'd known in the wrist and been laughed out of the room for: it was never her. It was done to her. In her sleep. By this one. His hand had closed on the edge of the bar. He watched it whiten from a distance, informationally, the way he'd once watched blood on his own gents floor, and he made it open, finger by finger, because there was a procedure even for this; there had to be; you built one on the spot if there wasn't.

"Who else knows."

"Nobody. Not Poll, not Kye. Nobody living but me and — " Dane stopped short of and it, which Col noted, distantly, as the first sensible thing the boy had flinched from all night.

"The band. Kye's band. Does it keep a record — can anybody get out of it what were said to it?"

"No. That's what loose means. It's the whole — I've checked. Col, I've spent two year checking. I've had Kye ask the Leeds lads sideways, said it were about summat else. There's no log, no account, nothing goes up to Lares off them, that's why they're forty quid and a crime. What went in that van stopped in that van." He said it with the exhausted thoroughness of a man reporting his own trap: he'd built it soundproof and then spent two years inside it listening.

"And it can't be — " Col heard his own voice go somewhere it had never been; he brought it back level by main strength. "Undone. Taken off her. The new ones, the — I've heard the talk same as everyone. Tell me what you know."

"It doesn't wash out." Dane said it at once, flat, a lesson conned by heart in the dark. "The old ones, you stop wearing them, it fades — weeks, months. These write it in once and it's wrote. It's not held there, it's not running, there's no machine to switch off — it's learned, Col. Same as owt else she's ever learned, same as her drawing, it's in with everything else that's her now, and there's no — I've read everything there is. There's doctors' forums. There's a professor in — I've read the actual papers, me, with a dictionary." A sound came out of him that had a laugh's skeleton and nothing else of one. "You can't unlearn a first cigarette. That's the sentence. That's the one I got it off. I've had two year to find a way round that sentence and I'm stood in your bar, so you'll know how I got on."

Col came out from behind the bar. He did it slowly, and Dane stood up off the stool to take whatever was coming and swayed and steadied himself on the bar's edge, and the two of them stood in the fire-tick and the gale-noise a yard apart, the big man and the lad, and what Col did was nothing. Stood. Looked at him: at the wet hair going grey no it wasn't, that was the light; at the good hands hanging; at the face he'd checked for a flaw two Christmases back with an engineer's square, the workmanship of the boy, the whole sound trustworthy carpentered decency of him, and somewhere in it, seamless, invisible, load-bearing, the one joint cut in the dark that had brought the whole structure down on Col's girl while she slept under a leopard throw with her shoes lined up; and Col understood, standing there, why he wasn't going to hit him, and it was not mercy: it was that there was nothing in reach worth hitting. You couldn't hit the minute. You couldn't hit the van, or the drawer, or the shoebox, or Leeds, or the telly, or the tide of the whole world that had floated the thing in. There was just a boy who had done, in one wet-eyed minute at three in the morning, with no logs and no witnesses and no undo, what men had been doing to women with locks and wages and vicars and doctors since the world was cooled — only cleaner, only kinder, only forever — and the boy had at least, at last, walked into the weather and said so out loud, which put him, Col thought, with a bitterness that tasted of iron, one whole confession ahead of most of history.

"Do you want me to stay away from her?" Dane said. Barely above the fire.

"I want yesterday back," Col said. "Seven hundred of them." He turned and went behind the bar, because the bar was where his hands knew what to do, and he took the boy's glass and swilled it and racked it, closing the account. "You'll say nowt to nobody. Not her, not Poll, not your dad, not a priest. Till I've thought. Do you hear me. You've carried it two year, you'll carry it one more week without dropping it on her because the carrying's got heavy for you." He heard the cruelty of it land and did not take it back. "Get home. Walk, don't drive. Come nowhere near this bar till you're sent for."

Dane stood a moment. He looked, in the end, not smaller but finished — surveyed, condemned, the notice served — and he nodded, and got his wet coat off the hook, and at the door, with his hand on it, he said, without turning, to the wood: "It were the best thing in my life before I did that to it. You should know that were true an' all. Both things were — " and the wind took the rest when the door went, two notes, into the gale.

Col bolted it behind him. Then he did what he did: the walk, gents, ladies, function room, corridor, lights; the fire raked; the till locked, unlocked, checked, locked; and then he stood in the middle of his shut pub with nothing left to do, and got the tin out of his cardigan, and rolled one at the corner of the bar with hands that behaved right up until the licking of the paper, and lit it — inside, at his own bar, under the beam of his own building, the first cigarette smoked in that room since the law changed a quarter of a century ago — and smoked it down to nothing in the dark with the lamp off, one small coal going up and down, up and down, slow as a buoy in the swell; and mopped the ash off the boards himself after; and went up the back stairs, third tread doubling under his foot the way it had for a hundred years, to lie in the dark beside his sleeping wife with the whole of it, and begin.


==============================================================================

26

He carried it the way you carry a full cellar hatch on your back: you can, for a while, if you keep your line straight and don't let anybody talk to you on the stairs.

The Tuesday after, he watched his daughter across the bar at Friday tea — she came alone, Dane was working, Dane was always working now, flu coming, he'd said on the phone, voice like a held breath — and she was on about the degree, Lincoln after Christmas, Hutton's chair one day, and she did an impression of Priestley meeting a level datum he disagreed with, and Ren laughed till she had to put the ladle down, and Col stood at his pumps watching the two women of his life laughing in the steam of his own kitchen and held his line straight and said the right four things in the right four places, and thought: this is what the boy's been doing for two years. This exact work. He had wondered, somewhere under the rage, how a soft-handed lad had carried it so long, and now he knew: you carried it because the alternative was to put it down on them.

Bassey he got to on the second Thursday, end of surgery, by the simple ruse of being the last appointment of the day about his knee, which was, as it happened, genuinely bad, so the first ten minutes were even true.

"And while I'm here," Col said, getting dressed, in the voice of a man remembering a small thing, "you'll know about these bands. Settle a thing for me — there's a fella I know, his lad's — " the lie he'd built on the drive over came out whole and smooth, he'd built it the way Dane built things, to bear weight — "his lad got one of them dodgy ones, the cracked ones, at a do, few year back, and some idiot set summat on him for a laugh. A — wanting. Nothing mucky, just — a want he'd not have had. And it's took. And the fella wants to know what's to be done. Can it come off. The new sort, this was. The permanent sort."

Bassey had stopped writing. He was a Friday-tired man in a room he'd sat in for thirty years, and he looked at Col for a moment with the flat clinical stillness that was the nearest he came to an accusation, and then he chose — Col watched him choose — to answer the question as asked.

"No," Bassey said. "It can't come off. And I'll tell you why, because the why's the part people won't have. There's nothing on him. Nothing's running. Folk think it's like a — a program, still going, and you find the right man in Leeds to switch it off. It's not. Them bands work the once, like a — " he turned a hand over — "like a bereavement works the once, or a first love, or seeing summat you shouldn't as a bairn. It teaches. And what's taught is grown in — it's wired the same as everything else he's ever learned, it is him now, there's no seam round it. You can't weed a thing once it's become the plant." He said it without relish, a man reading the tide table. "The lad can learn round it, in time — new wants alongside, if he's minded, if he's helped; the head stays plastic, thank God, that's the one door open. But the want itself? You don't remove it. You outnumber it. Years, that takes. And it never goes to nothing."

"And the one that set it," Col said. "If it could be proved — which it can't, there's no record, I'm told — what's the law say he did?"

"The law." Bassey took his glasses off and did the eye-rub, thorough, both heels of his hands, the gesture of the whole profession that year. "There's a case been sat in Leeds Crown Court two years now — you'll have read bits, they keep the reporting thin — lass did summat similar to her husband, or he says she did. Two years, and they're still fighting over what to charge it as. Assault wants injury and there's no injury. Poisoning wants a substance and there's no substance. Fraud wants money. There's a private member's bill and a professor on the wireless every few months and meantime the actual answer, Col, in the actual world, this morning, is: nowt. The law says he did nowt. The law's a list of the ways one person's been known to hurt another, and this way's not been on the list long enough to have a name." He put the glasses back on and looked at Col over them, and did, at last, gently, in the doorway of the thing, the one bit of doctoring the whole visit had actually been for: "This fella. The lad that got done. Is anybody in danger tonight?"

"No."

"Is there owt worth being rash over?"

"No."

"Then tell your fella this, from a man that's sat in this chair thirty year." Bassey stood, surgery over, and said it plain and quiet with his hand already on the door: "The ones that come through — this, and worse than this, and there's plenty worse — the ones that come through are the ones where somebody loved them steady on the far side of the finding-out. That's the whole of it. That's all I've got in the cupboard. Truth first, steadiness after, and no rash acts in between. And get that knee seen to, you're walking on it like a man that owes it money."

He told Ren the same night, because after Bassey there was no room left in him to sleep beside her holding it even once more.

He'd meant to build up to it and there is no building up to it; he sat her down at the kitchen table at half past midnight with the bar lamp off and the kettle not on — she clocked the kettle not on, he saw her clock it — and he said it the way the lad had taught him, all of it out before anything could stop him partway, the party, the drawer, the loose band, the crown, the shoes lined up, word for word the wording, of course, goodnight, Dane; and he watched thirty years of his wife's face do what it did.

She didn't cry at first. First she went to the science of it, hard and fast, the way she costed a menu — it can't, they can't, there's laws, there's that floor thing, it was on the news, they built a floor — and he took each one and laid it down dead in front of her, no floor on a cracked one, no log, no crime with a name, no undo, consolidated is consolidated, and he watched his wife run out of shelves to put it on, watched the fact of it come and stand in her kitchen with its wet coat on, unreturnable; and then Ren said, "her legs went. In January. Her legs went from under her on my kitchen floor," and pressed the flat of her hand hard on the table, once, as though killing something small, and then she cried — and it was the old crying, the pre-band crying, the kind that had gone out of the house with the six o'clock want, ugly and heaving and from the stomach, and Col came round the table and held her and she hit him once on the chest with the side of her fist, not for anything, for somewhere to put it, and cried the way she'd cried when her mother went, and Guinness fled the kitchen entirely, and the extractor hummed, and the sea worked at the town, and none of the improvements of the age were anywhere in that room at all; it was all just the old human plant, ineradicable, doing what it had always done at kitchen tables at one in the morning since kitchens were invented.

"I defended it," she said, when she could. Her voice had gone to gravel. "That's what I keep — I stood in this kitchen, Col, I stood there — " she pointed at the actual flagstones, prosecuting the room itself — "and I told you she's allowed to change her mind. I made you feel mad for — you knew. You're a great lump who can't say three feelings a year and you knew in your bones, and I'm her mother and I patted your hand and bagged the coppers." She wiped her face with the heel of her hand, savage. "'People change their minds.' I'll hear meself saying that till they box me. And the worst — " and here her voice dropped into the register he'd only heard from her twice in thirty years, at two gravesides — "the worst is I knew what it feels like from inside, and I still couldn't tell the difference. Me. I've had me own wanting turned like a tap, by me own choosing, and I looked at me own daughter turned by somebody else's and I saw nowt. It looks the same, Col. It looks exactly the same." She stared at the dark window. "That's the wickedness of it. It doesn't look like anything."

They came, in the end, past two, past the point of tea gone cold, to the only question with any give in it: whether to tell her.

Col put Bassey's physics on the table plainly, because it had to be weighed and he was the one who'd fetched it: telling her would not un-want it. There was no version where she heard it and the want lifted off her like a gull off a rail. She would hear it, and believe it or not believe it, and either way go on wanting to stay, wanting Dane, wanting the small good life she was that minute asleep in the middle of — only now knowing, or refusing to know, where the wanting came from. "We'd not be giving her back what were took," Col said, flat, honest, the case against himself. "There's no giving that back. We'd be handing her the receipt for it. That's all we've got to give her. A receipt, and a war in her own head till she dies, likely. Bassey said the ones that come through — he said there's coming through. He didn't say what through looks like." He turned his cold mug a quarter-turn, and back. "And so I've sat three weeks doing the other sum. She's happy. It's a real life. He's — God rot him, it's the one part I can't even — he'd have been a good — " he couldn't finish that one; it went down with the rest. "If we say nowt, she lives happy in a house we know's got weather in it. If we speak, we take the roof off with our own hands, in January, and stand there while she — " and he stopped, and the kitchen ticked, and Ren, who had been sitting very still through all of it with her hands flat on the table, said, in a voice with the crying burned all the way out of it, the low level voice she used for the final figure on a costing:

"She'll marry him."

Col looked at her.

"That's your sum finished, love. That's the line you've not let yourself put in. She's twenty in a month. They're saving — she thinks he doesn't know she's got the account, he thinks she doesn't know he's got one an' all, I do both their eyes at Christmas, I know everything. Two year, three, there'll be a ring. Then bairns — she wants bairns, she's told me, she wants them young like we — " Ren's voice caught on the we and came on through it. "And every one of them things gets built on top of it. On top of a sentence said onto her sleeping head by a frightened lad in a caravan. You want to talk about roofs — every year we say nowt, the house gets taller, Col. Taller and fuller. Folk in it. And the weather still in the walls." She stood, and took the two cold mugs to the sink, and swilled them, and set them on the rack, and stood with her back to him a moment, hands on the sink edge, looking at the black window where the garden would be; and then she turned round and was, entirely, terribly, herself again, the woman who ran the till, and said: "She gets told. All of it, no dressing. And he tells her — he stands there and says it out of his own mouth with us in the room, because she'll need somewhere to put her eyes that isn't him, and because if it comes from us she'll spend a year hating the messenger and calling him the victim, I know her, she's mine." A breath. "And not before Christmas. She gets Christmas. One more" — and there the till voice went, just for four words, into somewhere underneath everything — "one more good one. Then January. Ring the boy."

Christmas came and was had. That was the only verb Col could ever fit to it after: it was had. The goose, the crackers, Wally with his tin of Roses and the Grimsby video, Jassy and Dane — flu recovered, summoned by Ren herself on the phone in a voice you could have iced a cake with, because appearances were part of the sentence now and Ren intended every day of it served — Dane at the table doing the work Col now knew by name, the right four things in the right four places, the line held straight, the two of them, poisoner and grandfather-not-to-be, passing the sprouts down opposite sides of a table with the same hod on each back; and Jassy in the middle of it all, lit up, flanked by every adult who loved her, being lied to in perfect unison for the last time; and she pulled the big cracker with her mother and won, and wore the yellow hat all through the pudding, and if anyone ever built a machine that could take that afternoon out of Col Harker — the sight of her in the yellow paper hat, laughing at her own bad joke off the little slip, twenty in three weeks, happy in the house of it — he'd have paid the four thousand pound, some nights, in the years after. Other nights he'd have burned the factory down. There was no want in him anywhere that pointed just one way again, ever, about any of it; that was what the age had done to wanting; and on the second of January, at ten past nine in the morning, he rang Dane Pyke, who answered before the second ring like a man who slept with his boots on, and said five words, and put the phone down:

"Sunday. After close. Both of us."


==============================================================================

27

She knew it was bad because the function room was set for four.

Not set — there was no cloth, no cutlery — but arranged: four chairs at one trestle under the fairy lights nobody had taken down since some do or other, and her mum's good cardigan on, ten o'clock at night, and the heating left running after close, which her father did for christenings and deaths. Her first full theory, forming between the door and the table, was the pub: they'd sold, or the bank had moved, and they'd asked Dane along because Dane was family now in every way but paper and you'd want the lad there if you were telling the daughter the Case was going. Her second theory, overtaking the first as she got the coats-and-faces read done — Dane stood by the window not looking at her, grey as gutting water, hands hanging — was that he was ill. Something on a scan. And the whole of her went out to him across the room so hard and so fast that she had crossed and taken his arm before anyone had said a word, which meant that when it started she was stood holding on to him, which she would think about, afterwards, for the rest of her life.

"Sit down, love," Ren said. "Dane's got something to say to you, and you're going to want to be sat."

He said it all. She had to give him that much even that night, even inside the furnace of it: he stood there and said it in order, no crying, no asking anything of her, in the flat rehearsed voice of a man who has said a thing a hundred times to a bathroom mirror so that he'd be able to say it once in a room — her eighteenth, the after-do, the drawer, the band, everyone asleep; her asleep; the blanket, the shoes — he put the shoes in; she watched him make himself put the shoes in — and then, word for word, what he'd whispered, and word for word what it had said back; and the fairy lights did their small stupid twinkle on the bricked-up arch of the window she'd measured at sixteen, and the radiator ticked, and her parents sat still as pews, watching her, both of them, the whole time; and Jassy stood in the middle of it and felt, in order, the following, and could name every one of them even as they happened, which was the worst of it, that she was present and articulate all the way down:

First: relief. Actual relief, a physical drop of it, because he wasn't dying; whatever mad thing this was, it wasn't a scan.

Second: the laugh. It got out of her before anything could stop it — one bark of it, horrible in the room, because the thing he was describing was an episode of something, it was a story off a forum, a lad kneeling by a sleeping girl whispering to a hairband, and for a second the whole night tipped toward farce and she nearly had them all: "okay, whose idea — is this an intervention? Is it about the — because if this is about me missing Poll's birthday do—"

And then she saw her father's face, and the farce fell out of the bottom of the night.

Third — and this was where, running it back forever after, she could first see the machine, because the speed was wrong; grief should be slower than this — third came the case for the defence. Fully formed. Filed and cross-referenced, arriving not as thoughts arrive, effortfully, one dragging the next, but the way water arrives through a failed seal: everywhere at once. She heard herself present it and it was good, it was so good, point after point in her own true voice with her own true memories behind it —

"— I remember deciding, that's what none of you — I can walk you through the whole spring. The money. You want to see the spreadsheet? I built the spreadsheet in March, it's got seventeen tabs, did the hairband do the tabs? Hutton's letter came because Adeoti showed him the Elevations, which I started, me, at Easter, off the compass rose, off Dad's own — you told me about that floor, Dad, when I was eight! Is that the band an' all? Is everything I've ever loved about this town evidence now? That's the — no. No. Listen. I did not wake up different. You think I wouldn't know? It's my head. I've lived in it twenty years, I know its — I sat in that kitchen and made the best decision of my life with a folder, and now a — because a drunk lad with a guilt complex tells you a ghost story about a party, two years — two years, Dane! — after the — where's the recording? Where's the log, the — there's nothing, is there. There's his word. You're taking his word over mine about the inside of my own head. Do you actually hear — Mum. You of all — you're taking a man's word over mine about what I want."

It was unanswerable. She knew it was unanswerable as she built it, could feel the joints going home true, every argument bearing weight, and some cold sliver of her — the clerk, the last lamp — watched the sheer performance of it, the fluency, arguments she had never consciously assembled arriving gowned and wigged, and made, very quietly, in the last office, its small mark: too fast. Where were these kept. Who filed them ready. But the sliver had no voice and the flood had all of them; and her mother, who had sat still through the whole speech with her hands flat on the trestle, waited for the end of it, and then said, not loudly:

"The inside of your wardrobe door."

"...What?"

"When you moved out, I went up to hoover that room, and the door were stood open, and it were bare." Ren's eyes didn't move off her. "Five year, Jass. The staircase, the bus map with the star on. I watched that door get dressed like a shrine since you were fifteen; I never said owt because it weren't mine to — when did you take it down? Not roughly. The day. Tell me what you did with the — was it a bin bag, was it the recycling, did you fold them? You fold everything. Tell me the day, and I'll stand Dane a taxi home and never speak of this again."

And Jassy reached — furious, certain, pocket-patting — for results week, for the big sort-out, for the black bags everyone had seen, for it must have been then, it had the shape of then — and stood in the function room of her father's pub with all three of them watching, and felt, for the second time and this time in company, the explanation come away in her hand. Too smooth. No wear on it.
