A part that fitted the hole without ever having been the part; and the vertigo came up through the floor of her like the room had been built over water, one long second of it, two — the bare cork-holes, the grey sun-ghosts, no day, no bag, no folding, nothing where the memory should be, a corridor with a wall where a door —

— and the flood came over it, warm and instant and hers, and sealed it, and she heard her own voice arrive already scornful: "I moved out, Mum. Grown women take posters down. I'm sorry I didn't minute it for you — " and the moment was gone, tiled over, and only the clerk saw where the join was, and the clerk kept its ledger in a hand nobody in that room could read yet, least of all her.

What broke her stride in the end wasn't evidence. It was Dane — Dane, who had stood through her whole defence with his eyes down, taking his flaying like weather, and who, when she rounded on him at last — "and you. Why, Dane? What's — is this some, what, you want out and you haven't the nerve so you've invented a reason I'll have to — why would you say this about yourself?" — looked up and said, in the small wrecked remainder of his voice:

"Because it's true, and you were about to let me marry you on it."

The radiator ticked. And Jassy felt the sentence go into her and find nothing it could join to — that was the sensation, precise as a pulled tooth; it was true and there was nowhere in her for it to be true at; the want lay under everything like the water table under the town, and his confession fell on it like rain on a flooded field, and made no new difference, could make no new difference, there was no dry ground anywhere in her for the horror to land — and she watched herself do the thing the field does with rain, which was absorb it into what was already there: and what was already there was that she loved him and he was hers and he was standing in a cold function room destroying himself in front of her family, and the destruction slotted, seamless, into the shape of everything else about him that autumn — the cans, the adverts, the two a.m. question, the doorway look — and the case assembled itself before her eyes with the same gowned fluency as all the others: he was not a monster; he was ill. He'd been overworking for a year; he'd got some — off the forums, all that reading he did at night, she'd seen the search history months ago and thought nothing, bands, courses, consolidation — he'd built himself a guilt-castle out of a drunk memory of a party trick, a lad who whispered something daft at a toy in a caravan and had spent two years torturing it into a crime because that was Dane, that was pure Dane, the boy who re-sanded finished edges, who checked the beam nobody had asked him to check, who loved her past all sense and had found, in some spiralling fever of it, a way to make even her happiness his fault — and she crossed the room to him through her parents' silence, and took his face in both hands, and said, with the whole of her heart, in the gentlest voice in her:

"You need help, love. You've needed help for months and I've not — we'll get you seen. We'll get you right."

Nobody moved. Behind her, at the trestle, she heard her mother make one small sound and stop it with her hand; and her father said, into the fairy-lit quiet, in the low flat cellar voice, the worst thing anyone said all night, because it was the plainest: "He's not ill, Jass. I've checked every joint of him for two year. I checked his story same way. It stands."

"Then you're ill an' all," she said, and it came out of her calm as a tide table, and she was already turning, already gathering her coat off the chair with one hand and Dane's sleeve with the other, and the room let her do it, the room had run out of things that could be said in it. At the door — she would hate this the longest, of everything, longer than the laugh — at the door she turned round, one hand on the frame her own drawings had measured, and delivered the exit line, and it was magnificent, and it was fluent, and it was in her own voice with her own chin up, and she never once in later years managed to remember composing it:

"You know what none of you can stand? That I'm happy. That it's this — " the room, the pub, the town, the boy shaking under her hand — "that I want. Everybody needs a machine to explain a lass who stays. Nobody ever needed one to explain me leaving — that were just called ambition, everyone signed off on that one no questions asked. Well. I'm not a mystery and I'm not a victim and I'm going home, to my flat, with my — " one beat; a single tick, that nobody in the room could have named, where a word was fetched and came — "with my Dane. And you can all sit here with your ghost story and decide whether you want to be my family or my — diagnosis. Door's the same either way."

The walk home was four hundred yards of gale off the sea and neither of them spoke.

She held his hand the whole way, hard, the grip you'd use on a cliff path with a child, and he let himself be held, walking beside her like something salvaged and in tow; and at the flat she made tea neither drank, and put him to bed the way you'd fold a coat that had been through something, and lay beside him in the dark over the dead fryers with her heart going slow and even and absolutely certain, home, all's well, the day's own voice at the day's own close; and it was not until nearly four, on the far edge of sleep, in the no-man's-land where the clerk sometimes got a word out before the wire, that Jasmine Harker heard herself think, from a long way down, in a voice that did not frighten her nearly as much as it should have:

if it were true, it would feel exactly like this.

And the water closed over it, and she slept.


==============================================================================

28

The building in Leeds had a revolving door and a signing-in screen that misspelled her name twice, and the hearing room was called Suite 3, and everything in it — the carpet tiles, the water jugs, the little tented name cards — had the neutral act quality of the letters, a room designed by the same committee that had designed the sentences, a room you could not imagine anybody in the world loving; and on the panel's raised table there were three tented cards, CHAIR, REGISTRANT MEMBER, LAY MEMBER, and on hers, NURSE A. OSEI, and across the aisle the case presenter had no card at all, which Nita spent some of the worst hours of her life deciding was either an oversight or the whole architecture of the thing in one detail: the building spoke; the building needed no name.

Peg came all three days. This had been resisted and was not resistible: seventy-nine, with a thermos and the tartan shopping trolley, installed in the public seats each morning at nine like a magistrate — and on the first morning, in the corridor, by the water cooler, the thing happened that Nita would keep out of everything that followed the way you keep one coal off a fire: a tall old woman with Vera Trant's long bones came across the corridor and said, "You'll be the mother," and Peg said, "You'll be the sister," and the two of them shook hands once, hard, like foremen, and sat together thereafter, sharing the thermos, a two-woman public gallery with a combined age of a hundred and fifty and a combined verdict already in, watching the machinery do what the machinery did, and disapproving of it in perfect stereo silence.

The case presenter was a young man called Mr Ellard who was not cruel, which Nita had not expected and found harder. Cruelty she'd have known where to put. Mr Ellard was thorough and courteous and slightly sad throughout, a curate of the procedure, and he took the panel through it in the procedure's own tenses: that the registrant did, on or about; that the registrant failed to; and the charges stood up one by one on the screen in their numbered rows, and the strange thing, the thing that began the slow chemical work on her that three days in Suite 3 would complete, was that every charge was true.

She had disclosed information gained in a professional capacity to a third party — a hundred and eight miles away, over tea and a state-visit tablecloth, to the one human being on earth who was owed it. True.

She had failed to maintain professional boundaries with a patient's family — if boundaries meant the line past which you agreed to stop looking. True.

She had kept records concerning patients and their families outside approved systems — in biro, in a 2009 diary, under a church magazine, because the approved systems had a box for a bruise. True, true, true; there was almost a peace in it, the first morning, hearing her whole two years of care returned to her translated into the dialect of misconduct, every sentence accurate the way a chalk outline is accurate: the exact shape of the thing, and nothing whatever of what it had been doing when it was alive.

They read the diary on the second morning. That was the worst of it, and Mr Ellard did it without theatre, which made it worse: he simply read, into the microphone, in his level curate's voice, the entries flagged in the bundle at tab 14, and Nita sat with her hands folded and listened to her own handwriting come back at her through the building's acoustics.

"'April 30th. V.T. — saw her come up.'" A pause while the panel found the page. "'Watched it sink. Band on throughout.' And further down, panel — 'NB the frightened bit came up with the rest. It's all one water table.'" He let the room have its silence. "'Re-bagged the canvas.'" He looked up, mild. "Nurse Osei, we'll come to this in questions, but so the panel has it in context now: this describes you concealing from a patient's carer — physically covering up — evidence that his wife had been distressed in your presence. Yes?"

"It describes me putting a bag back," Nita said, "so that a locked room would stay worth locking."

"I see," said Mr Ellard, sadly, and read on; and the entries in that room were monstrous. That was the discovery of the second day. In her bedroom, at midnight, in biro, they had been the most careful work of her life. Read aloud in Suite 3, by a courteous stranger, to three tented cards — dates, initials, the tea towels, the box of thirty, whose voice supplied whose words, the four seconds it took a woman's face to go under — they were the dossier of a stalker. She heard it happen; she could not stop it happening; the acoustics of the building simply did it, the way a mortuary slab does something to a face: same features, wrong light, all the living reasons gone. And at tab 14, page 9, Mr Ellard paused very slightly, and read: "'Write the bus in,'" and looked at it, and elected, courteously, to spare everyone the attempt, and moved on; and Nita sat there while the one entry that held the whole of it — Wally's bus, the want with nobody at the stop, the entire weather system of what had come to her coast — went past the panel unglossed, filed, like everything else in the country that mattered now, under phrases no form had a field for.

Roy Trant gave his evidence on the second afternoon, and he was magnificent, and she had known he would be and it helped not at all.

He wore the blazer with the lifeboat pin. He called the chair "madam" and the lay member "sir" and got the registrant member's title right, which nobody did; he was seventy-three and stood the whole time out of courtesy to the room until the chair pressed him twice to sit; and he said, in the course of forty minutes, nothing — not one checkable syllable — that was false. Nurse Osei's clinical care had been beyond reproach; he wished that stated at the outset. The INR management had been exemplary. Their door had always been open to her — "we're of a generation, madam, that's glad of the knock" — and it had been a slow and genuinely painful thing, he said, to feel the visits change character; to find one's private arrangements the subject of — he searched, with his precise sorrow, for the kind word, and found it — of curation; to learn that one's wife's little frailties, entrusted to a professional in one's own sitting room, had travelled a hundred miles to be discussed over tea in another county. "I don't doubt the sincerity," Roy Trant said, at the end, unprompted, turning slightly to look at her directly for the only time, with an expression of such measured, public, forgiving regret that she felt the panel's sympathy move like a draught: "I have known many devoted people in a long life in education, madam, and devotion unregulated becomes — this. I bear Nurse Osei no ill will at all. I think she is perhaps lonely. I think the work was her family. It does not excuse; but it explains; and I'm sorry for it." And he inclined his head to the chair, and was thanked, and stepped down, and helped himself courteously past Nita's table without a flicker, a man walking, as he had walked for fifty years, through rooms that supplied his titles for him; and in the public seats Peg's thermos cup paused at her lip, and Grace Holt's long hands lay in her lap like tools set down, and neither woman's face moved at all, which in that generation was the loudest available scream.

The gambling arrived on the third morning, wrapped in kindness, as she'd been warned it would.

It came in through the occupational health report, which came in through her own honesty, which was the joke of the whole architecture: every plank they built her scaffold from, she had cut and handed them herself. Mr Ellard took no pleasure in it. He established, gently, the self-referral; the course; the "period of considerable personal difficulty coinciding with the index events"; and then he asked it, the question his whole three days had been walking toward, so softly that the microphone barely woke for it:

"Nurse Osei — and take whatever time you need — do you think it possible that your own experience of compulsion, of a want you couldn't govern and then, through the same technology, could — do you think it possible this sensitised you? That you looked at an elderly couple's ordinary, consented, clinically supervised arrangements, and saw — projected — the thing you feared in yourself? Control, where there was care?"

And Nita looked at her folded hands, and gave it the time he'd offered, because it deserved time; it was the best question anyone had asked her in two years; it was the question she'd asked herself on the A1 in the dark with the Wensleydale on the seat, and at the kitchen table over the referral, and on the edge of her bed with the diary open on her knees, eleven times, fifty; and she lifted her head and answered it into the microphone as plainly as she'd ever answered anything in a doorway.

"Yes," she said. "It's possible. I'd be a fool to sit here and tell you it isn't, and you'd be fools to believe me. I've been wrong before. I'd sooner be wrong now — I'd give the pension to be wrong now, sir, because if I'm wrong, there's just a nice old couple in a bungalow and a nosy nurse, and everyone goes home." She kept her eyes on Mr Ellard, because he had asked honestly and was owed it back. "But you asked what I think, so here's the whole of it. I think being got at by a thing myself is the only reason I could see it. I stood in a shop for six years with my own want doing my arithmetic for me, and it looked, from outside, like a woman buying a meal deal. Nothing to see. I know what nothing-to-see can have inside it — that's not projection, sir, that's the one qualification I've got that your bundle hasn't. And I wrote it in a book at home because — " and there it was; she felt the room arrive, all together, at the place the whole three days had been circling; the chair's pen had stopped; " — because you've a box for a bruise. You've a box for a burn, and missed insulin, and a man who shouts. I've filled your boxes in for twenty-two years and where the box was there, I used it. There's no box for a woman who's being made smaller on a schedule. There's no box for consent that's been — tuned, year on year, till it'd consent to anything, pleasant as you like. The forms assume the wanting's still hers. All your thresholds sit on top of that one assumption, and the assumption's gone, sir — the world grew a hole under your paperwork, and I fell in it, and I'm not the last." She unfolded her hands, and folded them the other way, the only movement she allowed the whole answer. "You'll do what you'll do with the charges; they're true; I've said so from the first letter. I'd only ask the panel to write down — wherever it is you write the things there's no box for — that nobody in this room, in three days, me included, has been able to finish the sentence 'Mrs Trant is safe because.' We've established I broke the rules. We've not once established she's all right. Them's different findings. That's all."

The chair asked, into the long quiet after, whether Mr Ellard had further questions. Mr Ellard, looking at his papers, said no; and the lay member, a retired teaching assistant from Pontefract who had said almost nothing in three days, leaned to her microphone and asked the only question the panel itself ever put to the heart of it:

"Just so I understand, Nurse — what is it you say Mr Trant has actually done? In a sentence. What's the offence?"

"There isn't one," Nita said. "That's what I've been trying to report for two years, love. There isn't one. That's the emergency."

The panel retired at ten past three to consider, and the building's afternoon went on around the little suite of them left in the corridor — Nita, the union solicitor checking trains on his phone with the gentleness of a man at a bedside, and the public gallery of two repacking the thermos — and it was there, on the corridor's wipe-clean bench, under a laminated poster about values, that Grace Holt, who had travelled from Ripon three days running and been permitted to say nothing in any room that counted, took Nita's hand in both of hers, the strong floury grip, and held it, and said the only summing-up the whole week produced that Nita ever afterwards rated:

"My mother had a saying for tribunals and weather. 'They can only tell you what it is. They can't tell you what it was.'" She squeezed, once, hard. "You told them what it was, love. It's in their minutes now, whatever else is. It's wrote down in the building. They'll have to keep it forever — that's the rules; my Harold was a clerk; nothing in a minute ever dies." And she sat back with her hands returned to her lap, tools set down, a tall old woman who had swung everything she had, twice, and hit nothing either time but the future; and Peg poured the last of the thermos into the one cup, and gave it to Nita without comment; and the three of them sat in a row on the wipe-clean bench under the values, waiting to be told what it is.


==============================================================================

29

The determination took eleven pages to say what it said, and the chair read the operative parts aloud at half past eleven the next morning in a voice like a tide table: facts proved, all charges; misconduct, found; impairment, found — current impairment of fitness to practise by reason of misconduct, a phrase Nita wrote down while it was being said to her, on the pad they provided, because writing things down was apparently what she did now under all conditions, including her own sentencing.

The sanction was conditions of practice, eighteen months: supervised practice only; no lone working; no unsupervised contact with patients' families; a named mentor; quarterly reviews. Not struck off. The union solicitor breathed out through his nose and pressed her forearm once, congratulation at a bedside; the panel had been impressed, he murmured, by her candour; it could have been the register.

And buried at page nine, in the section nobody reads, one sentence with no operative force whatsoever, which the lay member from Pontefract had — Nita would always believe this, with no evidence, to her dying day — sat in a retiring room for a day and a half refusing to give up: The panel records its concern, while making no finding in this regard, that existing safeguarding frameworks may not adequately capture emerging forms of harm associated with consented neuro-behavioural devices, and would invite attention to this lacuna at the appropriate level.

Lacuna. Grace Holt had it photocopied at the library in Ripon before the month was out, and sent one copy to her MP, and kept the other in the sideboard with her Harold's clerking medals; nothing in a minute ever dies. It was, Nita thought, reading it on the train home — Peg asleep beside her, upright, the thermos empty, the tartan trolley between her knees like a corgi — probably the whole of her career's yield, that sentence. Twenty-two years, eleven thousand doorways, and the sum of it banked at page nine of a document about her own misconduct: an invitation to attention, at the appropriate level, extended to a hole in the world.

She resigned three weeks later, and it wasn't the conditions, though the conditions did the arithmetic for her — supervised practice meant no district work, meant a treatment room in Louth with a mentor half her age initialling her dressings, meant the round, her round, parcelled out between a bank nurse and a healthcare assistant with eleven visits on the sheet meaning eleven. It was a Tuesday morning that decided it, her first back, in the treatment room, taking staples out of a farmer's shin — good honest work, work she'd have been glad of at twenty-five — and the mentor, a kind girl, thorough, stood at her shoulder the whole time as required, watching, initialling; and Nita heard herself making the farmer comfortable, the old liturgy, you're my favourite, that's why, and heard it land wrong in the observed air, heard the kindness become, under supervision, a technique being assessed — and she finished the shin, and dressed it, and signed, and countersigned, and went out to the car park, and sat in the Vauxhall doing the one audit left to do.

The job was the doorways. It had never been the tasks; the tasks could be taught to anyone with steady hands; the job was that you came in alone, and were let in as yourself, and saw what the house showed you when it wasn't being watched watching you. That was the instrument. That was the only instrument there had ever been — Peg's mother's weather book, Grace's Sundays, her own diary, the whole unfunded parish science of women who look — and the conditions, reasonably, proportionately, with the panel's noted regret, had confiscated it. What was left was nursing with a witness stood on your shadow, and she was forty-eight, and she had some pension, and Peg's house had no mortgage on it; and in the door pocket, when she reached across for the ice scraper because the windscreen had gone to fern, her hand found the Twixes. Nine of them, some soft with age, Elsie Brand's tithe, the round's whole currency of being let in; and Nita Osei ate one fun-size Twix in a frosted car park in Louth, slowly, with the engine off, and cried for the length of it exactly, and then stopped, because the other thing the doorways taught you was which griefs to feed; and drove home, and typed the letter that night on Peg's sideboard with the snooker murmuring, and Peg read it over her shoulder and said only, "About time. You were wasted fetching and carrying for that lot," which was Peg for I watched them take the best thing you had and call it mercy, and neither sentence needed the other said aloud.

The room at St Aidan's she took in April, and could not for six weeks have told anyone why.

The notice was on the church hall board between the bell-ringers and a Pilates: ROOM AVAILABLE TUESDAY MORNINGS, APPLY VICARAGE, and she read it twice on a Sunday while waiting for Peg's flowers to be watered, and heard herself, in the vicarage porch that same afternoon, taking it — a room that smelled of stacked chairs and Scouts, a serving hatch, a wall-boiler that ran to a rumble, four pounds a week "toward the heating," the vicar not asking what for, church halls having learned centuries since that the what-for comes later or never. She swept it. She descaled the urn, which had a lagoon in it you could have farmed. She put out — this took her the longest, one Tuesday morning alone, trying arrangements like a woman hanging one picture — eight chairs. Not in rows; rows were a service. Not a circle; a circle was therapy, and she had no letters for therapy now, no letters for anything, she was a private citizen with an urn. She settled on nothing: eight chairs in the loose accidental constellation of a kitchen after a wake, near the hatch, near the tea; and she put a card in the Costcutter window and another on the surgery board, which Deb placed dead centre without being asked, and the card said, in her best print, the truest sentence she could compose after a month of drafting shorter and shorter ones:

TUESDAYS, 10–12, ST AIDAN'S HALL. FOR ANYONE FINDING THINGS HARD SINCE COMING OFF A BAND, OR STILL ON ONE, OR BEING GOT AT BY ANYTHING. TEA. NO FORMS. — N. OSEI

"Being got at by anything," said Peg, reviewing the draft. "That's the whole town, that."

"It's the whole species, Mum."

"Well," Peg said. "You'll want more chairs."

Trish Lofthouse came the first Tuesday, at twenty past ten, in her work tabard, on the excuse of dropping off crochet for the tombola; sat with a tea for ten minutes on the edge of a chair like a woman in a doctor's waiting room; said nothing that mattered; and at eleven, at the door, with the tabard already on, said, into the room's steam, not quite at Nita: "It's still stood outside, you know. Waiting on me. Four year." And came back the next Tuesday, and the one after, and on the fourth Tuesday brought the crochet in and did it in the chair while she talked, which was when Nita knew the room would live: the day the hands came out.

By June there were five of them, and no two the same shape. Trish, walking her long way back for the second time. A lad called Robbie off the rigs whose focus course had lapsed with his contract and who described his own head, memorably, into his tea, as "a browser with forty tabs and the fan going." A retired schoolteacher from Withby, banded for grief by her own choosing after her husband died, who had come off it deliberately two years on — "I wanted him back, even the missing-him bit, especially the missing-him bit" — and been ambushed by the arrears, the grief arriving with two years' interest, and who came Tuesdays mainly, she said, to say his name to people who hadn't known him, which turned out to be a service no machine and no surgery had ever once thought to stock. And a man who came three times and never spoke and stopped coming, and about whom Nita did the thing the room existed to do, which was worry, and keep his chair in the constellation, and leave the door on the latch.

She had no theory. She made a point of having no theory, told them so, first thing, every new face: I'm not a counsellor, I've no letters, there's no method here and no folder; there's the urn, and the same chairs every week, and the same time every week, and me, and whatever you carry in you can set down between ten and twelve and nobody will write it anywhere. The nothing was the method. She was two months into it before she recognised where she'd learned it — that she was running, in a church hall, on four pounds a week, the only model of care she'd ever seen actually hold a human being together across years, which was a pub. Same time, same chairs, same faces, no forms, no cure, no discharge date; the Case Is Altered with an urn; Col's trade practised without the licence, as everything true in her life was now practised without the licence; and when the recognition arrived she laughed out loud alone in the hall, startling the urn, and put the kettle on again in its honour.

Roy Trant she saw most Tuesdays, at distance, that summer — St Aidan's being his church, the hall being its hall, the fundraising ladder being propped that season against the tower roof. He was courteous the first time their paths crossed in the churchyard, as she'd known he would be: a slight pause on the path between the yews, a slight inclination of the head, senior colleague to junior, no triumph in it anywhere, which was the completest triumph available; and Nita inclined her head back, civilian to churchwarden, and neither said one word; and behind him, at the bungalow a hundred yards off through the trees, the monkey puzzle stood over the square-cut hedge, and the upstairs curtains of the spare room were shut, in June, at ten in the morning.

She went in and counted her chairs, and filled the urn, and set out the milk. It was what there was. It was not nothing.

It was Tuesday.


------------------------------------------------------------------------------

PART FOUR — LAST ORDERS


==============================================================================

30

For seven months she lived in the flat with a man she loved and a fact she didn't, and of the three of them the fact ate best.

The siege lines got drawn in the first fortnight. Her parents were the enemy, then not the enemy, then something worse than either, which was patient: after the second shouting match — her doing all the shouting, in the pub kitchen, at close, her mother standing at the sink taking it like weather with her hands in the washing-up — they simply stopped arguing the case. No leaflets, no looks. Friday teas went on being cooked and her chair went on being laid, and if she came they fed her, and if she didn't her mother sent Poll round with the leftovers in a tub, and nobody said the word band within a mile of her; they'd gone, she raged to Dane, all long game; they'd decided to outlast her — and Dane, who was the one subject of the war and its only neutral, said, from inside whatever he lived in now, "aye, well. They love you," which was no use to anybody, and true, and the trouble with the whole year in six words.

Dane wouldn't recant. That was the thing she couldn't get any purchase on, the smooth wall of him: he did everything else she asked — went to the doctor's like a lamb, sat through the assessment she'd browbeaten him into, took the leaflets about occupational burnout, thanked the nice woman — and then came home and, asked directly, gently, over tea, whether he could see now where the guilt-story had come from, looked at her with those wrecked steady eyes and said, "It came from me doing it, Jass." Never louder than that. Never once, in seven months, louder than that — no insistence, no pleading, no evidence produced at midnight, just the fact restated whenever she walked into it, calm and worn as a stile: it's true; I did it; I'll be here when you can look. She told him he was cruel. She told him the kindest thing he could do, if he loved her, was take it back. And Dane — this she filed even then, at the height of the siege, in the last office, because it refused every other file — Dane, who had never in four years denied her anything, who'd have carried the sea up Keel Street in buckets if she'd asked, said no. "I took enough back off you," he said, and went out to the van, and sat in it in the yard for an hour, engine off, while she watched from the window over the dead fryers and hated him with a completeness that had, at its centre, like a wasp in jam, the beginning of belief. Because there was no version of Dane Pyke that held a line to hurt her. There never had been. The line was holding him.

She read. God, she read — nights, at the kitchen table, laptop and biro, while he lay awake pretending otherwise in the next room; she read the way she'd revised, in siege ration, systematically, telling herself she was assembling the case against, and for a while the case against went well: the mechanism pieces were paywalled or hedged, the tabloid stuff was hysteria, the Leeds trial had collapsed — collapsed! she'd actually punched the air, alone, at 1 a.m., over a court report — for want of a nameable offence. And then one night in April she found the forum.

It was a support board, buried, badly designed, for partners and families, and she'd gone in scoffing and stayed four hours, and what did for her wasn't the horror stories. It was the grammar. Post after post, stranger after stranger, in Perth and Preston and somewhere Dutch, describing from inside the exact weather of her own head in words she'd have sworn were private: the reasons arriving too fluent. The explanation that fits the hole without being the part. My husband says the day I changed and I can't find the day — there's no seam, that's what nobody understands, it's not like a memory missing, it's like the join was always there. And one — she sat very still over this one for a long time, the fryer smell coming up through the boards, the tide clock ticking its Bridlington lie — one from a woman in Carlisle, eight years on: You don't ever catch it in the act. That's what I'd tell the newly-told. Don't sit up nights trying to catch it moving, it doesn't move, it's not separate from you, there's no it. All you ever get is bookkeeping. You count what you loved before and you count what you love now and where the books don't balance and there's no purchase, no event, nothing you can point to and say there, that's where I changed my mind — that's it. That's all the evidence there'll ever be. The books just don't balance. Mine haven't balanced since 2019.

Jassy closed the laptop and sat in the dark and did, for the first time since the function room, with the cold hands of her trade, her own books.

The experiment she designed in May and ran in June, and she designed it as an experiment, on graph paper, because that was the last dignity available: if the want was hers, she could act against it. That was the whole protocol. People acted against their own wants every day of their lives — left jobs they loved, towns they loved, men they loved, walked away wanting with every step, that was called being a person; her mother had done it with a glass of wine every Friday for years, hold the want in your hand and set it down. So: a bag, the holdall, packed honestly — clothes, the blue sketchbook, the deposit account card, her half of the tin money; a train she didn't book because booking would be theatre, this wasn't leaving, this was calibration; if she could get to Hull, sit in a Costa for one hour with the bag at her feet, and feel it as possible — one hour of standing free of it on her own two legs — then the want was weather and she'd walk back into it gladly and marry him in a field and dance her parents into apology. She told nobody. She waited for a Tuesday when Dane was on the pots with his dad, and she packed the bag at nine in the morning with her hands steady, and got as far as the bench above the slipway, two hundred yards from the flat, four hundred from the station, and that is where the experiment returned its result.

It was not a wall. Afterwards, that was the part she found she could only explain to one person on earth, and that person poured teas in a church hall: it was not dramatic, there was no panic, no pain, nothing so honest as pain. It was a tilt. Somewhere in her the floor of the world tilted, smoothly, a degree at a time, the way the deck of a big ferry tilts, nothing you could film, and everything in her began to slide gently, frictionlessly, toward home — every thought arriving already turned: the bag was heavy; the bench was cold; Dane would be in at four and she hadn't got anything out for tea; the Costa in Hull would be full of students; what was she trying to prove and to whom; a grown woman on a bench with a holdall like a lass running off to sea; the sketchbook needed the arcade section finishing before the council lad came Thursday; she could always do this another day, doing it another day would actually be a better experiment, a controlled one, this was impulsive, this was exactly the sort of thing the leaflets — and she sat on the bench above the slipway with her hands gripping the bag's strap until the knuckles went white, watching her own mind produce reason after reason after reason, each one true, each one hers, each one arriving with the same fluency she'd heard in a function room in January, gowned, wigged, briefed in advance, in a voice indistinguishable from her own because it was her own; and she understood, with the flat completeness of a level line, that she could sit on this bench until dark and the reasons would never stop coming, that they were being issued from somewhere below the floor of her at a rate no willpower could out-argue, because willpower was upstairs and the issue desk was in the foundations; and at ten to eleven she stood up, and picked up the bag, and walked home. Wanting to. That was the finding. She walked home wanting to, relieved at every step, the deck righting itself under her, the day improving with each yard — and one flight up, over the dead fryers, she set the holdall down in the middle of the front room and stood looking at it, a good bag, packed honestly by a free woman four hours ago, and she said, out loud, to the flat, in a voice she did not recognise because it had nothing in it at all:

"All right."

Dane came in at four and saw the holdall still stood there and went the colour of the harbour. And she watched him ready himself — for her going, she understood later; he'd braced for it two and a half years; the sight of a packed bag was almost mercy to him — and she said, "Sit down," and he sat, and she stood over her own packed bag in the little room and told him the result: bench, tilt, reasons, ten to eleven. All of it, in survey language, flat, exact, the way you'd report a wall out of true — because he was still, God help the both of them, the one person she told things to; that hadn't been taken; nothing was ever taken. And Dane Pyke put his face in his hands and wept, at last, seven months late, two and a half years late, the way men of his line wept, silently, shoulders only; and she stood there and did not comfort him and did not leave, the exact width of the room between them, the bag on the floor like a surveyor's datum, the fixed point everything in the house would be measured off, from then on, forever; and that was the day the knowing arrived, not through his mouth, not through her mother's wardrobe question, but through her own instrument, read with her own eye; and the knowing changed the wanting not one degree, not one minute of arc; and now she got to live in it, which was, she wrote that night on the last page of the blue sketchbook, in her smallest print, under the last Marram elevation, the whole difference between her and every other happy woman on this coast: the books don't balance, and I've seen the ledger, and I still want what it says I want.

Poll got it out of her in August, all of it, in the salon after hours with the blinds down.

She hadn't meant to tell. But Poll had been circling for months, Poll knew the shapes of things before they had names, and one evening doing a repair on Jassy's thumbnail — bitten; she'd started on the nails that summer, at twenty, having never in her life — Poll simply put the file down and said, "Right. I've had enough of watching you carry it. Whatever it is. Out," and it came out. All of it: the van, the crown, the whisper, the wording, the bench. And Poll Denny, who had bitten Craig Tetley in Year 2 for a ruler, listened without one interruption — a lifetime first — going whiter and stiller by the sentence, and at the end, in the wreck of the silence, said the thing Jassy hadn't seen coming though God knew she should have:

"It was my drawer."

"Poll—"

"My van. My cousin. My forty quid — I bought the bloody thing, Jass, I put it on your head. I crowned you. Me." And Poll cried the way she did everything, entirely, ruinously, mascara like a road accident, both of them ending up on the salon floor with their backs against the reception desk among the fallen files; and it was Poll — of course it was Poll; it was always going to be Poll — who, having cried herself dry and repaired her face in the mirror over the basins with the grim thoroughness of a lass off to war, turned round and said: "Right. Well. I know where you're going, any road. Tuesdays. That nurse that got struck off — she never got struck off, our Kelsey's mam says she jacked it — her at St Aidan's, with the urn. The coming-off-the-bands woman."

"I'm not coming off anything, Poll. That's the whole — there's nothing to come off. It's not on me, it's in—"

"Her card says," said Poll, who had evidently read it and evidently memorised it, and who now recited it, at her best friend, across a darkened salon, like scripture and like a dare, "'or being got at by anything.'" She picked up her keys. "You've been got at, Jasmine Harker. Worse than anybody on this coast. And you're the only one of the whole boiling that got at herself an' all — you did your experiment, you read your own meter, you know. So." The keys went round her finger once, decisive. "Tuesday. I'm walking you there meself, ten o'clock, and I'm sitting outside in the car with two coffees like your mam at the dentist, and if you're not out by twelve I'm coming in. And Jass — " and here Poll's voice cracked one last time and got hauled back up by main force, because there was a friendship older than any machine in the room and it was calling its whole balance in — "you'll go every Tuesday. Every single one. You'll go for you and you'll go for me, because I can't undo my drawer, love, I can't ever undo my drawer, but I can drive you to the woman with the chairs till one of us dies. That's what I've got. Get your coat."

The hall smelled of stacked chairs and Scouts and the urn's limescale ghost, and there were eight chairs out in no arrangement at all, and a woman she half-knew — everyone half-knew everyone; the nurse; the one there'd been the fuss about — was setting out milk in a metal jug, and looked up when Jassy came in with Poll's push still warm between her shoulder blades, and did not say welcome, or you must be, or any of the things that would have finished her at the door. She said: "There's tea mashed, or the urn does a coffee that I'll not defend. Sit anywhere. They're all the same chair."

She sat. She said, that first Tuesday, almost nothing — the schoolteacher was talking about her husband, some story about a caravan awning and a gale, half the room laughing, the crochet woman's hands going, a lad describing his head as a browser with the fan on, ordinary wrecked people being ordinary and wrecked at ten in the morning with tea — and Jassy sat in the constellation with her cup cooling, twenty years old, the youngest by thirty years, thinking: this is where I live now, is it; this is what I am; and at five to twelve, with the chairs going up and Poll's horn already going outside like a docking tug, the nurse — Nita; you couldn't call her anything else by eleven; the room ran on first names or none — came and stacked the chair next to hers and said, not looking at her, in the tone you'd use about the weather:

"You don't have to tell me anything. Ever. Some don't. But you'll want a job to do between Tuesdays — trust me on that one thing; the week's long. So." She straightened, chair on hip, and looked at Jassy the way the woman at the arcade till looks at you when your two-pences are spent, kind and level and utterly without rescue in it. "You're the one that draws. Everyone knows you're the one that draws." It wasn't a question, so nothing had to be defended. "This week: walk to the end of the front, the far end, past the arcade, and draw one building. Any one. Doesn't matter which and doesn't matter if it's any good. Not because you want to." She hooked the last chair onto the stack, and delivered the whole method of the rest of Jasmine Harker's life in six words, and went to see to the urn:

"Because it's Tuesday, and I said."


==============================================================================

31

Dane came to say it on a Tuesday in November, mid-afternoon, the dead hour, when the Case held nobody but Mr Ferris and the fire, and he stood at the end of the bar in his work coat with his hands not knowing their business, and said he was off. Grimsby. His uncle had a berth going on the Ellen Kirk, decent boat, twelve-day trips; Ted had a lad lined up for the Constance; the van was sold to a fella in Withby who'd keep the round going, boilers-and-that, most of the same customers.

"She's told me to go," he said, to the bar towel, because some sentences you can't say at a face. "Not — she weren't cruel. She's not been cruel once, that's the — " His thumb found the seam in the varnish, the same seam, and travelled it. "She says she can't get well with me in the room. Them Tuesdays she does — she come home off one and she sat me down and she'd got it all laid out, kind as a nurse, like she were reading me a survey. She says every morning she wakes up wanting me and she can't tell yet which of them mornings is hers. She says as long as I'm there, the — the thing I put in her and me are stood in the same spot, and she can't get a level line on either of us. She needs me out of the frame." He looked up then, and Col saw that the boy had aged the way soft wood ages, all at once, in one season of weather. "So I'm getting out of the frame."

Col drew him a half of the dark mild without being asked, the first drink he'd poured him in a year, and set it down, and the two of them stood either side of the bar in the fire-tick — the confessional geometry, one last time — and drank, Col his tea, the boy his half, neither hurrying it.

"Grimsby's not the moon," Col said, at the end, because Ren would want something carried and this was all he could carry. "Lynn Nunn's down there. Wally's lass. Decent people about the fish dock. Get your dinners somewhere warm."

"Aye."

"And Dane." The lad stopped, coat half on. What Col had — he'd known this hour was coming for a year and had built nothing for it, no procedure, there was no procedure; there was a boy who'd wrecked his daughter in her sleep out of loving her too weakly to bear the weight of it, and who had then stood in the wreck for three years, holding the confession up out of the water with both arms, taking every consequence square, recanting nothing, until the last consequence asked of him was to take himself away, and he was taking himself away; and Col Harker looked at him and found that the hatred had worn down the way a stone wears, not gone, never gone, but smooth enough now to hold without cutting, and that under it, God help him, was something with no name that a man could only get at sideways, so he got at it sideways: "You'll do your ropes over-under on that boat. Whatever the rest of them do. You hear me. Over-under."

Dane stood in the doorway of the Case Is Altered with the November light behind him, and his face did what it did, and he said, "Aye. Over-under," and the door swung to on its two notes, and that was the last time the pub held him.

The postcard came in February, franked Grimsby, no message, no signature. A drawing on the back in biro — good biro, sure lines, a man who drew with his hands' whole memory: a crab pot, drawn true, every lath and lashing right, and inside the pot, where the crab would be, very small, a heart. Not a soft heart; drawn like a fisherman's tattoo, an honest working heart with its chambers on. Col stood at the till with it for a long time, and then he put it in the till drawer, under the tray, where the foreign coins and the first pound the pub ever took lived, and told nobody, and found it there every cashing-up for the rest of the Case's life, and never once moved it.

The roof went in January, in the manner of roofs: not dramatically, expensively. A slipped course over the function room let three weeks of weather into the void above the false ceiling — the false ceiling Jassy had condemned at sixteen; four foot of forgotten Victorian air up there, just as she'd said — and the quote to do it right had a comma in it, and the insurance had a clause in it, and Ren fetched the concertina folder and laid the whole position out on the kitchen table one Sunday night in March with the good biro and both pairs of glasses, and it wasn't a crisis, that was the thing Col kept insisting on and Ren kept agreeing with, too readily; it was a staircase with every tread lower: the wet trade half what it had been five years since; the food and the coffee good but the margin gone to wages and power; the season shorter; the quiz beloved and worth nothing; the two of them nearer sixty than fifty, and the roof, and the boiler's turn coming, and the cellar's chiller on borrowed time; and Keelhaven Coastal Ltd, patient as weather, had written twice more, each number politer than the last.

"Say it, then," Col said, into the folder's spread. "You've had it costed a year."

"I'll say half of it." Ren took her glasses off, both pairs, which meant the figures were done and the marriage was speaking. "The half I know: we can do five more years if nothing breaks, and things break. That's the books. The other half's yours, love, and I've never once been able to do your half for you." She reached across the invoices and put her hand flat over his, on the table, in the lamplight. "So I'll only ask it the once, and whatever you say we'll do: who are we keeping it for?"

And Col opened his mouth with the list ready, the list he'd carried fifteen years, the town, and began it — "For the — " — and the list stood up to be counted and came apart in his hands. For Wally: who was three years dry and left at nine and drank lime and soda, whom the pub had nearly killed and the age had saved. For the Sandras: one of whom couldn't sit near the crisps now, both of whom did aquafit Wednesdays and came to quiz less than either owned. For the Turbines: paid off and scattered when the array went live, Mackie married in Middlesbrough with a bairn and a band and a Christmas card. For Bob, for Mr Ferris, for the darts board, for the quiz — for a room, thinning year on year, of people he loved, who needed the Case the way you need a war memorial: truly, deeply, and one hour a week. And Ren sat with her hand over his and watched her husband arrive, in his own time, by his own stairs, at the bottom of it — she'd been stood at the bottom two years holding the lamp — and Col got there the way he got everywhere, slowly, entirely, and said, at last, looking at the backs of his own hands:

"I were going to say the town. It were never the town, were it. Towns don't drink mild." He turned his hand over under hers and held it. "It's half four in the morning in here, most days, in me head. Has been a good while. A man stood polishing a room, waiting on a trade that's — " and he didn't finish that one either, because the sentence had his daughter in it somewhere, and Wally's bus, and a whole coast of wants gone quiet like lights going out along a front, and some sentences a man leaves in the cellar; and instead he squared the folder on the table, edge to edge, the way his whole family squared things, and said the operative part, the till-talk, flat and done: "Not Keelhaven. I'll not have flats. If we do it we do it to somebody that'll keep a room here folk can sit in — I don't care if they sell it in thimbles at nine pound a go, a room, with taps, and somebody's name over the door. Find me that buyer and I'll sign."

Ren, who had found that buyer in October and been sitting on the letter since — a couple from York, Sam and Erin, early forties, banded and bright-eyed, who'd been down three times "for the building," who wanted, they said, a wine bar and kitchen, small plates, local fish, keep the fireplace, keep the name if the Harkers would allow it, they loved the name — Ren said, "I'll make some enquiries," with a straight face, and filed the folder, and put the kettle on; and that was how the Case Is Altered was sold: at a kitchen table, in the lamplight, to the sound of a kettle, between a woman who'd done the sums and a man who'd finally stopped doing the other thing, whatever the other thing had been, all those years, at half four in the morning, in the polished dark.

Wally let himself in three weeks after the heads of terms went round, at ten past three in the morning, with the key he'd had since some forgotten emergency of the last landlord's, because he'd seen the lamp from the street — Wally slept four hours a night now and walked the front before the gulls, one of the old men of the sea wall suddenly, which had happened to him the way everything happened to him, mildly and all at once — and he found Col where the whole town could have guessed he'd be, on the wrong side of his own bar, on the third stool, with the one lamp on and nothing poured.

Wally took the next stool but one. The fire was long dead. The pumps stood hooded. Somewhere in the walls the building ticked, contracting, doing its own night audit of a hundred and sixty years.

"Heard, then," Col said.

"Whole town's heard. Sandra Leggett had it before your solicitor." Wally settled himself, cap still on, a man in no hurry at the end of the world. "Wine bar. Small plates." He tried the words like a man trying a foreign coin in his teeth. "What's a small plate when it's at home?"

"Less fish, more drizzle. Ren's seen the sample menu. She says the lass can actually cook, which she says like it's the only thing she's prepared to be beat by."

"Aye, well." Wally looked down the dark room, the stacked stools, the shrouded pumps, the crisp boxes flattened by the door, Guinness asleep on the third stair like the last exhibit. "It were never going to be flats while you stood up, and it were never going to be forever whatever you did. Nothing on a coast is. Me dad used to say a pub's a tide mark. Shows you where the water got to, that year, them people." He was quiet a moment. "Good mark, this one. Hundred and sixty year of where the water got to."

They sat. The lamp hummed the way it had hummed through thirty years of cashing up, through the one glass and the tea that followed it, through the whole archaeology of nights this corner held, and it was Wally, in the end, who said it — because he was the only man alive with the standing, and because he'd plainly come down at three in the morning with it in his pocket like the key, the thing fifteen years at the same corner entitles a man to say once and never again:

"You'd rather I were still on that stool. Some nights."

The building ticked. Col looked at his oldest customer, his oldest friend, the pale patch on the wood two seats down that no polish had ever raised, the lime and soda years, the bowls trophies, the Thursday phone calls from Grimsby, the grandkids, the living man — and heard himself say, "No," and heard the room hold both of them, the no and everything under the no, the way it had held everything else for a hundred and sixty years, without comment, in the lamplight, till morning was nearly a fact; and Wally nodded, slow, the nod of a man who'd heard both an' all, and squared his cap, and stood, and said the last liturgy the two of them had left:

"Same again tomorrow?"

"If I'm spared," Col said.

And the door did its two notes behind him, and Col Harker sat on alone in the middle of the tide mark, in the humming lamplight, in the room where the water had got to, and let the morning come.


==============================================================================

32

Roy Trant died on a Thursday in February, in the vestry, counting the tower-roof float, and the manner of it was so mild, so entirely in character, that the town retold it for weeks with a kind of reverence: the columns ruled, the coins in their bags, the running total carried to the foot of the page in his upright hand, and then nothing — the pen set down beside the figure, not dropped, set down, as though he'd been called away to the door — and the verger finding him sat upright with his reading glasses on and the float correct to the penny. Heart. Seventy-four. The obituary in the free paper ran to half a page and every word of it was true: forty years' service to the children of this coast; churchwarden; lifeboat treasurer; devoted husband of Vera. The funeral filled St Aidan's past standing, three generations of the taught, men of sixty crying like third-formers, firm but fair moving down the pews like a response in the liturgy; and at the front, upright in good navy, hands folded, entirely composed, the widow — so brave, the pews murmured, so dignified; and only two women in the church that afternoon, one in the third row from the back and one come down from Ripon, watched that stillness with the cold in their stomachs of people watching a clock whose winder has just left the house.

Nobody cancelled anything, because nobody knew there was anything to cancel. That was the mechanism, in the end — no drama, no discovery, just the slow administration of death doing its blind work: the accounts frozen, the direct debits failing one by one through the spring like lights along a front, the courteous letters from a clinic in Hull addressed to a man under a stone; and the band on Vera Trant's head going on breathing its little breath, unmanaged, unrenewed, untuned, while the course it carried — old-model, punctiliously maintained, renewed quarter by quarter for six years by a punctilious man — began, in the fourth month, quietly, like any learning left unrehearsed, to fade.

Nita saw it happen the way you see spring happen: nothing on any given day, and then one day the verge is green.

She was in the churchyard most Tuesdays anyway, and Vera was at the grave most days, that was established parish fact — devoted, the pews said — and through March and April the two of them progressed from nods to remarks about the weather to, in the way of women at graves, remarks that were not about the weather; and it was at the grave, on a Tuesday in May, with the daffodils going over, that Vera Trant said the first of the things Nita wrote nowhere, having no book now and needing none.

"I'm cross with him," Vera said. To the stone; conversationally; in the pleasant tray-voice, so that it took Nita a moment. "That's not right, is it. He's dead, and I stand here with his flowers, and some mornings I could — " her gloved hand closed on the stems, opened again. "I miss him at six o'clock. He did the six o'clock news and the sherry, small one, we'd argue about the newsreaders. I miss that like a limb, I'm not — nobody's to say I don't." The chin came up; the chin was arriving back, that spring, a degree a week, Grace's chin, the chin of the girl in the wedding photo laughing at something off to the side. "And then I'll be stood at the sink, and it comes up in me like — like water in a cellar, this — " she chose the word with her eyes shut, and it came out low and exact: "fury. At what? He never once raised his hand. He never once raised his voice. I have been done out of something, Mrs Osei, I know it the way you know a thing in your body, I am seventy years old and I am done out of — and there's no him to stand in front of and say what, say how, say give it back. He's under there with the float correct to the penny and I can't get at him." The flowers went into the pot, one stem at a time, precise, trembling. "Grace says you know what were done. Better than I do, she says. That you wrote it in a book and they took your job for it."

"They took the job for the book," Nita said. "Near enough."

"Then you'll tell me. When I'm — not yet. I'm not — there's whole parts of it I haven't the floor for yet, the nurse at the surgery says, she's kind, the little one — but you'll tell me, when I ask. Everything in your book. Because everyone else has decided what I'm to be spared, my whole life somebody deciding what I'm to be spared, and I'll not have it again, not from kindness, kindness were the — " and the sentence stopped itself, and Vera Trant looked at her dead husband's stone in the May light with her sister's chin, and finished, flat and quiet, in a voice with nothing pleasant in it at all: "He were so kind. That's what nobody will ever — you can't get at kindness. There's no place on it to take hold."

The years came back thin, where they came back at all. That was how Vera put it herself, over the summer, in the front room with the good china — Nita went most weeks now, civilian, lasagne-bearing, one of the rota of women that coast has always produced for its widows, except that this widow's need had no name in the rota's long experience — "it's not that they're gone, the years. I can walk through them. It's that there's nothing to knock on. Like a book you read with a fever on you: I can tell you what happens, near enough, and I couldn't swear I were there." Six years of flower shows unjudged, of Sundays unrung, of a spare room locked on her own hands' work; six Christmases of a box of thirty; all of it accessible and none of it inhabited, a house of show-rooms; and the grief for it, when the grief finally found its floor, came wrong-sized and second-hand, the way grief comes for a thing you were absent from — she wept, when she wept at last, not over the paintings or the shows but over Stan Petch, of all the objects in the world; Stan Petch, dead four years, whose funeral she had attended, it turned out, serene, composed, in good navy, and felt nothing; a man who'd once — this came out in pieces, over three visits — a man who'd taught alongside Roy forty years and carried a torch for her the length of it, decent, hopeless, hedge-trimming Stan, who'd bicycled past the bungalow every Sunday of his retirement on no errand anyone could name; and she had sat in the pew at his funeral like a woman at a stranger's, and now, six years on, at her own kitchen table, the missing mourning arrived entire, with interest, for a man she'd never once wanted and had every day been glad of, "because he were mine to miss, do you see, whatever else he were or weren't, and it were took off me, my own — I never got to feel him go." And Nita sat with her, and the china cooled, and outside the monkey puzzle stood over the square hedge that a man from Withby did now, roughly, to Vera's visible and reviving disgust.

Grace came down through it all like weather off the Pennines, a fortnight at a time, and the two long-boned sisters filled the bungalow with a noise it had not held in Nita's eleven years of knowing it — bickering, mainly; the radio re-tuned twice daily as an act of war; the thermostat a Somme — and one Tuesday in July, arriving with the lasagne, Nita heard through the open kitchen window the sound of Vera Trant laughing, properly, at length, helplessly, at something Grace had said about a neighbour's fence, and stood on the square-cut path a full minute before knocking, giving it room, the way you'd stand back from a fire catching; and it was that same evening, drying up, that Vera said "Gracie, where's this go," about a gravy boat, and neither sister broke stride, and Nita, holding a tea towel in a dead man's kitchen, turned away to the window and did her crying with the taps on, one length of the draining board exactly, the doorway ration, and was done by the time the kettle called.

The painting came back in September, and it came back angry, which everyone but the paint shop found fitting.

She set the easel — the folding one, the North Landing one, extant, loft-found, its wing-nuts stiff with forty years — in the spare room with the door wide open, propped by a brick, all day, every day, "doors in this house stand open now or they come off their hinges, Gracie, I've told you"; and she painted the marsh, and the allotments, and the front, in a hand nobody could have called recovered: the old sureness gone, the knife-work coarse, five years of stillness and seventy of age in every stroke, lines that shook and colours that fought — and alive, unarguably, offensively alive, each small board with weather actually moving in it; and when the kind woman who ran the church bazaar suggested, tactfully, that they were perhaps a little fierce for the craft table, Vera priced them at forty pounds each out of pure retaliation and sold, that first Christmas, eleven.

The one she gave Nita she gave in October, on a Tuesday, arriving at the hall unannounced at ten past ten with it wrapped in a bread bag — the marsh at low water under a sky like wet slate, a small bad brave furious painting, the horizontals all slightly off level so the whole scene seemed to be righting itself, a world coming back onto its bearings — and she stood in the doorway of the room she'd never yet entered, among the chairs, holding it out, and said: "It's not much good. It's mine, though. Every inch of it's mine." And then, looking round at the constellation, at Trish's crochet and Robbie's tea and the urn's steam and the girl in the corner with the sketchbook — looking round the whole shabby Tuesday of it with the chin up and the tray-voice gone for good: "Grace says you show people here what it costs. Coming back." She set something else on the table then, beside the milk jug, small and grey and coiled, and it took the room a moment to understand what it was looking at, because it looked like nothing, it had always looked like nothing, that was its whole nature and its whole method: a soft band, the colour of nothing, its little light dark now for want of a dead man's direct debit. "Then show them this an' all," Vera said. "Not what it does. What it looks like. Because it looks like nothing, and that's the entire fact of the matter, and nobody believes it till they've held it." And Nita took it — the room silent, Trish's hands stopped on the crochet — and it weighed what a hairband weighs, which is to say nothing, which is to say more than anything else on the table; and it lived thereafter in the hall's kitchen drawer, between the spare fuses and the tombola tickets, and was brought out some Tuesdays for new faces to hold, and every single one of them said the same thing, holding it, which was: I thought it'd be heavier.

Peg inspected the painting the evening Nita brought it home, at length, through the bottom half of her glasses, while the snooker murmured.

"Where's it going?"

"There." Nita had the hammer out. The wall over the sideboard had held her registration certificate, framed, twenty-two years, gone now to a drawer she didn't open; the hook was still in. The marsh went up in its place, slightly off level, which suited it, and the two women considered it, the wet slate sky, the fighting colours, the world righting itself at forty pounds retaliatory.

"She'll want watching, that one," Peg said at last, with profound approval. "Painting like that at her age. There's a temper in every inch of it." She settled back to the snooker. "Better than what were up before, any road. That last thing were just an invoice with a frame on. This one's somebody getting their own back." And the young man in the waistcoat, on the screen, bridged awkward over the pink, took on the long red the whole room had said was impossible, and cut it clean into the corner; and Peg, who had seen everything now, twice, said to him, and to the marsh, and to the general administration of the universe: "There. Told you it were there. You've just to look at it long enough."


==============================================================================

33

The first Tuesday of the after — the first with Dane gone, the flat over the fryers holding one toothbrush and an echo — she arrived at the hall at ten past ten with that week's drawing done and her coat still on like armour, and picked, before the tea was even in her hand, the fight she'd been packing all week.

"I want to say something and I want it minuted." There was no minute-book; there was Trish's crochet pausing, and Robbie looking into his mug, and Nita at the urn with her back turned, carrying on filling. "Everyone's very gentle in here, and it's all — but there is nothing wrong with staying. That's what nobody in this whole — my own family looks at me like staying's the symptom. Like if I were well I'd be in London by Friday. Half this coast stays! Poll stays and nobody's took her to a tribunal about it. Wally Nunn's stopped on that same stool forty years and he's a — he's beloved, he's furniture. My dad's not left this town above six times in his life! It's a good town. It's my town. I know every parapet line on that front better than any — you could want to stay here. A person could just want it. Free as anything. It happens every day." Her voice had gone up through the room like a kite in a gust and she heard it and couldn't reel it. "So when I stay — when I'm still here at thirty, and forty, which I will be, likely, because the thing's in me for keeps, we all know that, there's no — when I'm here at forty, nobody, nobody, is to stand at that urn and look at me kind about it. It'll be my life. Whoever ordered it. You live in the house that's built, don't you. People live in worse."

The urn rumbled. A chair creaked. And Nita Osei, who in eleven months had never once argued with anybody's Tuesday, brought the jug to the table, and set it down, and said:

"Same time next week, then."

That was all. That was the whole of the resistance the room ever offered her — same time next week — and it beat her the way the green beat the darts men, by having no wall anywhere to hit; you came at it swinging and it received you like weather; and Jassy sat down, still in her coat, shaking slightly, and drank her tea, and at ten to twelve she put that week's drawing on the table with the others because that was the procedure, and the room looked at it — the lifeboat station, done tight and black and furious, every line ruled, a drawing with its teeth gritted — and Trish said, "ooh, I like that one, it's very neat," which was true, and was the worst thing anyone had said about her work in six years, and Jassy went home and stood it against the wall with the others and looked at it a long time.

Dane didn't write to her. Poll monitored this outrage on her behalf for months, primed to detonate — and Jassy, who had by then sat with it through a winter of Tuesdays, understood it in the end for what it was, and said so, once, flatly, to Poll's fury, in the salon: "It's the last thing he's doing for me. Staying out of the frame. He'll be sat on that boat with a biro and a — " and stopped, and did not finish, and Poll, watching her friend's face in the mirror, for once in her life let a thing lie.

The assignments went on. One building a week, any building, not because you want to. Through that first winter they came out clenched — the neat black teeth-gritted period, Nita called it later, though never at the time; at the time Nita said nothing whatever about any drawing except occasionally, maddeningly, "you've missed a Tuesday's worth of sky," — and then somewhere in the spring they began, without anyone deciding it, to change. She drew the gasholder frame at Withby, which nobody had ever called a building, and found it took the morning light like a cathedral clerestory. She drew Poll's static, the 1998 Pemberton, nail bar and leopard throw and NO COLD CALLERS, with the full deadpan care she'd once spent on the customs house — Poll cried, framed it, hung it over the nail table, showed every client for a year. She drew the caravan field in winter with the vans hunkered like cattle; the Costcutter, which was ugly, and which she drew so honestly that its ugliness became a fact of weather rather than a crime; the bus shelter; the substation; the tide mark itself, the line of the town where the water got to. Draw what's there, she'd told her father once, at sixteen, over the folio, not what's meant to be there — and it had taken the whole catastrophe of her life to make her obey her own instruction, because the blue-sketchbook Marram, she could see now, leafing back through it, had always been a town improved, a town redrawn for the interview panel, loved and corrected in the same line; and what accumulated in the hall's cardboard folder that year, Tuesday by Tuesday, was the other town, the town as found: unimproved, unapplying, wanting nothing from her; and it was better work. She knew it was better work. She had no word yet for why, and refused every Tuesday to let Nita off the hook of not supplying one either, and Nita smiled and topped the pot.

Her mother she came back to by kitchen. That was simply the door that stood nearest: a Friday in Lent, short-staffed, Kirsty's boy poorly, and Jassy behind the pass for the first time since school, plating mackerel, and the two of them fell into the old choreography, hip-checks and short words, and nothing was said and everything resumed, which was the Harker sacrament; and her father she came back to by fabric — by the Case's long dying, in fact, because the sale gave them a shared trade again: Sunday afternoons that spring, the two of them went round the building for the buyers' survey, Col holding the dumb end of the tape while his daughter measured his kingdom for its next life, and it was doing the function room — up the ladder, torch through the hatch of the false ceiling, and there it was, the lost four feet, the bricked arch of the tall window furred with a century's dust, exactly where sixteen-year-old certainty had said — that Col, steadying the ladder, said, to her boots, in the flat voice that carried everything: "You were right about the window, then. All them years back." And Jassy, up in the dark with the torch, in the roof-space of everything, said, "I were right about the window. I'd have found it faster with a — " and stopped, and the two of them let the sentence stand unfinished in the dust between them, because both now knew every ending it could take, and none of the endings needed the air; and she came down the ladder, and he held it, and that was her father and her mended, in the only dialect either of them had ever spoken.

The beam Tuesday was in May.

She was early, first in, kettle barely on, and the hall was empty and full of morning, and she stood waiting for the urn with her eyes going up — they always went up, her eyes, it was the oldest fact about her — along the queen-post trusses of the hall roof, tie beams the colour of strong tea, and she was three trusses in before she caught herself doing it: spans stepped off by eye, bearing checked, that one's been sistered, war damage or rot, forty-by-two-twenty softwood at — and she stood in the middle of the empty hall with the kettle beginning to sing, caught red-handed in her own oldest habit, the staring, the pricing, the eye that took a roof apart lath by lath out of pure uninstructed love — and it had survived. That was the thing that arrived, at half nine on a Tuesday, unscheduled, with no one to see: it had all survived. Whatever had been written into her that night in the van, whatever the tilt had taken and turned — the eye was still hers, had been hers at eight, on her father's shoulders, pointing at the arcade's date stone; hers before Dane, before the band, before London was a word; the want to go had been got at, but the want to see — nobody had touched it, nobody could, it was down in the foundations of the foundations, it was the thing the machine had had to build with, not on; and Jassy Harker stood under the tea-coloured trusses and laughed out loud, one bark of it, and was crying before the laugh finished, both at once, neither winning, the kettle screaming its head off; and when Nita came through from the kitchen at the noise, tea towel over her shoulder, and found her like that, wet-faced and grinning up at a roof, Jassy said the only sentence the moment had in it:

"It's still there."

And Nita — who did not ask what, being a woman who had spent a life among the things that survive in people, who knew exactly what a person sounds like when they've found one unlooted room in a burgled house — said, "Aye," and took the kettle off, and put the tea on, and that was the whole of the ceremony, that was the entire liturgy of the resurrection: aye, and the kettle, and the same chairs; and the day the folder of Tuesday drawings went past forty sheets, Nita took it home over the weekend without asking — first and only time — and brought it back Monday-morning-early to the salon, of all places, and put it on Poll's nail table, and said, "You're the one that organises her. She'll want the word from somebody that can't draw. That's a portfolio, that is. Tell her."

The Hull form she filled in at the hall, deliberately, at the trestle by the urn, on the last Tuesday of application week.

Part-time route: the studio school at Hull, two days a week, five years, RIBA part one the slow way — the handrail course, the plain cousin, the one Adeoti had made her keep on a form seven years ago; forty minutes up the line; keep Hutton's, keep the flat, keep the Tuesdays. She'd walked round it for a month the way her father walked round a decision, and the walking always fetched up at the same stile: she could not tell — this was the fact of her life now, the permanent weather — she could never wholly tell whose idea anything was. Hull kept her near home: the want approved it, purred over it, that was obvious, she could feel the purr. Hull was also, on paper her own cold surveyor's eye had checked three times, the best and only costed route from where she actually stood to the letters after the name — the life-sized version, the buildable elevation, the one with the fall on the roof. Both were true. Both would always both be true, about everything, forever: that was what had been done to her, that was the house she lived in; and she sat at the trestle with the biro over the box that said applicant's signature, doing one last audit of her own wanting and finding, as ever, the books unbalanced and no purchase anywhere — and then she heard the kettle, and Trish's hook going, and Robbie saying to the schoolteacher have you seen this, she's only drawn the gasholder like it's York Minster, and Nita, at the urn, not watching her, watching her; and Jasmine Harker stopped auditing, because the audit had no bottom and Tuesdays did; and signed it — not because she trusted the want, and not because she'd beaten it, but because it was application week, and she'd said; and the signing, she found, felt like nothing at all, like a delivery signed for, like an INR in range; and she'd learned what that meant now, and what it didn't, and she posted the form on the way home anyway, with Poll's two coffees going cold on the sea wall, and the tide a mile out, and the whole flat silver world lying there unimproved, drawing itself.

The acceptance came in August, and she read it standing in the middle of the flat, alone, twice, and her legs did nothing at all, and she was glad of it — gladder of the nothing than she'd have believed; you could build on nothing; nothing held — and then she went down the stairs two at a time and stood in the street outside the chippy with her thumb already dialling, twenty-one years old, batter-adjacent, for keeps, on her own two feet, and her father answered his phone the way he answered it in the cellar, half a ring before he could have looked:

"Now then."

"Dad." The gulls went over. Down the front the arcade's shutter was up, and Ken's cones stood over the compass rose, and somewhere under everything the sea kept its own long count. "It's Hull. I got in."

A pause on the line, the exact length of a man setting down a rope.
