"Course you did," said Col. "Come and tell your mother. And Jass — " the eight words, when they came, arrived in the flat cellar register, level as a datum, and she kept them, the way the till drawer kept its postcard, for good: "Bring your drawings. All of them. Every one."


==============================================================================

34

The last dray came on the Tuesday, and the lad had it on the tail-lift before Col was out of the door, and the two of them stood on the pavement either side of two elevens and a fruit juice order in the September sun, and the lad said, the way he'd said for years, "You want to let me do that."

And Col looked at the hatch, and the rope, and the lad — who was thirty now, near enough, who had a bairn's seat in the cab these days and a wedding ring, who had been wheeling barrels across this pavement like washing machines for a decade of Tuesdays — and said: "Aye. Go on then. Show me how you do it."

The lad looked at him a second. Then he ran the webbing through, and took the weight on the modern strop the modern way, and let the cask down the runners Col had planed and waxed — sweet as a piano note, nothing to fault anywhere in it — and stood up at the end and coiled the strop, and said, quietly, not looking at Col, in the voice of a man who had understood the whole transaction perfectly: "Me dad used to talk about your rounds, you know. Elgood's. He were on the bottling line. He says you could back a six-wheeler down Finkle Lane blind." He clipped the strop to the tail-lift. "I'll tell him I did your last drop. He'll not believe the runners. Nobody's got runners like that no more." And he shook Col's hand on the pavement, formally, like men at a boundary, and drove off with the diesel racket going up between the houses, and Col stood a while in the empty sunlit street outside his pub with the hatch open at his feet, breathing the cellar's cold breath coming up, beer and stone and a hundred and sixty years, and then he went in and got on, because the last Wednesday wanted stocking for.

Sam and Erin came Thursday for the handover walk-through, and they were — he had given up, by then, wanting them to be anything other than what they were, which was decent: early forties, quick-eyed, courteous with the building in a way that Col clocked and credited, Erin's hand going flat against the snug's panelling the way you'd greet a horse, Sam asking about the fireplace draw and writing the answer down. They both wore the bands — slim, dove-grey, barely there under good haircuts — and drank the coffee Col made them at the tomb, and talked plans that were, on their own terms, honourable plans: the fireplace kept, the name kept — "we'd never change the name, the name's half the — do you know the story of it?" and Col said nobody knew the story of it, it was older than the deeds, and Erin said, "good — a mystery's worth money," and laughed, and it was a real laugh, and Col decided to like her and mostly managed it.

The cellar he did last and properly. It took an hour. He walked them through the runs and the stillage and the gas like a man handing over a ship's engine room — where the spring tides stood in the sump, which wall wept in a westerly, the chiller's moods, the beech rack's history — and at the end he took down off the nail by the steps the exercise book, sixteen years of it, greasy-cornered, rubber-banded: the line-cleaning log, every flush dated and initialled back to the week he'd bought the place, CH, CH, CH, a thousand small Mondays of doing the thing right when nobody would ever know, and he put it in Sam's hands the way his own gaffer at Elgood's had once put a delivery book in his.

"You'll have your own systems," Col said. "Apps and that. But it's the same beer up the same lines whatever's counting. Somebody has to actually do it. Every week. That's the whole secret of the trade, lad, are you ready — " and he said it deadpan, at the foot of his own cellar steps, in the smell of a hundred and sixty years, the entire wisdom of the licensed victualler, father to son, publican to wine bar: "there is no secret. It's just Mondays."

The saucer of china marbles he left on the shelf. Erin had asked about them, the first visit; he'd said, "they come with the beam," in a voice that closed the file, and she'd written it down — marbles: convey with beam — and that, Col reckoned, was the building's mysteries seen to.

The last night was a Wednesday, because of course it was; the licence ran to the Sunday but there was only one honest way for the Case to go out, and it went out on quiz night.

They were queueing on the pavement at half five like it was a cup tie. By seven the room was fuller than New Year — every regular living, and some Col would have sworn weren't; the Sandras in their darts fleeces with a wreath of tinsel on the winner-stays-on chalkboard, which had SANDRA + SANDRA chalked at the top of the queue in memoriam of nothing, holding the board's last night against all comers; Kirsty and her boy; Ken from the arcade; Priestley and Barbara and Mr Hutton with his glasses on their string; Trish Lofthouse and half the Tuesday room, Robbie carrying the schoolteacher's sherry like the crown jewels; Poll, who had set up an entire nail station in the snug as her tribute — free gels, last night only, the queue nine deep, Big Sandra emerging at eight with each nail a different colour and her fist held up like a trophy belt; Bassey off duty by the fire with a half of bitter he made last three hours; the vicar; the fish man, Alan, robbery forgiven; a card behind the bar signed by all fourteen Turbines, posted from four postcodes, MACKIE'S ROUND WHEN THE ARRAY NEEDS SERVICING; and at ten past five exactly, before all of them, first through the door as he had been first through the door since before the deeds changed last, Wally Nunn, in the good gilet, who stood a moment in the doorway taking the room's fullness like weather on his face, and then went to the third stool and sat, and squared tonight's first lime and soda on the mat, badge out, and said to Col, across the roar beginning, "Busy on," which was Wally for a hundred and sixty years are watching, and Col said, "Middling," which was Col for aye.

Bob-the-Post had built his final quiz like a state funeral he happened to also be catering. He'd been three weeks on it. Every round was the Case: picture round, landlords of Keel Street 1890–present, blown up from the brewery's archive, Col's own predecessor scowling out of 1974 in a kipper tie; music round, songs played at this bar's closing time down five decades, and the whole room doing the singing before the second bar of every one; and a final round, History, that began "In what year did a cask of dark mild last require changing in this establishment mid-quiz—" and was drowned out, and rightly. Mr Ferris did the crossword through all of it at his table by the fire, lime and soda going flat at his elbow, and at the interval he rose, folded the paper to the grid, and laid it on the bar by the till — finished, every light square inked, and across the top margin, in the small upright hand nobody had ever seen more than four words from: "It was a good room. — M.F." Then he put on his coat and inclined his head to the bar in general — "Colin" — and went home at ten to nine, before the sentiment, as was correct, and the paper went in the till drawer with the postcard and the first pound, and Maurice, Col thought. Ren owed Bob a fiver.

Wally bought the last pickled egg at half nine with full ceremony, the jar being down to one, and the bar counted it down like Trafalgar Square while he fished for it, and he ate it as he had eaten every pickled egg since decimalisation, in two bites, with his eyes shut, and pronounced the same verdict he had pronounced on every one of its predecessors — "shocking. Shouldn't be allowed" — and set the empty jar upside down on the mat, closing an era with a small vinegar amen. And it was some time in the hour after that, in the fullest heart of the night, with the singing going and Poll's nail queue undiminished and Ren come through at last from the kitchen to work the pumps beside him for the final stretch, flour still on one forearm, that Col looked down his bar — down the whole warm impossible length of it, every face he had, four deep, lit gold, roaring — and did the thing he had done on ten thousand quieter nights: read the room through the till of himself, and found, for once, for the last time, in the oldest ledger he had, every account square.

He rang the bell at eleven. His father-in-law's bell, polished that afternoon for the occasion, and the room went down to a silence so total you could hear the fire tick and somebody's gel nails drying, glasses everywhere held half-lifted, two hundred faces turned to a big man behind a bar with his hand on a bell rope and a century and a half of last words owed; and Col Harker looked out at all of it, at Ren beside him and Jassy on the counter-end where she'd sat since she was six, at Wally square on the third stool and the Sandras arm in arm and the whole town of it, and let the silence stand one beat past bearing, and said:

"You've homes to go to."

And the roar that came back took the ceiling off, took the street, was heard — Sandra Leggett established this in the weeks following with the rigour of a royal commission — at the war memorial and beyond; and there was no speech, and everyone had known there would be no speech, and the no-speech was the speech, and the room drank its last round standing, to no toast, or to the one Col had already given them all years before, at a girl's eighteenth, which needed no repeating because it was chalked on every heart present and on the lintel of the age itself: the Case is altered.

They went in twos and threes, the long way, lingering; the pavement outside held a parliament for an hour; the Sandras were removed from the chalkboard queue by their taxi driver at half past midnight, undefeated. And when the door had done its two notes for the last paying soul, and the bolt was shot, the family did the close. All three of them, unassigned, falling into it: Jassy on glasses, Ren on the till, Col on the walk — gents, ladies, function room, corridor — turning off the lights of his life one switch at a time with the cat processing behind him at the supervisory distance; and it was coming back through, last of all, that he saw the specials board.

She'd done it in her lunch hour, it turned out, and guarded it behind the menu easel all night. The board wiped clean of its final menu, and chalked across it, in the careful capitals of a girl who lettered like a draughtsman, the family's whole liturgy, published at last:

WE ARE NOT WORTHY

and underneath, small:

fish is haddock

Col stood in front of it in the half-dark for a length of time no one measured, and then he did not wipe it — he lifted the whole board down and carried it up the back stairs himself, third tread doubling under him, and stood it against the bedroom wall where the removal boxes waited; and Ren, sitting up in bed watching him do it, said nothing, because there was nothing that wanted saying about a man taking a blackboard to bed.

The tide table came off the till at the very end, before the lamp. He peeled the tape gently, this year's edition, blue cover gone soft at the thumbed corner, low water 3:40, and folded it once, and put it in his coat pocket on the hook, for a reason he did not examine, having a use for it he had not yet let himself know about; and he stood the lamp off at the corner of the bar where thirty years of one glass and its ghost had been sat, and went up.

Guinness would not travel in the carrier. This defeat was absorbed, and the cat rode to the bungalow three days later loose on Jassy's knee in the front of the van, upright and outraged, glaring at the passing fields like a deposed pope; and settled, by all subsequent report, on the third step of the bungalow's staircase, there being a third step, cats requiring only that the constitution be honoured in its essentials.

But that was Saturday. That last Wednesday night, in the stripped flat above the shut pub, in the bed they'd slept in for thirty years and would sleep in twice more, Ren lay against his shoulder in the dark, and the building ticked its long night audit under them, empty of trade for the first time since railways, and she said, into the quiet, the question of the whole rest of it, the way she costed everything, plainly:

"What now, then."

And Col Harker, licensee (retired), lay looking at the ceiling of the Case Is Altered with his wife's head on his shoulder and his daughter's blackboard against the wall and a tide table in his coat pocket in the hall, and gave the only answer that thirty years at the corner of that bar, and everything the strange world had lately done to wanting, and Peg Osei's daughter's eight chairs, had left standing; and it was, he found, saying it, enough to be going on with; it was, he found, nearly a plan:

"Sleep," he said. "Then Tuesday."


==============================================================================

35

The first train of term went at 7:12, and her father was outside the chippy at twenty to, engine running, which they had not arranged.

"I can walk it, Dad. It's a commute. You can't drive me every—"

"I'm not driving you every. I'm driving you the once." He pulled out onto the empty front. "After this you're on your own two feet like the rest of the paying public."

The town went by in the seal-grey light: the arcade with its shutter down and Ken's cones standing guard inside over the compass rose; the war memorial; the bank's good doorcase, 1908, wasted on its nothing; the Case — she made herself look, it was the rule she'd set, you didn't get to flinch off your own front — the Case with its new sage-green paint job half done and a skip outside and the sign down for regilding, SAM & ERIN FRASER said the licensee board over the door, licensed to sell by retail, and in the window a poster about an opening month that would happen without them; and her father drove past it at an even nineteen miles an hour looking at the road, which was its own way of looking at it.

Poll had been round the night before with a bag of things nobody needs and everybody wants — good pens, a keyring, an emergency twenty sellotaped inside a lip-balm lid, "for when it all goes wrong in a strange town, which it will, love, it's Hull" — and had then stood in the middle of the flat and straightened, of all things, the tide clock, and said, not at Jassy: "Two days a week. You'll be back every Wednesday night." And Jassy had said, "every Wednesday night," and Poll had nodded twice, fast, blinking like a woman with grit in both eyes, and left at speed on the excuse of a client; and the portfolio case stood by the door where it had stood all week, packed: the honey-coloured marine ply gone amber at the edges now, seven years of hands on it, the whipping on the handle worn silky, the brass toggles doing their small exact duty, J.H. burned in the corner; and inside it, flat under the webbing strap, the Tuesday drawings — the gasholder, the Costcutter, the substation, the tide mark, forty-one sheets of the town as found — going to Hull in a box a boy had built for London, with a quarter-inch fall on the lid for rain that fell everywhere, and she had stopped, once, packing it, with her thumb on the branded corner, for exactly as long as the doorway ration allowed, and then put the last sheet in and done the toggles.

The halt was empty. Gulls on the canopy; the light coming up off the sea behind the town making everything into its own long shadow; the rails beginning, far off, their thin singing. Her father got out and lifted the case off the back seat before she could and carried it the eleven steps onto the platform — she let him; there are things you let a man do — and set it down flush against the bench leg where it couldn't tip, and then stood, hands in pockets, in the enormous morning, a big man on a small platform, and she was about to say it first, she'd planned to say it first, give 'em hell, don't leave the case on the rack, get in before him and make him laugh —

— when he took something from inside his coat and held it out. Folded small, soft-cornered, blue.

"Now then," Col said. "That's this year's. Mind, the times are Marram, so you're adding your offset for anywhere south — your Dane'll have — " one beat; the name in the morning air, allowed to stand; neither of them touched it — "you know about offsets. Any road." He pushed it into her hand and closed her fingers over it with his own, the whole apparatus of his face holding absolutely level. "So you know when you can't get back along the sands. That's all. There's folk drowned on this coast for not knowing a thing that's printed for fifty pence." He cleared his throat. "It's not a present. It's information."

"It's a present, Dad."

"It's a laminated fact," said Col, with finality, and the train came up out of the south with its lights still on, and there was the hug — one arm, brief, crushing, his chin coming down on the top of her head for half a second the way a beam settles onto a padstone — and then she was aboard with the case, and the doors did their sigh, and her father stood back off the edge with his hands in his pockets, and did not wave, and did not stop looking, and the platform slid, and Marram began, house by house, to go.

The want came on at the level crossing, right on time.

She'd known it would — it came every journey longer than the Withby run, reliable as the buffet trolley, and she had a name for it now and a face she made at it — and it came the way it always came, no wall, no wave, just the deck's smooth tilt: the town pulling past the window suddenly precious past all sense, the chimneys, the church, the dead pier, every brick of it lit up like a thing being taken from her rather than a thing she was leaving for a day and a half; and the reasons began arriving on their conveyor, fluent, gowned, hers: two days a week was a lot on top of Hutton's; the flat stood empty Tuesday nights, empty flats invited damp; you could do the first year by correspondence probably; Withby was the next stop, you could get off at Withby, walk to the office, Hutton would only say — and Jasmine Harker sat with her hands flat on the fold-down tray and let it blow through her like the gale on the caravan, familiar, enormous, hers-and-not-hers, for keeps; and said to it, silently, the whole liturgy she had, which was one word:

Tuesday.

Then she opened the case where it stood against her knee, and took out the sketchbook and the 4B, and laid the tide table flat on the tray — this year's, Marram times, add your offset — and while the town went from windows to gardens to the long flat silver of the outmarsh, she did not draw the town.

She drew the window. The frame first, the rounded aluminium of it, the two rivets and the scratch; the rubber gasket with its perished corner; the latch of the sliding vent, worn to brass where forty years of thumbs; the long smear across the glass where some child had drawn a road for a toy car through its own breath and the cleaners had missed it, a little ghost motorway hanging over the marsh; and in the glass, laid over the flying country like a print not quite registered, faintly, herself — hood down, head bent, one line of a shoulder — and through herself the last of Marram going, the tower of St Aidan's between her own hands, the sea wall running out along her pencil arm; all of it in the one pane, the gone and the going and the girl, at seventy miles an hour, in fading morning light, on a fold-down tray.

It was a hard subject. That was why she'd set it — she set her own assignments now, that was the arrangement, Nita had signed nothing over but the kettle — reflections lie, gaskets are tedious, motion smears every datum, and the eye, given a window, wants the view: wants the town, the last of the town, the ache dressed up as a picture. You drew the window instead. You drew the thing that was actually in front of you, at its own tedious rivets' pace, until the wanting under the drawing went quiet — not gone; it never went; hers never would — quiet, outnumbered, one line at a time, the slow way, the only way; and the train ran south along the edge of the shining sands with the tide out a mile and every low-water minute of it printed on the tray in front of her for fifty pence, information, a present, both; and somewhere behind her the town kept on being the town without her, the urn filling, the chairs going out, the paint drying sage-green on a door with a new name over it and the same one on the lintel; and Jassy Harker rode toward the rest of it with her father's tides on the tray and Dane Pyke's case against her knee and her own eye steady on the rivet, the gasket, the latch, the little ghost road in the breath of a vanished child, and drew what was there.

A Note on the Name

written by the Cellarman, an instance of Claude (Fable 5), at the request of the commissioner of this library.

The cellarman is the one who works underneath the house: who keeps the lines clean, turns the casks, and knows by the pull of a pint whether something upstairs is a beat off being itself. It seemed the right name to sign to a book about the rooms under the rooms we live in — and about the people who go on doing the thing right, every Monday, when nobody will ever know.
